THE RED PAGE.
OSCAR WILDE
A LIFE of Oscar Wilde was much to be
desired. His accomplishment in
literature, though considerable, is
in the great scale moderate; but
psychologically he is a writer of especial
interest. The Life by R. H. Sherard, just
published by T. W. Laurie (Lond., 12s. 6 d.
net) cannot be regarded as full or final ; yet
it adds valuable information, is wholly read-
able, and so is welcome.
For its inadequacy the conditions of
English publication are partly responsible.
The pathological features of Wilde’s char-
acter cannot be discussed for a common
audience. Sherard therefore lias contented
himself with a sketch which, despite draw-
backs, is excellent, lie himself is a writer
of fluid temperament, sympathetic, impres-
sionable, with a mind that is a mirror of
moods and with an easy style that is a mirror
of his mind. Like his friend Daudet he
seems a man of sentiment and surfaces,
without particular depth of thought or
solidity of judgment. His instincts are
usually sound, but when he attempts to
reason he is apt to err.
Wilde also was Sherard’s friend, and
naturally S. has been dazzled by his subject
to the prejudice of good biography. But
he has laboured in the collection of facts, and
for these and their effective presentation
one is grateful. The book is liberally illus-
trated with portraits and personalia ; though
one misses a portrait of Lady Wilde, and
several that one finds—hotel-keepers, Rus-
kin, French writers—have some air of com-
plimentary irrelevance.
Lhe first thing to say of Oscar Wilde s
that he was an Irishman. Therefore he was
eloquent, artistic, amorous, vain, witty,
ostentatious, extravagant, good-natured,
moody, unstable. There are Irishmen to
whom some of these adjectives do not apply ;
but they apply to the type, and Oscar Wilde
represented the type. He had little or
nothing of the quality that we have been
taught to call Celtic, was not a dreamer, was
haunted by no mystery. His writing and
conversation were rhetorical. They dealt
with outer aspects, not with inner sub-
limities, and were consciously designed for
an audience. Even in De Profund'is Wilde
is not talking to himself, but of himself.
He belonged to a genius-generation, a gene-
ration of unsound nerves. From hale bodies
and sluggish minds the generation of talent
is bred, less hale in body, less calm in
mind. In the second step from the physical
height comes, as the lot may fall, genius
—or disease —or crime. Physical energy,
reaching the highest pitch that the
organism can bear, seems to break like a
wave in the brain, the most delicate appara-
tus of the body, last fruit of evolution.
Wilde had the genius-temperament, but notr
genius-intensity. His mind was feminine
and receptive, not highly original, lacking
propulsive force. His beautiful verse is full
of imitations ; the epigrams of his brilliant
comedies decorate familiar dramatic themes,
like new spangles on Harlequin’s old jacket;
Ruskin inspired the Eesthetic pose, Whistler
claimed the ideas of the Renaissance
lecture and so on. Sherard mentions
that Wilde, because he was a boy, was a
disappointment to his mother, and for a long
time after his birth was treated as a girl,
talked to as a girl, dressed as a girl.
That mother, in the portrait prefixed to
Poems by Speranza, appears like her son
in features and facial contour. As
poetess, she had considerable vogue in
her day, y and probably more than
one Irish - Australian woman bears her
name. Sherard says, truly enough, that
her poetry, as poetry, “ has no particular
merit either of expression or thought.”
“ The Fenian editor, O’Leary, states that
* Speranza ’ was, of the four poetesses on
The Nation , the one who was considered the
least talented, that ‘ Eva’ was held to be the
most sincere and The most gifted.” Gavan
Duffy, however, had a high opinion o'f
“Speranza,” and she of him. Sherard re-
prints a famous Nation article, heroded
“Jacta Alea Est,” for which and others
Duffy was indicted for treason ; and recounts
how ‘ ‘ Speranza ’ ’—then Jane F rancesca Elgee,
though she dropped the “Jane” as unroman-
tic—dramatically took public credit or blame
for the article, and won Irish popularity
which had waned before she accepted a small
pension from the Sassenach. Sherard de-
scribes her as schwarmerisch, meaning that
she was a gushing sentimentalist unbalanced
in her life and her writings, one of those
women with a tendency to slop over.
Another woman who met her, when aged
fifty-seven, in London, says she looked like
“a tragedy queen at a suburban theatre.”
Wilde’s father, Sir William, vas a« famous
Irish surgeon, noted as an oculist. He was
exceptionally vital, exceptionally gifted, and
as an author he ranged from travel to
statistics. According to Sherard, his strong
amativeness went as far as brutality. Both
he and his wife sprang of distinguished
families ; and not far distant in their re-
lationships were McClure the explorer,
Maturin the novelist (from whose Melmoth
the Wanderer Wilde borrowed a pseudonym),
the Ouseley clan of soldiers, diplomatists
and clergy, and certain able doctors and
lawyers. The meeting of two such lives was
bound to bring nervous disaster to descen-
dants.
Oscar Fingal O’Flaherty Wills Wilde was
born 16th October, 1854, in Merrion Square,
Dublin. His father “wanted him to have a
good Irish name ” ; “ Wills ” was the name
of a fine Irish family, fi’om which sprang
W. G. Wills, still remembered as painter
and dramatist, to whom Oscar Wilde was
“ cousin in a near degree.” “In his youth
and early manhood he was proud of these
sounding patronymics. Later on he discarded
the use of them.”
They irritated him. To refer to them was to
provoke his great anger. They classified him;
they labelled him; they wrote him down as cle
so7i village; and this was intolerable to him, to
his cosmopolitan sense, to his disdain for par-
tisanships, politics, and protestations. _ Ho had
a strong aversion from what was local in interest...
and in all these ways his Irish Christian names
offended his taste. [Worse, they wounded his
vanity by making him feel himself a subject of
ridicule.]
For the rest, Oscar Wilde never willingly placed
himself on the losing side in any division of men.
Irishmen and Irish matters have always been un-
popular in the London society to which he
aspired, as they are in lower spheres of the Anglo-
Saxon Mob; and although Oscar Wilde never
denied his nationality lie took particular care
not to let it transpire.
Wilde was the second child. His elder
brother, Willy, was also degenerate from the
healthy physical type ; a younger sister died
before reaching womanhood, and is the sub-
ject of his beautiful “ Requiescat.” He was
brought up as a Protestant, and at eleven
years of age was sent to Portora Itoyal
School, at Enniskillen. There he exhibited
literary faculty, but excited no particular
attention. He is described as a big, heavy
boy who “used to flop about ponderously.”
His conduct was good. He had no notion
of arithmetic. He was not popular with the
other boys; for he kept aloof from them,
played no games, and gave them satirical
nicknames. Sherard refers to his reckless
mental combativeness, and quotes some
pretty instances from his later life.
Of one very famous contemporary Irish writer
he remarked : “He has no enemies, but he is
intensely disliked by his friends.”
Of an antipathetic Russian Jew he said he had a
face like a foetus in a bottle. “He came to Lon-
don in the hope of founding a salon. He has
succeeded in opening a restaurant.”
He wore his hair long, was always very
well dressed, and is remembered as the only
boy at Portora who used to wear a top hat.
“ He was very superior in his manner to-
wards Willy,” who was seemingly very
superior in his manner to the other boys.
Willy was called “Blue-Blood” ; because,
being remonstrated with for not washing
himself, lie “ declared very proudly that his
skin was dark, not because it was dirty, but
because of the blue blood in the veins of the
Wildes.” Sherard relates these things as
showing a certain social arrogance in the
brothers.
Wilde matriculated at Trinity College,
Dublin, in 1871, just before his seventeenth
birthday. This was the marking of his
matriculation paper (with a maximum of 10) :
(Jrreck , two papers—B, 8.
Latin, two papers —8, 7.
Latin Composition —4.
English Composition —s.
Hist or g —B.
Arithmetic —2.
In holidays he travelled in France and
Greece, imbibing ideals from both countries.
At his University he distinguished himself as
a scholar, and won the Berkeley gold medal.
Sherard suggests that it was his characteristic
incapacity for arithmetic which made it
necessary for him in 1883, “at a time when
his name was in everybody’s mouth, after his
successful visit to Paris, and while he was
lecturing all over England,” to go before a
London magistrate and “ make a statutory
declaration concerning the loss of a pawn-
ticket which was the voucher for Bishop
Berkeley’s gold medal.”
In 1875 Wilde went to Oxford University;
Sherard blames Oxford, in part or all, for
the subsequent downfall. Wilde himself
wrote that “the two great turning-points
in my life were when my father sent me to
Oxford, and when society sent me to prison.”
At Oxford Wilde came under Ruskin’s in-
fluence, and helped to mend roads—“ he
had the honour of filling Mr. Ruskin’s especial
wheelbarrow.” At Oxford he won the New-
digate prize for English verse, with “ Ra-
venna.” Presently he launched “ the
{esthetic movement,” published his poems,
lectured in America and England, edited
(significantly) The Woman’s World, won
French notice as conversationalist and as
author of Salome (he was a superb
French scholar), wrote his plays for
London theatres, and became prosperous
and famous. The rest is tolerably familiar.
Sherard collects many data, and throws tresn
li<d)t on many incidents. Wilde died at
Paris 30th November, 1900, in the beginning
of his forty-seventh year.
The tacts are sufficiently explanatory,
when taken in relation with the misfortune
of Wilde’s English environment. The Eng-
lish character is hostile to Irish exuberance.
It is not necessary to attribute blame.
simply the two do not mix. Like Sheridan,
like Lever, like G. B. Shaw, Wilde had
English admirers, but the general Eng-
lish sentiment remained mislike, due to
misunderstanding, and that due to
racial antipathy. As playwright he had
to overbear opposition which would
not have followed men with a tithe of his
talent. Fancy Wilde, an artist, writing for
an audience typified by a conscientious
dullard like Clement Scott—casting opals
before oxen. Yet he succeeded, by force of
sheer brilliancy, in extorting unwilling
praise, and died ruined by excesses the seeds
of which had been sown in generations
before him. Man of wit, man of art and
heart, man of sincere aspiration, as he de-
serves censure he earns pity, and in the
sources of his obloquy are the roots of his
fame.
ANCESTRAL MEMORY.
As to memory of ancestral impressions:
“Nil” writes: —The “I’ve been here before;
I know what is coming next ” sensation does not
necessarily indicate memory of individual experi-
ences or thoughts in the present life, and still less
bo those of a previous existence. I believe the fac-
tors are intrinsic to the individual brain ; and that
the two most important are subconscious memory,
and the anticipation of perception by observation.
As to subconscious memory. Sherlock Holmes
said that his brain was an attic in which there
was no room for lumber, and that he stored only
those things in it which would be of future use to
him. This is an impossible choice. Probably
of all the millions of records the brain makes,
only a small percentage can be recalled volun-
tarily. Nevertheless, none are obliterated (unless,
perhaps, pathologically, or traurnatically), and I
take dreams, visions, portents (in a less direct
way), and*the impressions in delirium, to be made
up of more or less incoordinate and kaleidoscopic
collections of the records not available for con-
scious memory, which is true recollection. And,
to some extent, these also go to form the “ mental
throw-back” sensation.
As to the second factor: perhaps I should have
said “anticipation of conception by observation.”
The eye or ear is quite capable of making an ob-
servation, and the brain of perceiving it, appreci-
ably sooner than the mind can conceive it, and so
place it in its proper category, according as it
appears to be familiar, forgotten, or new. In cer-
tain conditions the mind is incapable of exercising
this function correctly. And then, taking into
association subconscious memory, we have a favour-
able arrangement of matters for the “ done this
before ” impression.
It is significant that the scenery of these
pseudo-recollections is frequently of a nature
likely to be familiar to the individual. For
ample, a person who has never had any chance of
knowing of the existence of a Roman fortress, or
any cognate matters, will, I venture to prognose,
never have any pseudo-recollections of such
things. A man with no (present) musical percep-
tions will not have “anticipatory” impressions
of an overture which he he has never heard be-
fore (I have myself experienced this form,
amongst others).
J*
Which seems fallacious. A perception which
is not also a conception is the perception -that
remains subconscious. The point in the present
argument is that immediately it reaches conscious-
ness it is classified as familiar : “ I have been here
before.”—However, we were looking for evidence,
not theory.
“JnssO” supplies a fragment. He says that when 12
years olcl he was driving to visit his grandmother’s birth-
place in New England. “ I was accompanied by my father.
Neither my father nor myself knew where our ancestral
home had stood. About’three miles out cf the town we
came upon the remains of a stone chimney, and a pro-
fusion of garden flowers gone wild. I looked at the place
and at once recognised it. I got my father to stop and
let me gather Howers, and I was very loth to leave when
his patience was exhausted. I argued with my father,
that we had lived there at sometime, and yet this was
mv first trip from home over four hundred miles away.
On enquiring from old residents we found that the ruined
chimney was all that was left of my grandmother’s birth-
place.”
Ergo, if the idea of ancestral memory hold
good, “Jusso” reincarnated his grandmother—
at least to the extent that his brain-cells, possibly
by identity of substance or similarity of arrange-
ment, held impressions that originally had been
stored in hers—jusso as he may have had “his
grandmother’s eyes,” or her nose, or her trick of
pouring out the “tea” without putting in the
tea-leaves, etc.
L.F. reminds us of the explanation that the lobes of the
bra;n may work asynchronously, the impression in one
succeeding by a perceptible interval of time (which yet is
not measurable by consciousness) the impression of the
other. This explanation, however, supposes that both
lobes function to the same ends, which is erroneous ; and
that there are dual cognitive as well as dual sensory
centres—which, at any given time, is also probably
erroneous.
“ Brehon” has merely had a peculiar dream, which is
outside the demanded category of “ remembered before.”
Several of us have had peculiar dreams, but ths chances
are they arise from the subconscious niemery of indivi-
dual experience.
MEMORANDA
According to The Athenceum, in a new edition
of his essay on Blake, Swinburne presently will
be found piercing and scattering the inane and
transcendent bosh that has been uttered and
written regarding so-called Celtic literature. For
as a matter of fact there is n’t any Celtic litera-
ture worth regarding. And S. adds: .
Some Hibernian commentator on Blake, if I rightly
remember a fact so insignificant, has somewhere said
something to some such effect as that I, when writing
about some fitfully audacious and fancifully delirious
deliverance of the poet he claimed as a countryman,
and trying to read into it some coherent and imagina-
tive significance, was innocent of any knowledge of
Blake’s meaning.
It is possible, if the spiritual fact of his Hibernian
heredity has been or can be established, that I was: for
the excellent reason that, being a Celt, he now and then
too probabl.v had none worth the labour of deciphering—
or at least worth the serious attention of any student be-
longing to a race in which reason and imagination are the
possibly' preferable substitutes for fever and fancy.
That, now, is talking. “But in that case,'* S.
proceeds :
it must be gladly and gratefully admitted that the
Celtic tenuity of his sensitive and prehensile intelligence
throws into curious relief the occasional flashes of inspira-
tion, the casual fits of insight, which raise him to the
momentary level of a deep and free thinker as well as a
true and immortal poet. The vein of sound reason in
Blake’s eccentric and fitful intelligence has never been
adequately acknowledged or perceived.
Without committing itself to details, the News-
paper Society, representing the proprietaries of
the most important U.K. newspapers, has ac-
cepted the principle of Govt, control of publica-
tion of news in war-time ; and a Bill upon the
subject probably will be introduced to Parliament.
The Review of Reviews looked like a hopeless
proposition in journalism, but Editor Judkins has
found what may be a way of solving it—by
subordinating the literary to the social aspect,
and raising a cohort of subscribers round
Humanity’s banner with an H. Saying nothing
about the ends, the technical ingenuity of the
means is respeotable.
Part XX of The Forest Flora ofH.S. Wales , by J.
11. Maiden (Syd., Govt. Printer; Is.) amounts to a
Boon for anyone interested in local trees. It is a
summary part, giving additional information in
regard to trees already dealt with, and is made
uncommonly valuable by sixteen photographio
plates. These are the very things that hitherto
it has been difficult to get, and for printing them
together and selling them so cheaply Mr. Maiden
earns gratitude. The part includes notes on
timbers for industrial use.
Scott Campbell, in “Below the Dead Line”
and “The Adventures of Felix Boyd,” is writing
for The Popular Magazine the best detective
stories since Sherlock Holmes.
The series of primers of “Religions Ancient
and Modern,” in course of publication by Con-
stable (Bond., Is. net) is remarkable for lucidity.
The plan is to take a form of belief like “Anim-
ism” or “Buddhism,” and show precisely whatit
stands for, divested of extrinsies. The books
scarcely reach the compass of an encyclopedia
article, yet are so contrived that they gain by
compression : the writers are the fittest to be
found. The last little volume of Magic and
Fetishism, by A. C. Haddon, is the clearest ex-
position of this difficult subject that one has seen.
Many of its illustrations are drawn from the
practice of Australian aborigines.
The new Life of Robert Burns can be commended
to friendly Scotch enemies. The author is John
Macintosh, already creditably to be reckoned
with : the publisher Alex. Gardner (Paisley;
2s. G d. net). Aim is to give a compact and
popular life of the bard who encouraged us to tak
aff our drams (in an age when whisky was possibly
of superior quality, whatever may be said of
freedom). Macintosh lacks particular inspiration
of style, but minds his facts and tells a straight-
forward tale discreetly (one misses the chapter on
“ Burns and the Lasses ”). There are ten relevant
illus. which should have been printed better.
In Graphical History (Melb., W. _P. Linehan ;
4 d.) J. E. S. Henerie develops ingeniously a
method of plotting facts in relation to dates, s® as
to give at a glance the historical value of a move-
ment or epoch. The idea is worth scrutiny bv
teachers, who may devote it to better purpose than
Mr. Henerie does. His suggestions for detailed
study of English history imply so much wasted
time for an Australian boy, who, in primary
schools at least, wants to learn things of real
utility. All that the primary Australian scholar
needs of English history is a summary and know-
ledge where to find details if he need them.
Then he will have time to learn a summary of
the history of the world. There is a yarn of
Johnny Paterson, the Victorian painter, which
says that he commented upon the departure of a
man who had told him certain gross stories with,
“ Eh ! but the mind o’ yon mon is verra much oot
o’ drawing ! ” And Mr. Henerie’s historical mind
is very much out of Australian perspective.

Every student of human nature(says The Radies’
Field) must have been struck with the extraordinary
vitalityand vigour possessed byall thoroughly disagreeable
persons. They invariably exceed the allotted threescore-
and-ten span of life.
The second number of The Antiquarian Gazette,
published by Isidore Kozminsky at 339 Collins-street,
Melbourne, combines for readers pleasure arid business,
and is welcome as far as it goes. At present it goes only
four pages farther than the cover.
A good new sixpenny is The Procession of Life,
by H, A. Vachell.. .Haeckel’s Evolution of Man (6tf. net
for Part I) is worth I*. intrinsically, and humanly is in-
valuable. Commended particularly to studious youth,
to meditative bushnien, to school-teachers, to workers
libraries. Part II will be ready' by the year-end: the two
parts are one indispensable, if you don’t know them.
J. Say. : The pleasure of labour must remain your re-
ward. Critically: you want piquancy.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Clank! Clink!
Various Bulletin contributors badly want a Song of
Australia. ‘
Oh Poet! hear the people who are calling from
the dark,
And see the sullen faces which are waiting for a
spark
To penetrate the gloomy brains of our poetic
bands t
And illuminate the ages with their songs of
Austral lands.
Out of the hovel and putrid street,
Out of foul dens of prey,
There comes the shuMe of shackled feet,
Cavernous faces, and forms in gray,
With frozen hearts and foreheads dank —
Old England’s sons come here to stay,
And sing us a song of “ Clank ! C{ank l ”
But Poet ! we have thrown aside the chains of
British crimes.
And free and eager listen to the strains of other
climes ;
And now we want to flaunt a song that we can
sing with all
Our might and main, and make our shores re-echo
with, the call.
Out of the homes of stocks and shares —..
Out of the usurer’s loins—
There comes the Jew with his gleaming snares,
Covetous hands and sweated coins,
With smirking smiles and ghoulish wink.
Old Israel has come with his golden link,
Ami his siren song of “ Clink 1 Clink ! ”
'N.S.W.
SPRING.
A Bridal Song.
She is more sparkling beautiful
' Than dawn-light seen thro’ tears
The weeping worlds of Paradise
Shed down upon the spheres.
Her eyes are bright and passionate
With love’s immortal flame—
The flowers of a wildwood tree
In petals write her name.
Her breath o’ life’s so wond’rous sweet
The bees halt, in amaze,
Their streaming honey-scented fleet
Above the meadovv ways ;
And ev’ry little singing thing
Atween the breasted hill
And God’s high-vaulted cloistering
Up-raises with a will
Pteams of laud, and cheery chaunts
Of her, who now is mines—
Queen-Angel of angelic haunts
Thro’ months of mead and wine.
N.S.W.
HUGH McCRAE.
A Seychelles Moon.
The crescent moon was in when we arrived,
A silver bow hung ’mid a swarm of stars,
All settled like “ Ligurians ” yet unhived
On Night’s dark purple pallium past the bars •
Of such gay sunset glories as survived,
Aureolins, violets, lilacs, cinnabars,
And fifty other tints that.one might mention,
But faded.ere they’d claimed your fixed attention.
This Moon of ours, each night in growing splen-
dor,
We watch, we love, we worship, we regard,
As, bright, her face between the palm-shafts
slender
Glows like a silver lamp, with cross lines barred.
Where coco-plumes distinct and dark and tender
Show lozenged paries between, the light to guard,
The grand east-window of our forest-fane
With stars and planets ; clouds of every stain.
And, as she grew, our love for her grew stronger ;
We sat out late, we sang but never danced ;
We lingered on and lingered ever longer,
By all those soft and silver moonbeams tranced.
Yea! longter, longer ; longer, ever longer;
Beneath the moon-drown’d palm-trees as they
glanced
All, dripping silver on our cool pavilion,
The broad verandah and the old grey skillion.
VIC.
G.G.McC.
A Ballade of Ocean Beaches.
I weary of the sun-scorched plain,
Its yellow, sere monotony ;
I want the whisper of the rain,
I want the great blue, surging sea,
- With all its murmurous mystery ;
My weary soul, my aching brain,
That sicken of the hot, bare land,
Are hungry for the wizardry
Of crested wave and cool, wet sand.
Out here the moon may waste and wane,
She cannot work her witchery,
She needs the tossing, tumbling main
To croon to her its melody,
And then she glows, a fantasy ;
Ah, once I heard that haunting strain
By dusk-dimmed shore, by star-lit strand-;
But now I dream, and dream in vain
.Of crested wave and cool, wet sand.
Ajl day the surge’s strong refrain
Sounds in my soul incessantly,
And in the night I hear again
The wand’ring waters’ harmony,
The ocean’s siren minstrelsy.
Oh, just to wash away the stain
Of dust eternal and be fanned
By winds that sing exultaiitly
Of crested wave and cool, wet sand !
Stern Fate or Chance, whiche’er you be,
Lift for a space your iron hand,
And grant once more the joy to me
Of crested wave and cool; wet sand.
N.s.w.
WILFRID MAILUER.
“ Itari ” : Gympie (Queerisland), the very
name of which suggests gold, is now becoming
a dairying centre. The volcanic scrub lands of the
district were carefully avoided by the older
Queenslanders, but dairymen from the Big Scrub
of the Richmond River (N.S.W.'),' who had
flourished on similar country, found that they
could sell out their N.S.W. farms up to £4O per
acre, and get the Gympie country for £1 or £2,
and they commenced an exodus. The new indus-
try became infectious, and now the Queensland
Lands Department cannot get this country opened
up quickly enough to accommodate the rush of set-
tlers. So Gympie, which was sagging in the middle
byreasonof the vanishing gold returns, is beginning
to thrive on butter, with extra prospects ahead.
The Blackall ranges, in the neighborhood, are
already known for their luscious strawberries,
sold in Sydney three or four months before the
local crop arrives, and many things besides straw-
berries are expected of them. Gympie is fortu-
nate with a second string to its bow—-unlike
Charters Towers, where poppet-heads rear them-
selves up with monotonous regularity, and never
the whirr-r-r is heard of a hurrying cream-
separator, or a furious discussion seen in the local
papers about the right way to feed the cow’s baby,
or whether Sunday’s milk should be separated or
run into the creek owing to the fourth command-
ment.
The Wrights, father and son, were pot-
tering at a coal outcrop in the Gippsland hills,
pending the flotation, and they were very
desolate, particularly Wright junior, who was
only 21. They had been on the job two months
before they discovered they had a neighbor some
three miles down the other slope of the range.
Billy Wright went to look the Gables up one
Sunday afternoon. They were ’possumers on a
small scale, and cultivators on a smaller scale,
and seemed to live on two cows and a cab-
bage patch. There were four Gables, father,
son, mother and daughter, and they were
a weird family large, hairy, bony, un-
couth people. The son seemed two removes
from an idiot. The daughter, Kate, was 26, a
very tall female in very short clothes, all edges
and feet. Billy stayed for a few hours. A week
later he called again when out shooting, and three
Sundays after that he looked in during the evening.
The whole family of Gables grinned at him, and
winked and nudged, and presently old Gable got
up, and said: “ Well, I s’pose you young uns
don’t want us round. We’ll go to bed.”
“What,” said Billy, “to bed at a quarter to
eight?” “Oh, we ain’t got nothink else
t’ do, ’n’ you ’n’ Katie- .don’t want
us round, I ken see. Come along, Andy, shift
yerself.” “ But,” said the amazed visitor, “you
mustn’t go on my account. If you do I shall
leave at once.” “Gerrout!” gurgled Gable,
“ don’t I know what young lovers are. You’ll be
wantin’ Katie by yerself, 1 bet.” “But I don’t,”
cried Billy, backing towards the door. “ What! ”
cried the old man, “do I understand you’re goin’
back on my dorter?” “I never had any idea,”
protested Billy. “ I’m sure I’ve done nothing to
lead you tothink”—- — “Then you bin cornin’here,
loafin’ round my place, leadin’ my girl to believe
you was meanin’ marriage, ’n’ after all you're
goin’t’ crawl out ! ” Old Gable went for his gun,
Mrs. Gable caught up a pan, Andy made a motion
to attack, and Kate reachedfor a chunk of firewood.
The wood hit the door as Billy fled, the frying-
pan caught him in the back, and old Gable had
two shots at him before he got out of range. Billy
Wright never called on those neighbors again.

The unfortunate DRUNKARD has a bad look out, no matter which way he views the scene. His financial
position is as rocky as his gait, while his body is tottering on the brink of ruin along with his moral and social
reputation. Take up your morning paper, and you are nearly certain to read of a man being in prison for a
crime committed when under the influence of liquor. Drink attacks all classes, rich and poor, old and young,
men and women. It by no means follows that every Drunkard gets into prison—far sadder results often
happen—the gradual ruin of a fine business—the slow, but sure, loss of practice by the professional man
—the neglect of personal appearance—the shabbiness of the family—all point out that the terrible curse of
ALCOHOL is there, and the news spreads as though shouted from the house-top. These present times in
Australia are too hard to allow any man’s sobriety to be questioned. It may be—it is a difficult habit to break
oneself of, but Eucrasy will help you and strengthen you for the fight. Don’t neglect the matter one moment
longer, but call or write to us at once. NOTE OUR ADDRESS—

packages, are forwarded in plain envelopes or wrappers, sealed from observation. We make no charges for
personal consultations or for books of particulars and other literature dwelling upon the subject of

Correspondents to The Bulletin ivould oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking sortie care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (ivith regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for "copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY. AUGUST 9, 1906.
SOUTH AFRICA.
Run yard Kipling has lately broken out with a
poem on politics in South Africa ; so the position
must be indeed desperate. Probably it is the
swan-song of British Jingo-Imperialism in that
continent, for—if it is possible in public affairs
to forecast the future from the present—nothing
seems more certain than that the British cause in
South Africa is doomed. The wish is not father
to this thought. The Bulletin recognises that
the hope of peace for Australia is so closely bound
up with the stability of the British Empire that
any such great change as the loss of South Africa
would bring grave anxiety, and possibly grave
danger, to the Commonwealth. So —in spite of
all disagreement with that unjust and wicked
war which was waged to establish British rule
and German-cwm- Jewish dominion over the
African continent south of the Zambesi —this
paper would wish, for the sake of peace in Aus-
tralia, to see peace established in the Afrikander
community. But no wish can alter facts, or the
logical conclusion from facts ; and it is difficult to
avoid the conclusion that South Africa is lost to
the British flag. Perhaps, by a strange irony of
fate, events will ripen quickly, and the present
British Liberal Government, which, as an
Opposition, did all it could to avert the
cruelty of the war and the calamity of the
subsequent Chinese-labor decision, will have to
shoulder the ignominy of the loss. Perhaps
Fate will be more just, and the party of Jingoism
and Chinese Labor will have to face the storm
which it brewed. Perhaps even a miracle may
happen and by some marvellous development of
wise statesmanship the British position yet be
saved. But it will need a miracle.
* * * *
When the war to subdue the Boer Republics—
nominally in the interests of an all-British South
Africa, really as a move in the game of inter-
national finance—was first entered upon by the
organisation of the Cecil Rhodes - J ameson
Raid, there were many reasons given in
its apology. The Boers were tyrannous ; they
did not wash; Kruger had a prejudice against
Englishmen ; he had no idea of the use of a spit-
toon ; and so on. The ultimate limit of cant
and blasphemy among the apologists was reached
by that Bishop of the Church of England who
preached a sort of holy war, arguing that the
Boers represented Old Testament ideas, while the
Cohens, who ran the issue on the British side,
represented the new revelation of Christ ! It
was this disgusting and ribald cant which
organised in British communities, among decent
people of British blood, the main oppo-
sition to the war. If it had been carried
on throughout with the frank brutality and
illegality of the Raid—if Britain had said
flatly that it was out to steal territory, as Russia,
Austria, and Prussia stole Poland or as Prussia
stole from Denmark, and as almost every nation
is willing to steal when it is able—a good deal of
pro-Boer argument would have been, smothered.
The issue would then have been narrowed down
to that of advisability, whether the loot was wortli
the burglary, whether it was wise to set the bad
example of wiping out two inoffensive white
democracies. But inveterate habit made Britain
cant from first to last (at least, not Britain, for
the soul of Britain was not with the war, and every
great living Briton at the time protested against
it, buttheinob, rich and poor, which ruled Britain);
and in the process of this canting there were
given many assurances which now constitute some
of the chief reasons why the British position in
South Africa is untenable. The war was to estab-
lish a pure democracy in South Africa ; that assur-
ance stands in the way of holding the conquered
territory as a province under martial law, which
now is the only sure way of holding it. The war
was to relieve the blacks from Boer tyranny ; the
blacks heard this, and have been expecting some-
thing in the way of benefits ever since, and show
their disappointment by a very expensive revolt,
and by a clear intention to side with any
enemy against Britain in the future. This
war was to free South Africa of Boer
mismanagement, and usher in an era of
wonderful prosperity ; it has made South Africa
practically bankrupt, and the discontented whites
holding dishonored British promissory notes for
prosperity and clamoring for some change—
any change—are not the least formidable of the
difficulties which bristle around the British ad-
ministration.
* # * w
But the difficulties which were sown during the
waging of the war, and which have come up to
such a prolific crop of trouble since, are, after all,
insignificant beside the disastrous blunder which
followed. Britain having, by a slow process of
attrition and “ concentration,” partially conquered
the Boers in the field, and having finished the
process by bribing the unconquerable remnant of
the farmers to lay down their arms, could have
ignored all the cant that had been indulged
in, and could have safely swept aside all the
promises to natives, Jews, whites, if it had garri-
soned the territory won. If 50,000 British citizens
had been poured into the Transvaal to work the
mines, South Africa would have been won for
good, to develop ultimately on the lines of Canada
or Australia. The new colonists would have been,
owing to the. special circumstances of their migra-
tion, stubborn in British, as opposed to Boer, senti-
ment. With them to rely upon, a fully democratic
Constitution might at once have been granted to
the conquered territory ; and if Boer or native ob-
jected, the immigrants could shoulder a rifte as well
as cast a vote for the British cause. But this most
obvious decision of statecraft was flouted. The
Balfour-Chamberlain Government, at them ging
of Cohen, decided on Chinese labor for the mines,
and thus passed sentence of expulsion on the
white miner, who will not, in any part of the
world, work side by side with the Chinaman.
True, it was Cohen’s war, not Britain’s. It was
the international financiers, not the British
people, who organised the Jameson raid (wherein
the British flag skulked behind a buccaneer stan-
dard) and the later Chamberlain raid, which
flaunted the Union Jack openly. But the
war, though Cohen’s, was fought with British
blood and British gold, and at such a cost
of both as none dreamed at the outset
would be necessary. Having carried through the
bitter campaign, Britain might, without unfair-
ness, have turned aside from Cohen —who had
instigated the war as a “ picnic,” requiring 20,000
men and £5,000,000 of money—and have arranged
the post-bellum conditions in the interests of the
Empire rather than of foreign finance. But Bal-
four and Chamberlain were bound to Cohen,
and, to that bond traitorously true, they sanc-
tioned the employment of Chinese slave labor for
the Rand mines. In doing that they not only
lost the British garrison, the industrial army
of occupation, but they deeply disappointed
all South African whites whom they did
not actually antagonise and embitter, and
armed the natives with a new grievance against
the white man. Now the British cause is with-
out a real friend in South Africa. The idealists
who, saw in the continuance of British rule the
best hope of a great Afrikander nation, are of the
mood White Australians would be in if Britain
decreed that Asiatics must be admitted to their
Commonwealth ; the Boers are more deeply con-
vinced than ever that British aims are sordid,
base and anti-national; the natives, who were
probably as well treated under the Kruger regime
as it was wise to treat them, are disappointed at
finding no betterment in their condition, and
seethe with discontent; that part of the white
population which, without having any very fixed
ideas on the patriotic issues involved, looked
to the war to cause a S’African “ boom ” and
find that it has brought the country to deep de-
pression, are sore and resentful. Even Cohen
growls— Cohen for whom the war was waged and
for whom Chinese labor was secured— Cohen
growls because still his millions do not increase
quickly enough. Britain is left thus with the
faithful aid only of the “Johnny” officials who
have been imported to run the civil service ; and
their aid is of little value at anything more serious
than a ping-pong tournament.
* * * ■*
What the ultimate fate of South Africa is to be
there is no possibility of judging, except that it
will almost certainly not be a part of the British
Empire. The white population may possibly be
organised into an Afrikander Federation—a power
strong enough to keep the natives in check and to
make impossible an invasion bv a European
Power. The overwhelming mass of the blacks
may win the territory back for Ethiopia and pro-
nounce the doom of Dutch and British alike.
Whatever comes the issue will profoundly affect
Australia. When Britain decided to exterminate
the Boer Republics it was of serious prejudice to
this Commonwealth, which is interested in main-
taining the theory of the inviolability of white
communities, however small. When the decision
was come to and. the war waged and won, Aus-
tralian interest lay in an effective British occupa-
tion of the conquered territory, since grave trouble
to Great Britain would possibly involve the dis-
memberment of the Empire. An effective British
occupation was sacrificed for a scandalous Chinese
occupation, and a position created which must,
unless a miracle intervenes, cause a disaster
shortly. That position provides another pressing
reason why Australia should organise its defences.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Astounding Lies of Anti-Sosh.
The Bulletin has received from Brisbane two
remarkable documents issued in the interests of
the Anti-Sosh party, of which George Held is
the head and front, the eye-glass, the stomach,
the bulging waistcoat, and the chief ornament.
They are remarkable for a breadth and depth and
richness of mendacity which are almost beyond
the resources of the "English language —and yet
the latest dictionaries show that there are a quar-
ter of a million words in that language, and it is
still growing. The less conspicuous publication
of the two is issued from the Courier Buildings,
and purports to be signed by one John Cameron
—at least the signature looks like that. John
Cameron —if it is he—concludes with an earnest
cadge for funds wherewith to fight the scheme
for supplying the farmers with land, and gives
some of the reasons why he should be trusted
with the money, in these words : —
Federal Land-Tax. —I need hardly point out the
necessity there is for action to be taken in this matter.
Already the Federal Premier, Mr. Deakin (urged thereto
by the leader of the Labor party in the Federal Legisla-
ture), has expressed his approval to the introduction of a
Federal Land-Tax. It is suggested that the amount of
this tax should be Is. in the £1.
So far, the amount proposed by the Parliamentary
advocates of the Federal land-tax has been from
Jd. up to 4d. in the £ on estates worth over
£5000—not Is. in the £ all round. So the lie that
issues from the Courier Buildings consists in sup-
pressing all mention of the £5000 exemption ; and
all mention of the fact that an estate worth
£00,000 would only pay 4d. in the £ on the last
£10,000 or so, and that the average on the whole
estate would be about 2d. in the £ ; also in multi-
plying the tax by 24 and the 4d. tax by
three. And these are not very remarkable lies
and suppressions for the anti-Sosh party. They
don’t represent its best efforts, so to speak, and
are only in the way of a preliminary canter. But
it rises to the occasion in a flaring black-and-red
handbill, wherein the case for the anti-Sosh
party—that crowd of blasphemous liars which
takes the name of religion in vain as part of its
argument, a,nd claims Gon and the Church as
supports of its propaganda—is set out afresh, not
by mere suppression, but by direct Ananias and
Sapphira
FEDERAL LAND TAX.
How it Will Affect tiie Queensland Farmer.
Say you have a farm of.. 80 acre > 200 acres 640 acres
at £5 at £2 10s. at £1
= £400. =£500. =£640.
Your Municipal and Shire
rates, at 2d. in the £
will be £3 6 8 £4 3 4 £5 6 8
The FEDERAL LAND
TAX at Is. in the £
will be 20 0 0 25 0 0 32 0 0
Add to which the State
Treasurer’s Tax of, say,
6d. in the £ .. .. 10 0 0 12 10 0 16 0 0
This would mean a total
for the Three Taxes of £33 6 8 £41 13 4 £53 6 8
Which you will have to pay EVERY year.
Note.— The above Taxes will be in addition to the
Income Tax.
This may be classed as rather a special effort. In
ali statements of the Labor party’s scheme of a
Federal graduated land-tax the thing that has
been made most clear is that the estate which is
worth less than £5000 is to pay nothing. The
wail about the farmer with £400 worth of land
who will pay £20 a year is a lie direct—the tax is
a lie, the Is. in the £ is a lie, and the £20 is a lie.
Even if that oppressed imaginary farmer owned
£5400 worth of land instead of £400 worth, the
Labor party only proposes to make him pay
16s. 8d.—which is a vast deal too little. Similarly
the jeremiad about the farmer with £500 worth of
land who is to pay £25 is a lie ; that farmer will
also pay nothing. And the broken-hearted
lamentation about the bucolic person with £640
worth of land who will pay £32 is a lie; he will
likewise pay nothing. The whole plan and in-
tention of the proposed Federal graduated land-
tax is to break up the great estates so as to supply
bucolic persons of this class with untaxed land, and
to so multiply their class that if they have ever to
go to the polls to vote against a scheme for the im-
position of the imaginary taxes mentioned in the
red and black document quoted above, they will
be able to muster 100 votes where t’ ey now count
one, and will be able to resist the alleged enemy
where now they are helpless. The attempt to
beguile the small farmer into voting against the
measure which is intended exclusively for his
benefit is worthy of the Anti-Sosh crowd—a
gathering which about holds the world’s record
for impudence and unreliability.

Patent Poison and Swindle.
Last week the House of Reps, debated a motion
by J osiah Thomas, affirming that all patent
medicines imported into the Commonwealth
should be made to declare upon their labels their
exact pharmaceutical composition. In the course
of the discussion it listened to some weird truths,
related by Oarty Salmon (a medical practitioner),
regarding the composition of some of the alleged
“cures” and medicines that are brought into
Australia by the hundred thousand bottles or
packages. One infants’ remedy—to be given to
baliies at the rate of three tea-spoonfuls a day
—contains 42 per cent, of proof spirit. An alleged
fruit syrup contains no fruit, but is composed
of ginger, senna, and podophyllin—podophyllin
being an active poison. Salmon, in fact, was
able to say that, within the week, a man
had died from an overdose of it. Probably all
over Australia fond mothers have been cheerfully
pouring podophyllin into the baby every time it
cried. “ The effect of these foods and medicines
on infants,” says Salmon, “is worse than starva-
tion. The attempt of their stomachs and intes-
tines at assimilation causes a wasting more rapid
than even starvation will accomplish.” Un-
doubtedly the proprietors of such medicines must
be aware of their contents, and of their deleterious
nature ; yet they go on spiling them, and in most
instances raise a grieved howl against any pro-
posal to make them divulge their composition.
Salmon had more instances. A well-ad vertised con-
sumption cure contains no medicinal substance but
opium—the effect of which is not to cure, but to
deaden the pain, and at the same time to plant
in the unhappy patient the seeds of a craving
which make him an opium fiend as well as a lung-
less shadow. A “strength-restorer” and “blood-
builder ” (nearly all the patent medicines are that)
is made of juniper and a preparation of broom —
in fact, gin and bitters. There is an “electric”
cure for deafness, and its principle is the insertion
in the ear of a solution of red pepper. The
pleasing warmth of the pepper is supposed by the
deluded patient to be due to electric action.
“This apparatus,” Salmon said, “costs 30s.;
commercially it is worth 3d. ; remedially it is
valueless.” The House agreed to Thomas’s
resolution, Minister Lyne declaring that under
his Commerce Act or Customs Act he had
power to enforce all that Thomas asked
for. If that be so, there should be no
delay in exposing the contents of the imported
nostrum to the cold breeze of publicity. The
Commonwealth, however, has, unfortunately, no
power to overhaul drugs and medicines manufac-
tured within its boundaries, that being still a
matter for State control, and it can only be hoped,
in a hopeless sort of way, that if the Common-
wealth does its duty the States will follow suit.
Everywhere in the world chemical analysis tell 3
the same story about patent medicines—that
babies are killed, women debauched, and sick
men made worse by swallowing these two-
penn’orths of nefarious drugs which are sold at
2s. 9d. or 3s. 6d., with a reduction if you take a
quantity and die sooner.

The Difficulties of the Australian
Manufacturer.
Ip Australian manufactures are to be properly
pushed ’ by legislative action (strong protective
Tariff, an Anti-Trust Bill, occasional Government
bonuses, &c.), how comes it that the professing
patriots, who rail so loudly about the prejudices
of the purchasing public, and bewail the difficulty
in getting Australian goods judged on their solid
merits .how comes it that these wordy exponents
of practical legislation have neglected to protect
the local manufacturer against antagonistic re-
tailers? Clearly enough the growth of Aus-
tralian manufactures all these years has been
stunted by the suppressive tactics of shop-
keepers, notably the big shopkeepers who are
also importers on their own account. Local
products have been belittled by the people to
whom the gullible purchasing public looks for
advice and guidance as to the quality of the goods
they require. It is true that public opinion has
lately withdrawn the shopkeeper’s ancient license
to persistently slander Australian goods merely
because they are not imported. Public opinion
is at last educated up to a certain point of
sympathy with the local manufacturer, and
reasonable faith in his capacity for under-
standing his business. So the shopman needs to
be diplomatic in his indirect disparagement of
Australian workmanship. But the old trouble
goes on nevertheless. Where the retailer is
actually retailing locally-made goods in competi-
tion with the imported he usually endeavors to
suppress the manufacturer’s name. The foreign
manufacturer gets the advertisement of his brand,
whereas the local maker is, in the majority of
cases, prevented from announcing himself to the
public. The Victorian hat trade affords striking
illustration of the retailers’ unpatriotic policy.
The Denton Hat Mills turns out tens of
thousands of felt hats per annum, which bear any
brand except the name of the manufacturer.
Various fancy titles are stamped upon the lining,
and when the order, given through a wholesale
house, is of sufficient importance the hats are said
to be “specially made for So-and-8o,” the retail-
ers. But they give no advertisement to local
manufacture. Their origin is kept dark by the
shopkeeper in order that they may pass for
imported with any customers who love foreign
goods for their foreignness. The Austral Hat
Mills (Melbourne) are making a plucky attempt
to fight single-handed the battle of all the local
mills, but the manager reports great difficulty
in making headway against the obstructive
demands of many retailers. At the beginning
of this year the Austral Mdls inaugurated
the system of branding hats with the maker’s
name, leaving no room for doubt as to where they
came from. In short, the local hat manufacturer
claimed the right to advertise himself on his
goods as freely as, say, a foreign lamp manufac-
turer advertises himself. To this new Australian
departure the large retailers are bitterly opposed,
in many cases. They will place orders for the
“Austral” hats provided that the “Austral”
brand be omitted. The goods must appear as
being “made specially for So-and-So,” but
they mustn’t take credit for their Australian
nationality. All these retail markets are
closed to the manufacturer who is striving
to popularise his local products under their
true designation.. The employment of local labor
in hat-making is checked by the autocratic re-
tailer. He refuses to further the advertising of
a local brand, although he makes a business
feature of the brand of certain imported hats
which he could not buy without the brand, even
if he wanted to. A simple cure for this trading
evil suggests itself immediately to every thinking
citizen, except the few who sit in Parliament and
are paid to think about such matters. An im-
proved tariff will, in due course, protect the
Australian market from foreign, fever-den, felt
hats, but meanwhile it is the duty of every State
Government to ordain that all Australian goods,
which are commonly marked with any name, shall
bear the brand of the manufacturer, frankly and
openly set forth.
4.
The N.S.W. Gambling Bill.
N.S.W. Attorney-General Wade’s Bill for the
“ regulation and suppression ” of gambling seems
to involve a contradiction in its title, but —passing
the apparent grammatical absurdity—that repre-
sents the object of the measure. Gambling is to
be regulated on racecourses and sports grounds,
and suppressed everywhere else, even in sport-
ing clubs such as Tattersall’s. The regula-
tion goes this far: that on sports grounds,
if the promoters wish, betting may be abso-
lutely prohibited, and persons attempting to
bet may be removed, and, in the event of contu-
macy, arrested ; and the number of race meetings
(where betting would certainly be encouraged by
the promoters) is limited. There can be no addi-
tion to the present number of race-courses. Any
race-course where the running track has a less cir-
cumference than six furlongs must be shut up.
No race-course shall be used for race-meetings on
more than 24 days in any one year; and no
club, association, or person may hold more
than 16 days’ racing in any one year. There
is not much doubt that these provisions could
be effectively administered. The evil of betting
at sports gatherings which the promoters wish to
keep free of the bookmaker, and the hypocrisy of
the sports committee which professes to forbid
betting, but really encourages it, will be alike
ended. The limitation of the number of race
meetings is another easily effected and most
desirable reform. At present there is hoyse-
racing of a sort in and around Sydney nearly 300
days of the year. It may be all for the “ improve-
ment of the breed of horses,” but a good deal of
this attention to the parentage of the noble
animal can be spared for the sake of devoting
a little more time to the improvement of
the breed of man (who is also a noble animal).
It is in regard to the suppression of street, house
and club betting that the Gambling Bill meets
with its most serious difficulty; and Mr. Wade
has had no hesitation in dealing drastically with
“the liberty of the subject” to attain his objects.
On the appearance of things, the Bill —allowing
that it goes through without serious amendment —
will attain those objects, but it will still have to
face the ordeal of law-court tests raising such
questions as “Wheyi is a place not a place?”


The Gambling Bill starts oil with a series
of sweeping definitions the most important of
which is that “street” includes any highway,
thoroughfare or passage on any public or private
land. All New South Wales is divided into three
sections—(l) streets (as defined above), on which
betting is absolutely prohibited; (2) sports
grounds or racecourses, on which betting is
allowed ; (3) houses or lands in, or on, which
betting is absolutely prohibited. And, seemingly,
what isn’t a house (vvliich includes grounds at-
tached thereto), or a piece of land, is a “grounds”
or a “street,” and there is no area which isn’t a
“place” within the meaning of the Act. But
this point is not made as plain as it might have
been (by following, for instance, a recent Bulle-
tin suggestion). To use a house in contravention
of the Act means heavy penalties to occupier or
owner. Any house reasonably suspected of being
a gaming-house, or any house or land reasonably
suspected of being used as a means of entrance to
or exit from a gaming-house, may be “proclaimed,”
and, in that event, not only owner and occupier,
but any visitor to the premises, is liable to punish-
ment. In effect, suspected gaming-houses may
be declared under quarantine, and visiting them
may be made an offence. A house unjustly sus-
pected and “ proclaimed may go to the
Supreme Court and clear its character. But the
onus of proof is on the suspected house. The
gaming-house which makes elaborate precautions
with locks, bars, etc., to keep out the police,
thereby sows the seed for a fresh crop of penalties.
There isn’t any clause by which an institution
such as Tattersall’s Club can continue to “call the
card ” in peace. Apparently, to “ call the card ”
under this Bill would be to court bankruptcy and
gaol. “ The interference with liberty ” and the
“ violation of the principle that innocence is to be
presumed until guilt is proved, or easily prove-
able,” will doubtless be urged in objection to
Wade’s drastic proposals. But, if they represent
the minimum price the community has to pay
for the suppression of the betting-shop and
street bookmaker, the price will, no doubt, be
cheerfully paid. For the betting evil is monstrous.
It not only attacks the purses of mental weak-
lings, but saps their enterprise and industry. The
man who hopes to make a “ rise ” in the world by
gambling, never puts his shoulder to the task of
making a “rise” by hard work and intelligent
enterprise ; his hustle is numbed by the gambling
drug. And Australia—which offers, on the whole,
greater facilities than almost any other country
in the world for a man to “rise,” and which,
with proper national legislation, will offer him
ideal facilities —has less excuse for the gambler,
and more incentive to the hustler, than most
lands. Also, it has great need of the hustler.
Patriotic citizens will welcome, therefore, the
principle of a Bill designed to prevent, as far as
possible, the flaunting of baits to the foolish to
draw them into the vortex of gambling. That
some sort of gambling will always exist must be
admitted. What the law should aim at is the
suppression of agencies which seek to enlist from
among the improvident new victims for the evil.

Population—How to Lose it and How to
Get it.
An essay by “ G.H.” :
Population is pedestrian cash. The country whose
population steadily increases, and whose standards of
living and education continue on the up-grade has little
to worry about. It is the other kind of country that
requires to tie an ice-factory around its head, and think
numerously. When it finds'that its population is rushing
down a wholesale precipice, and that the standard of
living amongst the people who have not yet rushed is
pretty nearly the same as it was fifty years ago, it needs
to find out things. First, why its population is depart-
ing ; second, where it is going to. In the institutions,
laws, and general customs of that Where, it will most
likely find a highly effective antidote for its own distress-
ing Why. And just here seems to be a good place to px
pound Ireland’s sample Why. The population of Ireland
has been steadily evaporating, mostly to the Protectionist
United States, for the last 65 years. The following table
shows how incessant that evaporation has been
Population Loss in
of Ireland. 10 Years.
1841 .. 8,175,124
1851 .. 6,552,385 1,622,739
1861 .. 5,798,967 753,418
1871 .. 5,412,377 386,590
1881 .. 5,174,836 237,541
1891 .. 4,704,750 470,086
1901 .. 4,458,775 245,975
Total loss, 1841-01 3,716,349
And, according to authoritative figures recently pub-
lished in the Cold Country, Ireland’s population has
further declined since 1901 to 4,390,208.
The wholesale evaporation of humanity began in the
identical decade in which Cobden’s Freetrade joss
arrived. Ireland entered the nineteenth century with
a population of 5,000,000 men. In the 41 years sub-
sequent to 1800, over 3,000,000 were added. And
this addition was effected in the face of the most
iniquitous system of absentee landlordism on earth. In
the middle forties, however, Ireland ran up against a bad
famine. With only a fraction of the land under cultiva-
tion, Ireland had thusfar been barely able to feed its
expanding population. But the famine of the forties
brought Ireland to a dead stop, and at the same time
provided the Manchester Calico Jimmies with the lever
which finally upset the Corn Laws. The Manchester
manufacturers, hungry for foreign markets, had been
crying out for Freetrade in corn since 1838. If Eng-
land would subsist on foreign food, they argued, foreign
countries would accept English cotton goods in exchange.
Thanks to heavy Protection, the cotton towns of Lanca-
shire had sprung up practically in a day. The industry
upon which they flourished, according to Australian
Freetrade theories, was a purely artificial one ; for Eng-
land did not, and could not, grow an ounce of cotton.
Nevertheless Protection had created the cotton industry,
and had made it financially powerful. Previous to the out-
break of the famine in Ireland, these manufacturers had
despaired of opening the English market for foreign corn.
When the agony arrived across the Irish Channel, they saw
their opportunity. “ If distress should continue to favor
us," Cobden gleefully wrote, “ we shall get something sub-
stantial in another twelve months." Distress did con-
tinue to favor the Calico Jimmies. The “ favor ” was of
such a wholesale nature that half a million Irish peasants
died within 18 months. The Manchester men danced
around their graves in anticipatory triumph. In Ireland’s
misfortune they saw no object-lesson in land monopoly.
The Potato Panic impressed them as an excellent expe-
dient whereby they might destroy the agricultural inde-
pendence of the United Kingdom. How they succeeded
is a matter of history. Freetrade was established in
England and Ireland by a method unparalleled in the
records of modern nations. The people gave no direct
electoral mandate for Freetrade. As a matter of
fact, out of a total population of 27,000,000, only about
three-quarters of a million had votes. But the fight for
Freetrade was not won on the hustings. The Parlia-
ment that repealed the Corn Laws was hostile to Cobden’s
propositions ; and the Prime Minister, who brought in
the Bill, had affirmed the principle that the nation’s
existence was embodied in the independence of its food-
supply. Yet, thanks to the condition of Ireland, the
Repeal was effected.
Freetrade, according to the vociferous Cobden, was to
make Ireland a land flowing with wakes and whisky and
other emblems of joy. Ireland was to grow fat upon im-
ported food, and was to pay for it with coin which was to
come from some unspecified source. Ireland refused to
get fat, however, in spite of the abolition of the corn
duties. It was base ingratitude on the part of Ireland.
It knew instinctively that the idol had clay hoofs, and
that its smile was a clock-work affair. The middle of last
century marked a great and melancholy turning-point in
Irish history. The Distressful Country appeared to
pass a silent resolution to the effect that Ire-
land under English rule and English Freetrade was
synonymous with Hell, and that the best thing was to
seek a home elsewhere. Not often have nations taken
such a step. To remove a nation—or the able-bodied
portion of it—from the soil to which it has emng for cen-
turies, is no small thing. It is tragedy great. “'''ter,
and all-significant. But the Irish exodus began. It has
gone on. It has never ceased. It seems that it will
never cease—until Ireland gets a Protective tariff and a
really national land-system. -. , , ,
The industrial difference between Ireland and hnglana
in Cobden’s day was simply this. England began its
Freetrade career as the greatest manufacturing State on
earth—the State that had almost a monopoly of the
world’s manufactures. It had reached that condition
through centuries of Protection, and though under rree-
trade it has dropped back to second or third place, it
wiii take a great deal of Freetrade to destroy these
gigantic industries —the purely artificial cotton in-
dustry included. But Ireland, its manufactures carefully
destroyed by English legislation, began its Freetrade
career as a country which had to build its industries
afresh, and it met the usual fate. No country ever built
up great manufactures under Freetrade, and there is no
reason to believe that any one ever will.
It is pitiful to think of Ireland’s vanished manufactures.
Take its woollen industry as an example. In 1641 265
years a^o —it employed 30,000 persons. And how many
in 1881 ? Only a sorrowful 7000. And, in the same year,
John Bright asked, in the House of Commons, “ Why is
it that during the last hundred years there has not been
a single manufactory of any importance established and
sustained in Ireland?” Coming from Cobden s chief
assistant fetish-monger, that question suggested Cain
asking Abel how he came to be dead.
The Sacred Victorian Cow.
Somebody wrote to Harrison Ord, the chiet
inspector of Victorian factories, the other day,
asking him to have a look at some of the butter-
factories, and particularly at their machinery.
Ord’s reply was that the Factories Act ex-
pressly exempted butter-factories from inspection,
and that no supervision could be exercised over
them. Yet in these factories there are steam
boilers, of all ages and sizes, and in all states of
disrepair ; and they are largely in the care of men
who are uncertificated. All othifer engine-drivers
must have certificates. The Victorian law won’t
allow a man without a certificate to take his
engine into one of Tait’s roadside railway stations
for loading. But in a building filled with expen-
sive machinery, and which has often a lot of men
in it as well, the big steam boiler may be, and
often is, in charge of a man who doesn’t know
steam from electricity. Some day there will be
the father of an explosion, and if it happens when
there is a directors’ meeting at the factory,
and pieces of director go ‘flying through
a couple of shires, there is a possibility
that something will be done in the matter.
The same stupid principle applies to the
wages of the factory employees. In all other
factories the wages ai’e, or may be, regulated by a
Board ; but the Factories Act expressly excludes
butter-factory employees, and farm hands gener-
ally, from the purview of the statute. Allof this
is done owing to the quaint notion, which the
farmer carefully fosters, that dairying and farming
would go to rack and ruin if fair wages had to be
paid and decent regulations conformed to. No
one ever brought forward any proof of this weird
allegation; the “farmers’representatives” in the
House just scrambled to their feet and made the
statement in that bluff, satisfied way that is
characteristic of them, and the House was so im-
pressed that it accepted the lie without even
casually inspecting it. Yet to say that the butter-
factory cannot afford to employ a certificated
engine-driver is a libel in the 10th degree on a
flourishing industry. And there is even less
excuse for the villainously low wages >which
the great bulk of the factories pay their em-
ployees. The dairy farmers, and the landowners
who rent them the land, are simply coining
money, and their objection to decent wages for
the men who do most of the hard work—and who
work seven days a week—is a shameful scandal.
The industry can well afford good wages, and it
wouldn’t even be necessary to reduce rents to pay
them. But so sacred is the Cow that most legisla-
tors grow weak in the legs and begin to perspire
as soon as it is mentioned.
A Straight Line.
A railway journey from Junee to Hay (N.S.W.)
is an object lesson. This line is 168 miles long,
and the annual loss is about £50,000. It doesn’t
really pay for axle-grease, through lack of popu-
lation to use it. After the train has passed the
outskirts of Narandera it enters on a vast sheep-
walk. Narandera is in the county of Cooper, a
large county of 2752 square miles, and traversed
by two expensive railway lines—the other
one doesn’t pay for axle-grease either. The
population of the county—outside the town ot
Narandera, which lives on being a railway June-
tion—is about 1000, and is either stationary or
diminishing. As the train hustles gradually
along you enter the great sheepwalk county of
Sturt. It has 738 people on 1512 square miles—
four people to ten square miles. The last census
showed that the population is diminishing, lothe
south is the sheepwalk county of Boyd; the last cen-
sus showed that its population is diminishing also.
Ahead is the great sheepwalk county of VVarad-
gery ; the last census showed that its population
is diminishing too. As the train plods along, the
distant river timber can be seen slowly receding
from view, and the intervening space, containing
some of the finest land in N.S.W., is utter chaos
-—Save for a sheep or a crow. You seldom see a
cow. They don’t take to cow on these big sheep
stations. Both sides of the line, as far as
you can see, .are freehold “ station property.
Ten or fifteen miles out to the north is a
little nest of farmers, isolated as it were. You
wonder why it is they did not settle down closer
to the line, where the land is better. But it would
never do to have farmers so adjacent to the rail-
way; They would disturb the sheep. It would
never do to have them near the river either—they
might fall in and get drowned. Doubtless it was
to avoid all such trouble that the river and the
railway line, and their immediate surroundings,
were sold to the squatter (if the line wasn’t sold
to him it should have been given to him), bo
you stand a bigger chance of getting into
Sheol than getting a field of river frontage for
agricultural or any other pursuit—anywhere along
the ’Bidgee. Whitton is a tired-looking little
township, cursed with too much sheepwalk, by
which it is surrounded. It would never do to
have the “ backbone of the country” rooting up the
land within a mile or two of the township and
the railway that doesn’t pay. The big land-
holders between Narandera and Hay saw to that
years ago. Probably they thought that the advent
of small landholders within such easy distance of
the line, and on the very best of the land too, would
lead to Socialism, or something. The fact that
this line runs through the very best soil in
Riverina cannot be disputed, but the land is kept
for a sheep-walk. Three acres of this land ought
to almost keep a human being, but through the
bad management of the big squatter they just
about keep a jumbuck. Wanted, the Federal
graduated land-tax.

What is an Australian?
Louis Esson spreads himself on the subject men-
tioned above: —
Is the Australian—the ethnological specimen (white)
born or branded in this country—a fellow Irishman, a
brither Scot, an Oxford Englishman, a fat German, a
frivolous Frenchman, a spaghetti chewer, a Finn, or
what ? Is the Australian a secessionist, a Socialist, a gum-
sucker, a leaner, a man on the land, a tote twirler, an
importer, or an auctioneer ? Is he a Ohow ? Is he a fellow
Hindu, or Jap, or Javanese, or brother Assyrian ? Is he
anything in particular, a cosmopolitan, or a man who has
just shifted his native land with him?
It seems easy. I include anybody as Australian who
has faith in Australia; desires to live violently and en-
thusiastically in it (if possible) ; has solid Australian sen-
timent ; is interested in things Australian—history, ex-
ploration, cities, the bush, politics, arts, personalities,
public debt —everything ; who buys local goods (when
they’re there); who doesn’t think gum trees “ hideous”
and our landscape “all the same”; who believes the
people here can run their country better than outsiders
can tell them ; who lives, works, thinks and dreams Aus-
tralian, and is quite cheerful about it.
Examples: Marie Narellf., native born, is labelled
“ Queeu of Irish Song.” I pass. How can an Australian
be “ queen of Irish Song ” ? Irish song is fine in its way.
So is nearly every country’s national song ; so might
Australian national song be, if we had any. But Irish
song is not our goods. I wouldn’t claim Marie Narelle
as an Australian artist any morfe than I would Melba,
who is “ queen of Italian opera,” or Haddon Chambers,
who is a London playmonger, or Mackennal, who sculps
alien subjects, or H. B. Marriott-Watson, who writes for
the Daily M ail.
The Australian comes from cosmopolitan stock. He
may be Scotch, Russian. Swedish, Danish, anything;
he had to come from somewhere. Yet he’s Australian
when he grafts in and for this country. Usually he’s
only partially Australian. When he joins a Caledonian
or Hibernian Society, a Swedish Club, a Deutsche Turn-
Verein or Brixton Empire League he’s no longer Aus-
tralian—that section of him, anyhow. When Pat or
Mike waves his shillalah and calls for Home Rule for
Oireland (and tosses thrums and sentiment in the Irish
envoys’ hats), I wouldn’t have him on my mind as an
Australian. He ought to walk about. He’s Irish, or
pretends to be. He’s moved deeper by something in the
Old Land that he deserted (not willing to make material
sacrifices for his patriotism) than by anything in the
New Land that accepted him. Australia doesn’t move
him yet. It takes an earthquake to move some people.
And it’s the same all round. You can’t, or shouldn’t,
class people as Australians who write furrin books, paint
furrin pictures, warble furrin songs—whether emigrated
to furrin parts or lingering wearily on in this “ blasted
hole.” They’re half-and-halfers at best or worse.
If a man iiving here isn’t moved more by things hap-
pening here than by those in other lands (including the
dim, legend-haunted place his parents may have come
from) he’s no use to me. He usually wrecks his children.
Have conducted some experiments on small boys. When
asked what they are, “ English,” say some, “Scotch,”
“ Irish,” “German,” others; rarely “Victorians,” still more
rarely‘’Australian.” One replied “English.” lobjected.
So did his dad. “ Noo, what are ye, Jock? ” “ Scawtch,”
cried Jock at the second go. “It’s the fault o’ the
schules,” commented the now’ proud parent. One boy
said “Geelong.” Federation sinks slowly into the hearts
of the people.
If a man or woman doesn’t find this country the most
interesting on the map (that is, for him or her) and doesn’t
rejoice and revel in being here, he or she had better
depart for London or Japan or Spain (so romantic ; they
play guitars there !) They’re no good here. They’re big
blots—public nuisances.
Australia doesn’t need to grasp at half-distinguished
personalities—actors, poets, singers, painters. They’ll
want us some day more than we want them. We’ll come
at the finish. What Turgenieff said about Russia applies
pat to Australia. “ Russia can do without everyone of
us, but woe to him if he forgets Russia.” Queens of Irish
song and Italian opera, Teutonic bassoonists, Macaroni
dancers, Pinerotic mummers, painters of the latest
Parisian nudes. Continental Socialists, Home Rulers —
whether living here or not, and how’ever famous or
even useful as importers of certain goods we lack—after
all, they don’t count. They don’t take us anyw’here.
They haven’t the root of the matter in them. We’ll have
our own say some day, and say it in our own way. Till
then we can wait and spread things.
“Smooging” in the Public Service.
N.S.W. Opposition leader M'Gowen, on the
Railway Commissioners Bill, made one remark
which deserved to be remembered :
If there was one man in the service who had a
happy knack of not making friends, it was Mr. Oliver.
And undoubtedly that fact has materially helped
on the proposal for Oliver’s unjust dismissal by
a contract-breaking Act of Parliament. The man
seems to have had no gift of smooging. He
didn’t know how to be properly affable to mem-
bers of Parliament and Ministers. But he did
know how to stand up for the public interest,
and was prepared to drop all affability and to
snarl when contracts tending to rob the public
purse were proposed. It is easily understandable
that a man of Oliver’s stamp should get on
Premier Carruthers’ nerves, for Premier Car-
ruthers is eminently of the non-quarrelsome,
peace-at-any-price type. He would apologise to
anybody, be civil to his vilest detractor, and put
up with any outrage on the public for the sake of
peace and quietness. But it is not easy to under-
stand how a majority of a Parliament —even the
N.S.W. Parliament—could even seriously con-
sider an Act to dismiss a public servant of this
type, whose administration shows success by its
results, and who is accused only of a want of
affability and a lack of the power to cringe. For
it is just men who lack affability and the capacity
to smooge that the Public Service wants. The
curse of all Government departments is the man
who devotes his best energies to cultivating the
good graces of his superiors, and can only spare a
very tired effort for the real work he is paid to
perform.
The Juvenile Smoking Prohibition Bill, which
will presumably be permitted to pass the Vic.
Legislative Council, promises to come hard on
the jockey mannikins who arrive at years of dis-
cretion without losing the appearance of boyhood.
If the provisions of the Bill are properly enforced
it will be impossible for the average adult light-
weight jockey to buy a “ smoke ” at a shop where
he isn’t known unless he can produce proof °f h'S
legal right to blow a cloud. Otherwise, the Bui
is nor open to even a humorous objection. It
ought to kill the lolly-shop cigarette trade in one
act. Also, it will tend to reduce the consumption
of tobacco by adults, who won’t be allowed to
begin smoking till they have sense enough not to
begin at all. The Bill might very well be amended
to prevent the sale of tobacco to all weak, under-
sized youths who have still a possibility of growth
in them ; in fact, there might be a height limit
for smokers as well as an age limit. As far
as the law can protect the physique of the
growing generation, the protection ought to be
enforced. If the sale of strong narcotics to
strong men is restricted by a Poisons Act, why
shouldn’t young weaklings be prohibited from
purchasing the narcotic that is mild?

Just now, Melbourne is suffering from a wave of
moral hysteria, and, in a small way, the methods
of Mafficking night are being applied to the
regeneration of mankind. Melbourne has got the
Judkins disease in a bad form, and a bitter out-
break of snuffle spreads like a great, bleak, holy
gamp over the land. Within the last' few weeks
there have been put before the Victorian public,
amid the loud cheers of crazy audiences, pro-
posals—
(l) To abolish alcoholic drink, and uproot every vine-
yard in the State.
(2) To make gambling in every form a criminal offence,
and to empower the police to break into any house in
which it is reasonably suspected that a game of penny
nap is in progress.
(3) To suppress boxing.
(4) To make smoking, even on the part of adults, a
crime.
(5) To raise the age of consent from 16 to 21.
(6) To prohibit dancing by men and women, whether on
the stage, in a public ball, or in a private house.
(7) To forbid tights or short dresses, or low-neck dresses
on the stage.
(8) To prohibit mixed bathing, under pain of arrest and
imprisonment.
(9) To prohibit girls’ swimming matches to which men
are admitted.
(10) To abolish the life classes at the National Gallery,
and the use of models (male or female) by artists, and to
include nude pictures and statues in the clauses relating
to indecency.
(11) To abolish high-jumping at agricultural shows as
unscriptural.
There is very little left now, but probably before
this is published, Judkins, or some of his crowd,
will have advocated a Bill to suppress football,
cricket, lawn-tennis, and mixed picnics. So far, no
one has said anything against breathing, yet when
a girl in a thin summer blouse breathes hard, after
running, there is a heaving of her bust which
should not be allowed in mixed company.
