George Reid rose last week to say that “it had
been alleged he was looking forward to having
pauper labor in Australia,” and that the state-
ment “ was one of the meanest and foulest slanders
ever uttered against a public man.” Well, here
are Reid’s own reported words of a few years
ago:—
We all HOPE that some day Australia will have a great
woollen industry, but that can only come when we are not
so well off. In the plenitude of time, when our millions
become tens of millions, we shall have a crop of misery
which will solve the difficulty in regard to cheap manu-
factures.
These were reported in the daily papers as Reid’s
own words on the subject. They set forth that he,
as a Freetrader, saw no way of getting a great
woollen industry except by raising “a crop of
misery ” —human misery—and at the same time he
hoped for the woollen industry, which reads much
the same as if he said straight out that he hoped
for the misery. A man with such an unhappy
way of expressing himself need not plunge about
so furiously when he is accused of looking forward
to having pauper labor in Australia. By his own
words that is just what he is—or was—looking
forward to. In fact, he seemed to be doing more
than looking forward to it; he seemed to be cry
ing out for it.

One of Vic. Premier Bent’s strongest points is his
attitude on the railway and land questions.
Thomas now declines to even think of a railway
unless the landowners, whose property will be
improved by the line, pay something towards its
cost. A case in point, showing the soundness of
Bent’s theory, is the proposed 60 miles of line
from Wickliffe-road to Lintons, across a por-
tion of the West Victorian plains. For more than
two-thirds of the distance the line, as surveyed,
will run through large properties. For 12 miles
it traverses McPherson’s Nerrin Nerrin estate (a
property of about 60,000 acres), and it runs for
nearly the same distance through the adjoining
estate, equally large, of Ceramballac, which
belongs to a Chirnside. The construction of the
line would add about £2 per acre to the capital
value of the two estates mentioned. That is, it
would be equivalent to presenting Chirnside and
McPherson with £120,000 each. And Bent
doesn’t see why Chirnside and McPherson
shouldn’t be satisfied with a smaller present than
that, so he demands a contribution from the big
landlords. Until they come to terms, the line
will not be further considered, there being, un-
fortunately, no Betterment Tax system, under
which Chirnside and McPherson would have to
pay whether they liked it or not.

The Need for Men.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The world needs Men —the world needs earnest
fighters,
Strong men endowed with granite wills ;
The world needs scribes —needs grim, defiant
writers
Whose ink like boiling lava spills.
Too long we bow to charlatans and pandars—
Too long we commerce with buffoons ;
Caesars we need, and fiery Alexanders —
A band of resolute dragoons
To charge the ranks of sophistry and error,
To lay all lies and liars low ;
A cohort that shall strike eternal terror
In each disciple of Yes-No.
The world needs Men—the world needs mighty
Preachers
To draw mankind to surer goals;
The world needs Prophets—needs new Seers and
Teachers.
Men’s bodies are incarnate souls.
Grey catalogues of saints avail them little
Why proffer hands they cannot clutch ?
The faiths of yore are clammy, cold and brittle
They crumble ’neath the eager touch.
Give us a Faith —a faith in this Existence —
Give us a Heaven here below ;
We weary of the mirage in the distance.
We sicken of the vain Yes-No !
The world needs Men—not Mountebanks and
Jesters —
Not Pimps and pliant-conscienced Knaves ;
Wrong rankles still—malignantly it festers,
And all the earth is full of graves;
The world needs Men—Oh, don’t you hear it
asking ?
The world needs you—it needs you Now.
The world needs Men to set them to their tasking,
Behind the potent era-plough ;
The world needs us—it calls us to our labor—
The world needs US, and we must go ;
And we must work—must draw the mighty sabre
Against the Yes that is mostly No.

On land nationalisation he considers it would cost

ment of the Commonwealth.
In winding up, he said he desired to sink the fiscal
issue pending the death of Socialism, and that Protec-
tion, bad as it was, didn’t matter.
Finally, he seemed to imply once more that Socialism
didn’t matter much either.
4.
The Penny Post.
There is an appealing magnificence about the idea
of instituting an Australian penny post which
commends it to the unpractical mind. Little
wonder that it has won the approval of the
Deakin Government, which has for its leader a
man of poetic imagination, for its Treasurer a fine,
hearty spendthrift, and in its ranks no cautious
financial authority. Austin Chapman came back
from Europe filled with the idea of signalising his
term of office as Postmaster-General by bringing
Australia within the scope of an Imperial penny
post. John Eorrest, who holds the money-bags,
carries generosity to excess, even in spending his
own private revenue, and Deakin, the final autho-
rity, is not the sort of leader to closely scrutinise
any proposal about which a colleague is enthusi-
astic. So a penny postage proposal is brought on
with a rush, and, The Bulletin believes, without
a single thought as to ways and means. To
have a penny post between Australia and
England would cost very little; but a neces-
sary accompaniment of that reduction is
penny postage throughout Australia, and the
revenue loss from that would be enormous. The
postal authorities estimate it at £300,000. The
Cabinet, in the flush of its enthusiasm, cuts down
that estimate by 25 per cent. But the Postal De-
partment, which can take a calm view of the
matter, is much more likely to be correct in its
calculations as to the cost. It is also opti-
mistically forecasted by the Cabinet that the
deficit, would “soon” be made up by increase
of correspondence. But no reasonable anticipation
would construe “soon’’into less than 10 years;
probably it would really mean 15 years. And
that is a long, long “soon.” Meanwhile, there
are far more pressing matters on which the Com-
monwealth can spend £300,000. There is the
acquisition, for instance, of the Northern Terri-
tory, its colonisation, and its linking to the great
cities by a railway. There is the building of the
Westralian railway. There is the provision of
naval defence. There is the re-organisation
of the citizen militia, and the establishment
of, at least, a small-arms factory. There
is the matter of old age pensions. All
these are essentials. “Penny postage with-
in the Empire” is a sentimental luxury. If the
Commonwealth revenues were superabundant
there would be nothing to be said against the
cheapening of postage. But that is far from
being the case, and penny postage inevitably means
the postponement of some much more essential
matter which would make a call on the revenue.
Under the circumstances it is the duty of Parlia-
ment to put a check on the generosity of the
Government. Time enough when Australia is
secure to think ot cheapening postage. More im-
portant now to make it a dear matter to send an
invading army here from a foreign country than
to make it a cheap matter to send away an order
for imported goods.

The councillors of Upper Yarra have just given
amazing evidence of what the Victorians who
support the ’Pootra brand of politics are capable
of. Long ago they borrowed up to the legal limit.
Then they raised an overdraft, till the bank grew
weary and stopped further advances. Since then
they have given cheques, though at the time there
were no funds to pay them, and nobody has charged
them with issuing valueless paper. Their secretary
and engineer grew weary of the calls and insults of
the council’s creditors, and resigned his billet, first,
however, telling his shiftless bosses that their
present rate of Is. 3d.—levied on valuations that
were so low as to be quite bogus—was insufficient
to meet obligations, and that nothing lower than
2s. would do it. This council lately cadged £l5O
from Bent, on condition that it also raised £75
itself; yet, before it had raised a solitary £l, it
calmly let contracts up to £225. After the secre-
tary had wiped the mud off his feet and slammed
the door, the council considered the situation.
It was determined not to pay a penny
more of rates; but a genius pointed out
that, if it raised the rate to Is. Gd. and simul-
taneously lowered the valuations, it and the other
property-owners would actually pay no more in
the aggregate, but would get at the State for an
extra £2O or £3O of subsidy, a higher subsidy being
paid to a shire that rates itself at Is. 6d. than to
one that rates itself at Is. 3d. And this unblush-
ing scheme to diddle the people of Victoria by
faking the valuations was agreed to, not one coun-
nillor rising to describe it as plain swindle or any-
thing like that.
This is the S.M. Heb.yld’s latest attitude—if it
can be called an attitude — on the N.S.W. railway
trouble (extract from its leading article of
August 1) :—
It would be as idle to praise the Commissioners for this
year’s surplus as it would have been unkind to lay the
previous year’s deficit to their charge. They simply sat
down and watched the surplus grow. The favorable
seasons did the good work, and the Commissioners merely
sat at the receipt of custom.
Then followed this other yarn from the same
paper’s leading article of August 3 :
Curiously enough, if Mr. M‘Go wen’s ideas were followed
out there would be no Railway Commissioners at all. He
insisted last night that the latter had done nothing to
bring in the great revenue recorded by the Department.
The system and the seasons had done everything. . . .
Mr. Eddy’s work, on this showing, counts for nought.
. . . It is useless to argue with such prejudice.
Two points here stick out prominently. One is
that the S.M. Herald is so anxious to call any
Labor member a fool that it calls him one even
when he agrees with it, and this unseemly haste
makes the ancient Hunter-street publication look
ridiculous. On August 1 the Herald said the
Railway Commissioners deserved no credit for the
surplus that has been produced this year. On
August 2 Labor-leader McGowen said the same
thing, and on August 3 the Herald said that “it
was useless to argue with such prejudice.” The
second point is that the Herald keeps one atti-
tude for the Australian-born official and another
for the imported one, hence itsassurance(Augustl)
that Oliver, the Australian, should not get credit
for the good results shown to-day, and its fury
(August 3) lest the same theory should be applied
to Eddy, imported.
Coroner Cole (who is an M.D. also) has decided
that Donald M‘Lkod, the balancer whom parsons
and press alleged, in a loud, horror-stricken voice,
to have been kicked to death atFlemington (Vic.),
died from accident. The medical evidence, and the
other evidence given at the inquest, showed that
nobody kicked the man at all. He was struck a
few times, but nothing to speak of, except one
blow that broke his nose. His neck had been dis-
located either through his turning his terror-
stricken head too suddenly to watch his pur-
suers, or through falling with his head doubled
under him. Even then the dislocation was so
slight that it could probably have been reduced
and the man’s life saved. But those who assisted
him bumped him on to a stretcher and let his head
fall back in their haste to render first aid, and so
caused a laceration of the spinal cord that finished
him off. M'Leod went out to Elemington with
a red coat, some tickets, a bag, a false name and
no money, and died through running away from
his creditors, the case being aggravated by mis-
guided sympathy. If only Bent hadn’t been in
such a hurry to give Worrall that advertise-
ment !

The recent anti-Socialist N.S.W. Farmers’ and
Settlers’ Conference, having rejected by a big
majority the Progressive Land-Tax as being “rank
Socialism,” spent its seventh sitting enthusiasti-
cally agreeing to these few items :
(1) The State should publish a list of brands in use over
10 years.
(2) The State should handle and ship wheat in bulk.
(3) The State should post daily information as to the
market price of wheat at railway stations and post offices.
(4) The State should conserve water.
(5) The State should grant to single families in the
country the same educational concessions now given to
two or more families.
(6) The State should provide facilities for higher edu-
cation in country districts.
(7) The State should erect schools where desired by
parents along railway lines, irrespective of whether other
schools can he reached by train or not.
(8) The State should provide horse-paddocks, feed, and
sheds for the use of children who ride to country schools.
In fact, the State should do every kind of Social-
ism that costs money ; but the kind of Socialism
that would bring in a little money directly in the
shape of land-tax, and a heap of it indirectly by
cutting up big estates and making 100 agricul-
tural taxpayers grow where now there is only one,
is abhorrent to the grasping and brazen cadgers of
the Farmers’ and Settlers’ Association.
Thomas Bent now talks of buying out the Chows
who have monopolised the furniture trade in
Melbourne. It is the best solution of the difficulty,
he says. How? Does Tommy, as an advocate of
“ Safe Socialism,” think of taking over the trade
as a State industry ? If not, how would he pass
the purchased businesses on to the white workers ?
And what will he do with the Chow who is bought
out? Will he turn him loose to monopolise some
other business ? If Thomas buys out the cabinet-
making John, a steamship ticket back to Chow-
land will have to be part of the contract. This
Chinese worry in Melbourne shows that Customs
duties are only a portion of the big principle of
Protection to Australian industries and indus-
trious Australians. Protection set up a furniture-
making industry in Melbourne, but it did not
preserve it for the Australian workman. Mel-
bourne would have been little worse off if it had
been situated in Shanghai, and now it is neces-
sary to buy the foreigner out of the business Pro-
tection created. This is no argument against
Protection, but it is a convincing condemnation
of half-pint measures drawn up by timid half-pint
legislators. We want quart legislators.
If freedom of speech be essential to
progress, good sense and good taste are essential
to freedom of speech. An encouragement to the
exercise of these things lurks in every drop of
D.C.L. whisky. And if the drop be duly regulated
in size and frequency cheerfulness in good doing
flourishes like smiles in a genial human climate.
But is it of any avail to talk whisky wisdom to
rabid teetotallers ?*

Apart from the fact that Burleigh wore
knee-breeches and the Virgin Queen an outstand-
ing collar like a brewery-horse’s, the lazy dramatist
finds some solid advantages in pitching his story
in the days of Good Queen Bess. In these later
slow-moving times the young man can’t be rushed
into matrimony until he has had his Sunday tea
at her mother’s for two years; and there is a
three-volume novel at the very least in the
couple’s picnickings and philanderings before the
arrival of the final five-shilling rise in salary that
can make them one. For dramatic treatment,
when the last train leaves at 10 minutes past 11,
there are serious disadvantages in this style of
courtship—more especially as the young man
nearly always does it in plain tweed clothes
with ink-spots on them, or, in the most
magnificent cases, in the same sort of gar-
ment that the waiter wears. They probably
did much the same sort of thing in the days of
Good Queen Bess; but it is quite easy to make a
theatre-full believe that they didn’t—that it was
then the thing for the young man to drop into
a garden in trappings of silk and velvet, with
a sword by his side, and win and woo Rosalind or
Dorothy before the shadow of the stately castle
tower stole one ell across the moated grange—or
whatever the old thing was that happened to be
there. And if another Sir Silk-and-Velvet hap-
pened to drop in—as it seems he had a habit of
doing—why, then, by our troth, there was room
and occasion for some pretty sword play, inter-
rupted by the arrival of an angry parent (with a
large stomach, and also in velvet—in brown vel-
vet) ; and pointed references to dungeons and the
block followed. So we have something like a
revival of “Elizabethan drama.” We also have
“Deadwood Dick” literature; and in a cold,
deadly analysis there is not much elemental
difference between them. They belong to the
same school —one for boys, the other for girls.
# * » *
It is most fit .and proper that Miss Tittell
Brune should lead the latest outbreak of this
class of “ Elizabethan drama ” at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney. For Miss Brune is the pre-eminent
Girls’ actress. On Saturday she opened her new
Sydney season ; and before 4 o’clock there was a
petticoated crowd around the gallery doors,
waiting to scramble for the pick of the seats
nearly four hours later on. Shop girls mostly,
they seemed, spending their Saturday afternoon
off in this patient adoration of the little Ameri-
can ; and at 8 o’clock the tightly-packed house
received her with almost frantic enthusiasm.
Three-fourths of the circle was Girl, in one stage
or another; there was Girl in the boxes; it was
Girl who capped the crowd, leaning over the
topmost guard-rail, high up in the gallery.
It seems hopeless to ask Why? Have the
paragraphists done it, just as the other week
they sent tens of thousands to stare at the “Light
of the World”? Or has she done it for herself?
Anyway, there is the adoration, and it is the real
thing. Act after act, call after call; and Miss
Brune comes on panting as though she had run
two miles and got there. It matters little whether
she has done well or ill. Her “ Dorothy Vernon,”
now played here for the first time, is much below
the standard of her own earlier work. But the
unsatisfied critic still has to confess that the house
is utterly against him. Dorothy \ ernon is a
spoiled child; and spoiled children run in all
shapes. But Miss Brune’s spoiled child leaves no
fixed impression ; she is not one spoiled child,
but several, and of different ages. She
can make love, though—or be made love to!
When her head is on his shoulder (Thomas
Kingston has arrived to supply the shoulder),
nobody could be more “ clingsome,” nobody could
say more prettily that she will be his —all his.
Thomas Kingston, still spare of limb as in the
old days, retains his good voice and graceful,
spirited ways; on- this occasion he is the young
man who drops into the garden, pretends he is
somebody else, and wins Dorothy in four minutes
by the post-office clock ; but he has to pass through
three more acts, filled with deadly peril and
elaborate stage-carpentry, before he can carry her
off. Queen Elizabeth arrives late in the evening
and disposes of things in her red-headed, regal
way ; and, though a good deal of what she has to
do is almost foreign to the piece, Miss Vaughan’s
characterisation is one of the distinguished fea-
tures of the performance.

“ Jo,” who was always being chivvied and
chased about, just like our own “ Jo ” Carruthers,
but without deserving it as he does, comes out of

give unhappiness to all.

New names are liable to happen on Sydney
Tivoli bill at any moment now ; for these are the
last nights of Brinn, who juggles with cannons on
his face, and Miss Lilieth Leyton, who has helped
to bring back the old farce to music-hall favor.
Meantime there have been numerous resurrec-
tions, of whom Kelly and Agnes are the most
notable. But Little Cliff remains the bright star.
Mostly the stage youngster is a parrot, more or
less well-trained. But this one struck the Tivoli
first as a youngster whose work wasn’t mechani-
cal ; and now it is opening its eyes wide at his
versatility.
* # * *
“In London Town,” staged at Sydney
Royal, on Saturday, shows Miss Francesßoss with
golden hair a-down her back ; and Walter Baker,
instead of making love to her with exuberant
boyishness, leaves that pleasant task to Max Max-
well, and acts her prodigal father, who has come
home after finding a gold reef where the busks
were thrown out, and is searching for his child with
a million in each pocket. Otherwise, it is about the
same Bland Holt melodrama. As usual, Walter
Baker has, in his youth, fallen a victim to the wiles
of villain Styan, has “donetime,” hascomeoutof
the place where time is done to find his wife stolen
by the villain, and goes away to America, after
leaving his little daughter in the care of the father
of the extra-villainous villain Norman. When
the play opens, villain Norman has accumulated a
big record of plain and fancy crimes. Among
other things he has “led astray ” one girl who is
an illegitimate daughter of the elderly hero's wi^ e
by Norrison (Styan), and he has his eye on
Alice Dalton (Frances Ross) who is Hargraves’
legitimate daughter. Failing with her the
villain pursues her through four acts and passes
off his lady love (Miss Harrie Ireland) as Har-
graves’ real daughter. There's the story, which,
of course, comes to a happy ending in Scene 3 of
Act IV. It’s a yarn in which the improbabilities
don’t bump against public notice at every turn,
but are occasionally disguised more or less—often
less. Allowing that people can be gulled in the
simplest way (and look at the number of votes G.
H. Reid can poll !), everything that happens “ In
London Town ” could happen, and, if the long
arm of coincidence creaks at the joints occasion-
ally when overstretched, Mr. and Mrs. Bland
Holt come in and relieve the situation with some
humorous by-play.
* * * *
Acting of “In London Town ” is, as is
usual in Bland Holt’s theatre, of sterling merit
throughout. Both Miss Ross and Miss Ireland
are excellently suited, and the fact that Baker
plays an old man part—and plays it remarkably
well—gives something of an air of novelty to the
cast. Max Maxwell takes on the responsibilities
of juvenile lead with success, and an outstanding
feature is Arthur Styan’s effective character
sketch of the old blind rogue, Norrison.
** * *
Sixteen-year-old Ella Caspers’ contralto
voice was sampled by a Sydney audience for the
first time on Monday night, and the audience
liked its flavor so well that it intends to keep an
eye on the singer, and an ear open for the
development of her voice. The latter is of rich
and tender quality, and if the fair-haired pos-
sessor, who is going to settle down to earnest
study in Sydney, fulfils in maturity her vocal
promise of to-day, she will reach a music-peak.
And, when she does —well, long may she peak !
Rivers Allpress, “dear” Mr. Sykes, Mr. Nield
and the Garcia School girls were amongst those
who gave assistance at the concert.
** * *
“ Are you a follower of Brahms ? ” That
is what the horde of performers in the Amateur
Orchestral Society are asking Sydneysiders these
days. If you are not, you will never join the
heavenly throng. Brahms, the last of the Immor-
tals, died in ’97. Last Thursday, the A.O.
Society performed his Symphony in D, for the
first time in Sydney, and the society thinks it did
it so well that the Master’s spook was seen by the
performers in the act of applauding. The
Symphony is fearfully long, and this writer
is assured that, if one were educated up
to it by five or six hearings, the hearer
would learn to love it as the crow loves the beasts
that perish if they aren’t toolong about perishing.
As it was, this listener sat back with shut eyes,
whilst the tenths and twelfths were getting inter-
locked, and whilst the cross accents and triplets
were doing .something fiendish with two bars of
three-four time, and tried to think of what a
famous American critic had said of Brahms. He
was “a composer of great spiritual obsti-
nacy.” Now, that is very soothing. The orches-
tration bristled like a thousand black cats, but
by repeating inwardly again and again “ Brahms
was a composer of great spiritual obstinacy ” the
listener was kept cool as iced goods. But in spots,
in many spots, the Symphony bursts into flower
and fruit, and parts of it ejects cut jewels.
When these spots spotted, the listener, by dint
of deep brain-sinking, recalled the facts that
“ Brahms was modest to gruffness and he
loved beer, the society of Women and good cook-
ing, and sea air and basses are the ground
elements of his music.” The three thousand or so
persons who audienced the piece received it about
as warmly as they do their gas bills—which only
proves that they are not educated up to Brahms.
But they roused up heartily for the rest of the
programme, especially for Miss Doyle’s singing.
The damsel is a Brisbane girl (high mezzo-so-
prano), a pupil of Hazon’s, with a most attractive
presence; but nervousness caught her by the
throat, and often nearly strangled her pretty
notes.

HONOR THY FATHER.
At Melbourne Royal, any night, when Anderson’s in
charge,
The soul that seeks dramatic thrills can get ’em, fine and
large,
For that’s the place where evil deeds are done in won-
drous ways
By Willard, of the skating gait and sneerful, cynic gaze ;
Where Laurence Dunbar wades in blood for Ada Guild-
ford’s sake ;
And Cross gets “ pinched ” for stealing things which he
would scorn to take ;
Whilst, ’neath the shadow of her hat, Miss Duggan vents
her grief,
And ’Erbert Bailey spreads himself in “ comedy relief.”
In England, Russia, France, or Spain the changing scenes
are laid,
But ever by the same bad man the same girl gets be-
trayed :
She wears the same long cloak of shame, her hair hangs
down her back,
And wrongfully her “ fall ” is charged against the hero,
Jack.
Poor Jack I His uncle casts him off, he suffers all the
time,
He stands suspected by the world of many a scarlet
crime ;
But still the long-lost heiress shares that hero’s troubled
life,
And waves her arms and proudly bears “ the sacred name
of wife ”
When any villain comes to woo, and throws her off the
roof,
Because he wants to win her love and seize the missing
Proof.
At last the other woman brings a message from the dead,
And sins enough to sink a ship are heaped on Willard’s
head.
The comic Bailey prances round ; Miss Buckley pats bis
cheek;
The supers’ hearts are full of joy—too full to let them
speak.
For Jack has proved his innocence ; the clouds, he says,
are psat,
And Virtue, good old Virtue, reigns triumphant at the
last.

On Saturday the new season of comic
opera begins with “ The Little Michus,” which is
expected to explain the meaning of many
mysterious advts. re the Gazeka. Favorable
verdicts on “ The Little Michus” have been de-
livered in Sydney, and more recently in Adelaide,

French opera with yearning eyes.

R. G. Knowles, after putting in a pros-
perous seven nights at the Princess’s, has taken
his nimble tongue inland, and at Bendigo he will
conclude his tour on Friday. The Princess’s,
just now closed for want of a tenant, will be
devoted to a reproduction of “The Fatal Wed-
ding ” at an early date.
At Melbourne Town Hall the renewed
municipal organ, with Edwin Lemare at work
upon it, accounts for large audiences twice a week.
Last Saturday’s recital was the tenth of the series,
and the reception given to the performer indi-
cated a growing popularity. Mr. Lemare, by the
way, is going to deliver four afternoon lectures on
Wagner’s “Ring,” at the Athenaeum Hall, this
month. To-night (Thursday) an amateur attack
on two original Australian comedies will be con-
ducted by the author, Arthur Clarice, at the hall
aforesaid. Other halls seem to be suffering a
recovery from the recent epidemic of benefit
concerts.

Last night of his season in Australia
William Collier told his Sydney audience :
We had a terrible time before we came to Australia.
We had to pass through earthquake and fire in ’Frisco ;
and then, to get to Sydney, we had to pass through Mel-
bourne, which was almost as bad.
And more to the same effect. This represents
characteristically the mummer’s attitude in life.
Melbourne didn’t rush to admire Mr. Collier; in
fact, it stayed away from his hard Yankee fun-
making pretty unanimously. So, to him, Mel-
bourne is Mud—a city with no artistic appreci-
ation and no life. Actors (which term, for the
purposes of this paragraph, includes actresses)
always judge a city by the audiences it gives
them. If there is poor business, that city is a
wretched place and all its citizens should be in the
penitentiary. Similarly, plays are judged by the
actor according to the “ fat ” they afford him. A
good play is one in which he has most of the
iimelight; a play in which he is subordinate is
“rotten.” Writer remembers a charming actress’s
judgment on a new play. “The first act was
lovely—just lovely. But I don’t know how the
audience endured the rest.” The conclusion arrived
at (and afterwards found to be correct) was that
the play was much the same all through, but gave
the lady a fine show in Act 1., and no show at all
in Acts 11. and 111. and IV. Melbourne probably
won’t pass any sleepless nights owing to Collier’s
criticism. If it has any tendency to insomnia, it
can comfort itself with the words of high praise of
Melbourne, mixed with disgust of Sydney, which
some other actor man has uttered for equally bad
reasons ; and when Sydney meets that other actor
man, it will have no need to lie awake either.

The Comic Opera Co. has passed on from
Adelaide Royal, much regretted. “The Shop
Girl ” called up various recollections, mostly
pleasing, of Bradfield and the old Gaiety crowd.
The one thing certain is that the piece was better
staged this time ; the mountings of the plays have
been a great feature of the brief season. So has
the wealth of feminine talent. Florence Young
was a constant feature, but hardly any cast could
find places for Clara Clifton, and Evelyn Scott,
and Alma Barber, and Connie Milne, and Nellie
Wilson all at once. “Paul Jones” was the
farewelling production, and the vacant place was
immediately filled by “ The Man from Mexico.”
Improbable is a feeble word with which to
describe this American jumble of convicts and
step-dances, but it serves well enough to reintro-
duce a more volatile Willoughby and a more
genial Ward than of old. As for Miss Palotta,
she does not alter. The Tivoli continues in the
ways of Variety, with a marked and mutual
reliance on male talent. There is some fair
conjuring, and tumbling, and acting; and two
good points are a farcical sketch that does not
end with an explosion, and the absence of any
fliekergraph. The “marvellous” Corrick family,
however, has come back to the Town Hall, and,
in the intervals of making a wide variety of
music, the Corricks rely on the flicker-film a good
deal.
(»ne of the first results of William Lyne’s
Federal Bounties Bill has been the decision of
Victorian Minister of Agriculture Swinburne to
start the State growing flax. He sent an
officer to Maoriland for some thousands of flax
plants. These are to be planted on about 250
acres of State lands in the Blackwood and Leon-
gatha districts. Swinburne reckons that flax-
growing ought to be a profitable industry in
A' ictoria, and he is putting an officer of the Agri-
tural Department in charge of this State Socialistic
enterprise, with'orders to hustle like all Gehenna.

“ Cold made that monarch croak.”
To save that Queen from catching one,

AT POVERTY POINT.
Prince’s Court, the Melbourne Summer rendezvous for
blokes and tarts, is to have a rival. St. Kilda Beach is to
be made into an attractive trysting place for Bill and his
“ bit of skirt.” A syndicate has secured the permissive
occupancy of the reserve in which Pat O’Rourke
came mysteriously to his death a few weeks ago. The
bloodstain is to be removed, and the reserve is to be
surrounded with a 12-foot iron fence and the country
inside it christened “ Dreamland.” What the people who
pay to go inside are going to dream about, and how they
are going to do it in acres are secrets that have not yet
been divulged, but it is safe to forecast on the strength of
the name that Judkins and co. will fall up against the
new idea.
Edward Miles, the Cold Country’s oldest Thespian,
who went to the pit in his private box, the other day, at
the age of 92, took to the boards in ’34 at the suggestion
of Grimaldi. He played Romeo at 19, harlequin at 60,
and retired in his 82nd year.
The late Johnnie Toole was a devil for his practical
joke. It was necessary to his reputation as a tremendous
humorist to go round, playing off tricks and wheezes ;
and during his acting in Melbourne he strained some
points to provide amusement for his friends out of hours.
His favorite jest was to go into an ironmonger’s shop,
and ask for a pound of tripe, or to march up to the teller
in a bank, and demand a glass of beer. Old Johnnie told
of many successes with this freak, but he never told
of his escapade in entering a dainty milliner’s shop
in Swanston-street and asking for an iron stove.
“ I want to buy an iron stove,” he said. “ Have
you any good iron stoves ? ” The young woman behind
the counter looked amazed, and then she said gently;
“ I cannotsay, sir, Will you sit down ? I shall inquire.”
Johnnie sat down, and the girl went to inquire. She re-
turned, and talked soothingly to him about the beautiful
stove that had been sent for, till the mistress of the
establishment came hastening in, via the front door, with
two policemen in tow, and tried to give the eminent
comedian in charge as a lunatic at large. J. L. T. had
to identify himself with his own photograph before the
police were satisfied that it was wise to let him go.
Dunedin Star dramatic critic the other day set himself
to be bright and allusive, and wrote of one character in a
melodrama that he was “ called upon to wander through
a desert ere he sights the Canaan of his part.” The
comp, set it up “can-can,” and so it was printed ; and
the humbled paper had to explain next day that there
really was no indecent dance in Mr. ’s part.
Harry Rickards, of the Tivoli and other Australian
theatres, entered, some time ago, into an engagement
with Ferdinand Glinserretti, leader (and owner) of a team
of five acrobats, for an Australian season. Hearing that
a change had been made in the team, Rickards declined
to keep the contract, on the ground that it was no longer
the crowd he had engaged. An action in the King’s
Bench, in which Glinserretti claimed £2OOO damages, was
the result, and the verdict went against Rickards for £5OO.
Miss Tittell Brune gave a quite too sweet performance
of fresh, young-girlisli innocence at Spencer-street station
(Melb.), the other afternoon. When she arrived in a
brougham to catch the Sydney express, a bodyguard of
eight gushing damozels took her in charge. It looked
as though she must have invited them to play a hasty
game of romps with her before the train went off. The
average age of the affectionate young creatures was about
17£, and some of them wore their back hair in a pigtail
with a ribbon at the end of it. They loaded her with
bunches of violets ; they hung on to her arms, fought for
the privilege of getting close to her, and generally con-
ducted themselves like lambkins. The actress meanwhile
played the part of a slightly elder sister, unconscious of
everybody save her bevy of playmates. It was only once
or twice that Miss Tittell marred her clever impersona-
tion of artlessness by glancing keenly about her to judge
what kind of effect the show was creating for advertising
purposes. Can a well-known actress on a railway plat-
form ever forget to lose sight of the fact that she is adver-
tising herself?
The just-deceased Russell Sage, the American multi-
millionaire, was as well-known for his meanness as for his
millions. His name was familiarised all the world over
by the “Belle of New York,” in which “the Queen of
Kammic Appera” (American pronunciation) used to sing:
When in my diamonds I appear
I look like a beautiful chandelier,
And Russell Sage would fall down dead
If he’d to pay my salary.
Chronicled that, when San ’Frisco was shivering, the
famous tenor Caruso rushed into the street in slim attire
and, excitedly accosting a well-known attorney, said,
“ What shall Ido ? ” “ Oh, don’t bother me, you d
Dago; I have troubles of my own,” said the attorney.
Earthquake is a great leveller. It brings 10-storey hotels
to the dust and reduces the greatest tenor on earth to a
mere Dago.
“ Cockaigne ” : Toole may have been a good actor in his
early time, but, when I saw him, he was only a comic
lecturer, and not so good as Greville at that. London
steams the acting out of man or woman. Ask any Austra-
lian what he thinks of Beerbohm Tree. Even at his little
London house Toole’s seasons had much of the bogus
element to get up the reclamO for the provinces. Usual
trick. “ One hundred nights in London. Must be good.”
When he went to America they said, “ Here’s the joker.”
So they gave him a dishful of clams to eat, as “Our
Oysters.” After negotiating one, Toole said, “ I feel as if
I had swallowed a baby.”
“S.S.” : It took Milo and Cruickshank a week or so to
make an impression in the fog of London, but when they
did arrive they arrived with emphasis, and presently
their names sprang from the foot to the head of the
Pavilion bills, and were screamed at Johnnie Bull in capi-
tals half a yard high. The London papers now say
“ there has never been a greater attraction at this house,
and you are lucky if you catch a glimpse of the stage,
much less of the lady.” Laing, who devised and worked
up the whole show in Melbourne, has now only one dread
—the fear that his Pansy Montague will be sought in
marriage by some wealthy earl. He took an aborigine
nullah away with him for the express purpose of warding
off eligible peers.
“ Snell” : “Little Mary ” regarded as an entertainment
struck me as the clumsiest device that ever brought
loads of gold to a successful dramatist- It is a problem
play in the true sense of the term, since what it’s all
about is supposed to.remain a problem to the audience
till the last gasp. Then it is discovered with grievous
disappointment that the solution was not worth waiting
for. Barrie throw's in a few decent scraps of character,
and turns a ray or two on a human truth here and there ;
but after a curtain-raiser, a prologue, and an explanatory
first act, people are still W'ondering- what it’s all about,
and waiting anxiously for the entertainment to be sprung
upon them. They lave all heard that the mystery
hinges on the digestive apparatus of man, and they think
it will work into a blaze of humor presently, but it never
does, and the denouement is, after all, only a bald ex-
pression of the obvious. Barrie asks us to believe
that Moira Loney gets into an aristocratic family,
and in the course of eight weeks up-ends its
eating arrangements, and revolutionises its mode
of life and its moral attitude, without anybody dis-
covering that she is responsible for anything. The play
is a succession of great preparations for things that don’t
happen, the author is all the time leading up to jokes
that don’t come, and the curtain falls on a huge disap-
pointment. I’ve read that “Little Mary’s” success in
London was due to the gratitude society felt because the
play had produced a phrase that would enable it to refer
to its “ innards ” without feeling that it was rude. Cer-
tainly this is something to be grateful for, as that portion
of us has been almost unmentionable outside medical
treatises since people knocked off talking familiarly of
the lower chest in Smollet’s time, but it’s an amazing
excuse for the success of a bad comedy.
“ T.D.”: Melbourne’s ethereal pianist, Una Bourne, has
arrived in the Big Smoke, more slender and lily-like than
ever, after making a round of the Continental cities.
When I saw her, t’other Sunday, she was one of an in-
teresting trio of former prodigies from Australia, other
two being Amy Castles and violinist Nora M'Kay. Come
to think of it, Australia has an astonishing record of pro-
digies for its age, and beats the older countries hollow
when it comes to the subsequent development of artistic
maturity.
There is no doubt Tittell Brune is a women’s woman.
One little girl at a large dressmaking establishment asked
for the afternoon off to go and bid the lady adieu. She
got it, also a month’s holiday without pay for her “im-
pertinence.” “ But,” said the little worshipper, “ I don’t
care. I asked Miss Brune to kiss me, and she did.”
Ellen Terry, who has just had a jubilee, is engaged by
Beerbohm Tree for a revival of “ Winter’s Tale,” the play
in which she made her first appearance on any stage.
he will act Hermione in her old age. Probably she will
yet act Juliet.
A number of girls in this country will be more or less
thrilled by the news that Maurice Farkoa, the effeminate
Frenchman who appeared with the “Three Little Maids”
co., has got rid of the white forelock which streaked his
head of hair. Just now he is playing the part of a
Chinese lover in “See See,” a new comic opera, and his
hair is all black. He has either dyed the eccentric fore-
lock black to harmonise with its surroundings, or he has
ceased to dye it white, or he has unpinned it and put it
away in a trunk.
“Nym”: In your issue of last week “Melb.” asks if
Tom Lewis, the lad old “ G.8.W.” used to tumble on his
soles, was his son ? In this matter I plead ignorance, but
I do know that “G.8.W.” bad one son, George, by his
charming wife. When last I heard of him, George Lewis
was an orchestra conductor at the Empire, or Tivoli, or
something in the musical line, in London.
It is said that no woman ever was so innocent as Edna
May looks.
A three days’ gorge was lately held to celebrate the
anniversary of the annexation of Cook Islands to Maori-
land. Multitudinous heathenish antics were gone through
in the guise of old-time dances; ceremonies were per-
formed in honor of land, sea and war deities, and a native
drama was staged. The actors didn’t spend much at the
costumiers—just painted themselves various colors,
daubed their heads with mud, and rushed on. Encores
weren’t allowed. It was a wild production, and might
take in Sydney for a half-night season.
“ Melb.” : At one time G. B. W. Lewis got £lOO a week,
a la Hernandez, for equestrianism. In 1872, Mr. and
Mrs. Lewis took a dramatic co. from Australia to
Bombay. Shafto Robertson was a member ; he was a
brother of Mrs. Kendal and of dramatist Tom Robertson.
On the voyage Shafto fell from the deck into the engine
room, and was killed, on the same day that Walter Mont-
gomery suicided in London. When Creswick came here
he was bitterly disappointed because his old chum
Coppin would not engage him for the Royal. He was on
the point of returning to London when the Lewises en-
gaged him for the Bijou.
“T.D.”: The high water mark of success for an ora-
torio singer in England is to secure a footing as soloist at
the great provincial festivals, where Handel and Men-
delssohn serve as the supporting pillars, and a roof is
provided by the compositions of Elgar, Parry, Standford,
and other British composers of “solid” merit—in two
senses. So far, two Australians, Melba and Crossley,
have been included among festival singers, and a third
will shortly have to be added in the person of Melbourne’s
Mary Conly, who is to sing at the great “ Three Choirs ”
turn-out at Hereford towards the year-end. Madame
Conly is one of the singers about whom comparatively
little has been chronicled in Australia, probably for the
reason that she has been kept so busy with English work
that she hasn’t had very much time to send the wily
paragraph around.
Melba still retains her position as the operatic “ star ’
of the London season. Her appearance in even such an
old-fashioned work as “Rigoletto” draws a “brilliant”
audience, with King Edward and a crowd of royal parties
in the foreground. A “ Melba Night” is the same attrac-
tion that a “ Patti Night ” used to be. Talking of Patti,
she may be described nowadays as a cockney fetish. She
gave an afternoon concert at the Albert Hall in June, and
the musical critics told her that she retained “ all her
ineffable purity of tone,” and was acquiring new “ depth
and richness in her lower notes.” Which means that
Patti, at 63 years of age, is not so much a singer as an
institution. People go to see her and applaud. At the
concert in question the other vocalists were Santley
(older than herself), the veteran tenor, Ben Davies, and
Ada Crossley, whose voice, of course, was a refreshing
contrast to the others, but who got no particular mention
from the newspaper flatterers of the elderly Madame.

Australian singer, Frances Alda, had a law suit in Paris
lately. She had leased aflat from M. Alexandre Henzey,
a member of the French Academy and a grand officer of
the Legion of Honor, and, in spite of his protestations,
adorned her balcony with boxes of flowers and kept a
lapdog in the flat. So Monsieur of the French Academy
and the Legion of Honor brought an action against her,
in the Second Chamber, for £l2O and the rescinding of
the lease, with the result that Frances had to pay him
£32, and shift her flower-boxes and dog elsewhere.
“ Melb.” : How much better is Knowles able to pull a
great house by himself than he was when encumbered by
assistance 1 Then he and a galaxy of artists, such as
Henry Lee, could not magnetise a crowd among them.
This was Garner’s last shot, so far as concerns us. He
was too far ahead of the time—and Rickards. A man of
sound taste, Arthur Garner. He could see form at a
glance. But what’s the good, if you haven’t the tide
running with you? Garner wrenched round the
whole Australian drama, and paved the way for Brough
and Boucicault, when he brought hither his London
Comedy Co., including Blanche Stammers, her mother,
that elfinishly-gifted, unlucky Fred Marshall, and so forth.
Your Coppins and other Artful Dodgers had been saying
that nothing so intrinsically good could pay. Maybe it
did not pay mightily. As middleman with Williamson
and Musgrove, Garner was the impracticable, the seventh-
heavenly sort. To he sure, he made a mush with Fleming
Norton —but only because F.N. was patronised and spoiled
in London by royal highnesses. Taking Garner all round,
he would fail where such managers as Henry Irving,
Charles Kean, Phelps and Edwin Booth fail—-in romantic
and somewhat noble ideal.
“ The Moocher ”: Re Ella Caspers. Rejoiced to hear
that, anyhow, one budding genius thinks Australian
teaching good enough for her. I framed The Bulletin
photo, of her; and wish she may prosper. Have often
thought that teachers here are, to a great extent, respon-
sible for the efflux to the Old Land of all our promising
young musicians. Herr Screamer finds a girl in a far-out
town. She hasthe makings of a good voice. He sees in her a
good advt. for himself, so he booms her hard and then ships
her to England. Result: much talk about clever Herr and
an increase in his roll of pupils. Few seem to notice that
she is heard of no more ; and, by the time she is forgotten
out here, Herr is ready with a fresh wonder. No doubt
Ella Caspers has a hard row to hoe. When she is
“ finished,” the Very Best People here won’t like her.
She is bound to be “ offly crude, you know.” Yet I think
the people, the mere vulgar majority, will like her all the
better for being “ made in Australia.” Anyway, here’s
luck to Ella !

Nemesis, or some other long-armed avenger,
has overtaken all the available members of
Melb. Hospital committee who helped in
that competitive design affair some months
ago. The committee awarded the first prize
of £3OO to a close friend of one of themselves,
despite the report of the appointed judges that
his design was nothing like the best. Three of
the minor committeemen who voted for paying the
money to their colleague’s friend stood for re-
election last week. They were opposed by three
men almost unknown in public life. These three
went in easily, and the old ones came to the
ground with a heavy noise. Apparently the
governors (which is .Johnsonese for subscribers)
thought that a hospital committee so willing to
look after its friends has little time left to look
after the patients.
Victoria is again wasting money on its
Fisherman’s Bend Canal, the tortuous channel
whereby steamers crawl up to Melbourne between
two flat and muddy wildernesses. The canal was
made on the advice of that imported engineering
genius, John Coode. It cost almost as much as
would have sufficed to dig a straight channel from
Melbourne to Port Melbourne, abandoning the
Lower Yarra altogether, and the straight channel
would have reduced the distance from Melbourne
to the Bay by one-half, consequently there would
be only half as much channel to keep dredged.
Why Sent can’t make up his mind to abandon
the troublesome old canal, and let the Lower
Yarra go to perdition, and make the short cut
even now, no one seems able to understand.
The Local Government Extension Bill,
now before N.S.W. Assembly, makes provision
for much municipal socialism. Schedule 3,
embodying the powers which may be conferred on
a municipality, enumerates, among other things,
the ownership of water-supply and sewerage,
trams and light railways, ferries, markets, light-
ing, swamp reclamations, butter factories,
dairies, public baths, hydraulic power, public
batteries, laundries, kitchens, pawnshops, lodging
houses, amusements and workmen’s dwellings.
Useful powers conferred are to regulate all solici-
tation or collection for charity, the opening of
roads, and the erection of houses. Put together,
these represent a fearful amount of municipal
socialism.
WHEN THE CIRCUS CAME TO TOWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the circus came to town,
With its coaches and four, and its steeds galore, and the
band, and the painted clown,
Out to the street with a shout we’d fly,
To look at the elephants marching by ;
Our hearts would beat, and hopes run high
As we followed it up and down ;
And naught in the air, the sea, or sky
Loomed half so large in our youthful eye
As the circus that came to town.
And after it we went.
And we got in the way of the men when they were rigging
the circus tent:
And around the animals’ cage we’d fool,
And push the ape from his three-legged stool,
And get chucked off by the bucking mule
(Which a charm to the business lent).
We wagged it a week, awaj r from school,
And got found out, as a general rule—
A thing that we’d all repent.
When the circus came to town
We swallowed our tea in a trice, and then to the tent
would hurry down,
And we took the full of our shilling’s worth,
And we cheered the drummer’s pond’rous girth,
And clapped our hands in the height of mirth
At the tricks of the painted clown.
And wond’rous thoughts in our minds had birth ;
And we thought that the band was the best on earth,
Of the circus that came to town.
We fondly recalled the scene,
When the elephants went and the circus tent no longer
adorned the green,
The cowboy decked in his gaudy scarf,
The juggling man with his magic staff,
The songs that we heard on the phonograph,
And the smell of the kerosene ;
And we started a show on our own behalf,
And “ performed ” on the back of the poddy calf,
And sighed for the might-have-been.
Now the circus comes to town,
And it rattles along, and a bare-foot throng is cheering it
up and down ;
And the elephants still are marching there,
And the thud of the drums is in the air-^
But the hands of Time the mask will tear
From many a painted clown ;
Clumsy, indeed, is the dancing bear,
And the wonderful band has a tinny blare
When the circus comes to town.
N.S.W.
MARY ANN.

PENEOLD'S
WINES
Methodist parson Worrall, of Victoria, a
loose-tongued fanatic who talks violently of
heaven, hell, hotel bars, horse-racing, and other
matters of which he knows practically nothing,
was summoned to the Vic. Parliament House
last week to receive the dignity of a censure
from the Speaker for having uttered “a false,
un-Christian libel ” upon members of Parlia-
ment in general, and Sam Gillott in particular.
The Speaker’s censure was a wisely worded
address, therefore parson Worrall, in getting it,
did actually receive an undeserved dignity.
Bent’s Parliament, having summoned the absurd
preacher to the blessed Bar of the House, was
bound to take him seriously, and the verdict
of the public is that it served Bent’s
Parliament jolly well right. The incident
is regrettable only for the fact that Brother
Worrall’s libel was not nearly so true as it might
have been. He left unsaid the things that he
ought to have said, and he said the things he
ought not to have said, and there was no health
in the ravings of Brother Worrall. The pub-
licity given to the parson’s nonsense may help to
hasten the suppression of Law-breaker Wren, in
his present capacity of a mock philanthropist and
champion of the Labor party, but it can’t be much
of a help, anyhow. For the moment, the uncom-
promising Methodist preacher looks rather less
foolish than the legislators who called him to
account for his foolishness.
WELSHED.
“ Did you have any luck at the races , Bob? ”
"Rather. W hy I hacked the winners in the first
four races , and I’d ’ a’ hacked him in the
last, too, hut I hadn’t any money left.”
Every party (almost every man) in the
Victorian Parliament came with discredit out of
the farcical Worrall scrimmage. Bent and his
backers blundered through a series of humilia-
tions. The Labor Opposition consented to the
initial foolishness of calling the screaming parson
to the Bar. and, by so doing, forfeited its claim to
be regarded as the defender of tfc free preach.’ 3
When the parson arrived, and stood to his pop"
gun, Prendergast,.Warde and others made free-
dom of speech their text, but not ail their mouth-
could give their action the aspect of any-
thing but a political dodge. MacKinnon and
l eacock sat alertly watching for chances to cap-
ture toe Methodist sympathy ahead of the Labor
party. Through the debate, Sam Gillott, the
person most concerned, sat with his hat pressed
down over his eyes—a depressed and Worried
trimmer. As a side issue, Worrall stated that,
after the performance was over, he and Gillott
met and shook hands cordially. Worrall pro-
fessed to believe that there was blood on Gillott’s
hand, but perhaps Gillott wiped bis allegedly
gory paw on the tail of Worrall’s coat before the
shaking operation took place, and thereby saved
Worrall from going away with the appearance of
Macbeth after ho had made a vacancy where
Duncan used to be.

Ordinary Victorians have to take what
they get in the way of railway carriages, and be
thankful that the thing is a carriage and not a
trolley. But the Rev. Henry Worrall and his
admirers arc apparently not pleased with any-
thing infermr to the best rolling-stock in the State.
-i“ e hysterical shepherd, who carries two hand-
kerchiefs to weep into, seems to have been
sobbing some more, for Hicks, M.L.C., deemed
it necessary to move in the House of Proputty—
That, as the railway carriages of the special train run
from Bendigo to Melbourne on Tuesday were quite unfit
for use, the Commissioners should provide better and
more suitable vehicles.
Ihe tickets for the special that conveyed the
martyr to be eaten by Bent’s lions were sold at
11s. each little more than half the ordinary
second-class return fare. Present writer and
numerous other persons have frequently had
to journey to Bendigo during the past few years
(paying full fare) in antiquated second-class car-
nages which had no ventilators, and in which
each window consisted of an immovable sheet of
glass. Also, the paint was worn off the grimy
walls, and the seats were so close that the knees
of the two rows of passengers almost met. Com-
pared with these, the carriages of the Worrall
cheap special were palatial, and, besides, martyrs
ought to put up with some inconvenience. Yet
’lcks, M.L.C., shows a lofty disregard of the
insanitary carriages that are inflicted on the
ordinary, full-paying passenger, and grows indig-
nant only when Worrall and his followers are not
favored with satin and rosewood for 11s. That’s
so like Teles.

One of the most cheerful things in connec-
tion with the Martyrdom of St. Worrall hap-
pened at the Commonwealth Parliament. A
number of Judkins’ friends turned up there to see
the martyr come through the flames—apparently
they had been too busy pursuing the flesh and the
devil to know that two lots of Parliament were in
session. There were a couple of parsons among
them, and, after listening for two hours to Bigjohn
Forrest’s Budget speech, it dawned on them that
something was wrong ; possibly they had come
too late, and the martyr had met his doom already.
As they were about to make inquiries, a wicked
pressman happened along, and, after directing
them to the State House, thoughtfully informed
them that Worrall had been fined £lOOO, and that
Bent had been ordered to sit upon his chest till it
was paid. Then they went away sorrowfully.

THE CRAZE FOR ATHLETICS.
THE MISSUS : “ Julia Murphy , have you been drinking?”

It is surprising how seriously Victorian
swaddles take themselves. For about three days
every year they go to Langwarrin or some such
place and revel in scenes of imaginary bloodshed.
It is all imagination, and the best soldier is the
one who imagines most. Last Langwarrin camp,
an imaginary battle of singular imaginary fury
was fought on Mt. Eliza. The attacking party
imagined that Captain Crouch, who was one of
the defenders, had surrendered to them. Captain
Crouch imagined that he was still fighting, and
probably died sword in hand like an imaginary
hero. The attackers, however, told the papers,
which are always imaginative; and, after some
controversy, a Military Board was appointed to see
whose imagination was in better working order.
After hearing some acres of evidence and solemnly
considering it, the Board has now brought in a
comic opera finding quite in keeping with the rest
of the piece. It says that Captain Crouch never
technically surrendered, and, therefore, of course,
he was quite right in imagining that he hadn’t;
but it adds that he looked as if he had surren-
dered, and so the other side was quite justified
in imagining that he had surrendered —which
leaves things rather more mixed than they were
before.
The Victorian Government is in a difficulty
over Mrs. Cleary, widow. Long ago the late Mr.
Cleary, by virtue of his miner’s right, settled and
built a house on a bit of land in the bed of what is
now the Waranga Basin. A miner’s right isn’t
hereditary, so Mrs. Cleary, who still occupies the
place, is apparently just a trespasser on State
lands. But she declares she will not leave with-
out compensation, and when the Goulburn River
is turned into the great new reservoir next week
she will be drowned if she doesn’t leave. Mrs.
Cleary says cheerfully that she will just stay and
drown. The Bent Government recognises that if
it drowns even a trespassing widow in cold blood
it will probably lose its billet, and if it sends the
police to drag her out on a cold hillside it will
also create ill-feeling. Besides, she might even then
go back and drown herself at the old address,
whereupon people like Worrall might say Bent
killed her. Yet the open payment of compensa-
tion to a trespasser would be an awkward prece-
dent. Probably the best thing would be for Bent
to say openly that Mrs. Cleary will just have to
drown if she wants to, and at the same time send
round someone surreptitiously to bribe her to go
out. The amount spent might, perhaps, be put
down on the Estimates as repairs.
Sydney Morning Herald, when it joined
the syndicate to publish H. G. Wells’ forecast of
America’s destiny, caught a Tartar. Wells
deals it out heavily to private enterprise at inter-
vals. Extracts on Chicago :
It was like a prolonged, enlarged, mingling of the
south side of London, with all that is bleak and ugly in
the Black Country. It is the most perfect presentation
of nineteenth century individualistic industrialism I have
ever seen —in its vast, its magnificent squalor it is pure
nineteenth century. . . , It is indeed a nineteenth cen-
tury nightmare that culminates beyond South Chicago in
the monstrous fungoid shapes, the endless smoking
chimneys, the squat retorts, the black smoke pall of the
Standard Oil Company. ... Its owners have no more
sense, no better moral quality, than to make it stink in
the nostrils of anyone who comes within two miles of it,
to make it a centre of distribution for disease and decay,
an arena of shabby evasions and extra profits, a scene of
brutal economic conflict and squalid filthiness, offensive
to every sense. (I wish I could catch the soul of Herbert
Spencer and tether it in Chicago for a while to gather
fresh evidence upon the superiority of unfettered indi-
vidualistic enterprises to things managed by tlie State.)
Want of discipline ! Chicago is one hoarse cry for
discipline !
And referring to the bright spot in Chicago—the
well-known “ Field Houses ” :
And this Field House is not an isolated philanthropic
enterprise. It is just one of a number that are dotted
about Chicago, mitigating and civilising its squalor. It
was not distilled by begging and charit3' from the stench
of the stockyards or the reek of Standard oil. It is part
of the normal work of a special taxing body created by
the Legislature of the State of Illinois. It is just one of
the fruits upon one of the growths that spring from such
persistent creative effort as that of the Chicago City Club.
It is Socialism—even as its enemies declare.... !

grievous lack of moral courage on the part of the
man with the long rags on his body and the right
to wear the wonderful figment on his head.

THE INCOMPREHENSIBLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Come, let us wander Forth ! Ah ! Once Again
The Breathless Postman and the Borrowed Pen,
Also the Office Boy, who Staggers up
Bearing the Note that tells me Where and When.
Unmoved, he yields the Missive to my Clutch,
For Love he knows not, nor Cares overmuch,
But Whistles thro’ his Teeth and gets him Hence
(I’ve met, I think, At Least a Million such).
O ! Youth that never wrote to Ladye Fayre,
Or waited at some Windy Corner where
The Wintry Blast did shriek the Written Words—
“ I’LL run Out, if I have the Time to Spare.”
I do remember me a Summer Gone
She, too, was fair; and Oh ! her Eyes—they Shone !
(She was a Tea Room Maid and always Gave
A Smile away with Every Currant Scone.)
But now ! Ah me ! (plus) Well-a-day ! Alas !
(What pyramids of Grief we do amass 1)
We wandered Many Miles beneath the Stars
And parted—but we saved her Father’s Gas.
Please not to scorn this Tale, or Look Askance.
I only thought to Interest. Perchance
There is a sympathetic Soul somewhere
Who can appreciate a mild Romance.

Salvarmy Commissioner M‘Kie draws a
luscious picture of the ball-room :
It was said that throughout the world 50 per cent, of
the girls who earned their living by shame fell first at the
dance. Private balls and dancing parties were the feeders
of public halls, and public halls were the harvest of the
libertine. They were the rivers that emptied themselves
into the ocean of abandonment. It was almost an utter
impossibility for any woman to walk into a ballroom in
the ballroom dress of the day without some man sinning
against her in his heart. It could not be proper for a man
who was neither father, husband, nor brother, even to
hold the hand of the lady as it was held in the saloons,
much less put his arm round her waist and draw her to
him until they could almost hear each other’s heart heat.
The Lord wanted us to enjoy life, but not at the expense
of other men’s wives and daughters.
It would certainly not be safe to invite Brother
McKie to a dance. This excited saint seems able
to imagine all sorts of sinful emotions that the
average ball-roomer is too world-weary to know
anything about. He knows exactly what artistic
feelings are roused beneath his fresh and innocent
old weskit by the sight of a pair of white shoulders
appearing through the mouth of the cavern of
evening dress. If he had started as a dramatic
critic when he was young, and sat among rows of
bare shoulders in the dress circle, watching the
sinful show on the stage, he would have found,
after a week or so, that he took as much interest
in all this beautiful meat as if it were hung up in a
shop and marked 6d. per lb. A Salvarmy Commis-
sioner seems able to get more excited about waists
and shoulders at the age of 40 or so than an ordi-
nary smoke-dried worlding can at 25.

A man named Ogier had a rare stroke of
luck, the other day. He was charged in Mel-
bourne with “receiving” a watch, the property
of newspaper man Cunningham— not the Cun-
ningham who steers the Argus, but the other one.
The exhibit was identified, and the case looked
all the blacker against the man because of
a whole heap of other charges. But while the
jury was making up its verdict a messenger handed
the Judge a parcel, which had just been left at
the Crown Law Office, containing an exactly
similar watch and a note bearing the words—
“ Cunningham’s watch. Ogier is innocent.”

Ballarat East has got
a prisoner with pluck.
Last April he departed
through an underground
passage, climbed a wood-
pile, placed a wheelbarrow
on top, and so reached the
coping of the gaol wall,
then jumped 25ft. down
on the other side. He
broke his leg, but managed
to crawl about a quarter of
a mile, and was found,
nearly dead, in a gutter.
He was in hospital after
that for months ; but dur-
ing the last few weeks
he had been hobbling
about on crutches. The
gaol surgeon reckoned that
the hobble was mostly
pretence, but before be
could give effect to his
thoughts the prisoner
cleared up any suspicion.
One night he stood his
bed up on end, and, work-
ing from the top, knocked
a large hole through the
brick ceiling of the
ward in which he was
confined. Then he climbed through the
hole into a dark labyrinthine kind of gar-
ret, started to belt a hole in the roof, and
was doing well when he was missed. A warder
reached the garret by another route and found
the nearly ex-prisoner waiting for him with a
brick. For reasons connected with a revolver he
didn’t throw the brick. Now, had the prisoner
got away, his only chance lay in doing the 25ft.
drop over again, and on the doctrine of chances
and averages he would probably have broken his
neck this time. Even Brassey, who was an ex-
perienced faller-off, couldn’t make a habit of
dropping 25ft. with impunity. And, rather than
stay in a nice warm gaol, this hardened breaker
and departer was ready to take the chance.
* * *
The other day some nature-lover told
Premier Bent that those in charge of that beauti-
ful Melbourne city playground, the Fitzroy Gar-
dens. were cutting down big trees by the dozen,
and replacing the grassy lawns with dolly flower-
beds, in the Monkey fashion. Bent went and
saw, and said that if the destruction didn’t cease,
the annual vote would. The axe and saw lay idle
for a few days; then they got to work again in
the same old manner. But it is significant that
all through these gardens the English elms, oaks,
and other trees are carefully guarded. It is only
the native trees that are hacked and hewed about
and rooted up, as though they were an eyesore and
an abomination.

“0.K.” : The various American ways of
uttering the English language are ordinarily
classed together under the term “accent,” but
whereas the accents —
there are several of them
—are peculiarities which
may be pretty accurately
mimicked by an Austra-
lian, the American pro-
duction of words begin-
ning with a vowel seems
to be inimitable by
foreigners. You notice
it in the talk of the
women, rather than the
men, as though it were
almost a distinctively
American sexual pecu-
liarity of speech. The
pronunciation is labored,
not to say clumsy, where
the word starts with a
vowel—especially “o”
or “ a.” The cockney
drops aspirates from
where they ought to be
as easily as a Frenchman
who is speaking English.
Or he will put in a false
aspirate with absurd em-
phasis. The American
woman, well-educated or
otherwise, has a tendency
to struggle with “o’’ or
“a” at the beginning of
a word, as though she
were fighting against the
temptation to put in an
aspirate. She “makes a
mouthful ” of the smallest
words. Miss Ola Hum-
phrey, the leading lady in
“The Squaw Man,” is
one more instance of an
American actress who can-
not adopt Hamlet’s advice
to the players. Words
which should be spoken
“ trippingly on the tongue”
have to be brought out
from under the tongue, or
forced with difficulty from the back
of the throat. The effort is palpable. One can’t
imagine these American ladies reeling off long
speeches with the ease of, say, Miss Maud Milton
(of Musgrove’s Shakspearean Co.), although her
musical intonation was rather American in its
cadences. What is the original cause of the pecu-
liarity, and why is it more markedly feminine
than masculine?
There are some practical people in Ade-
laide. A local youth was accused of impossible
behavior in the family circle, and the accuser was
his own father. Did the maligned son commit
suicide, or biff his unnatural parent in the eye, or
clear his character, like Walter Baker, by enlist-
ing in the army and shedding much blood in the
forefront of the armed scrap? He didn’t. He
sued his father in the local court for £49 19s.
libel damages, and obtained a verdict for the full
amount.
“ Spring ” discourses on Blood :
How these stodgy weeklies love Blood 1 The issues of
Sydney Mail and T. and C. Journal on a recent date
were two big, red smears. Each sheet seemed to pro-
claim that it was more blanky than the other, and that
in its extra blankiness lay its claim to he rushed by the
public. One journal sent its photo, fiend to the slaughter
of beasts at Glebe Island. A picture showed a fierce-
looking man, stained with Blood, about to plunge
a murderous weapon into a peaceful moo-cow;
s bcr , aJtSLifWta.“
sheep. In another were shown designs cut in the
still quivering warm flesh by butcher artists stained with
Blood. Over all these hung a cloud of warm-blood
odors reminiscent of Chicago. These sickening, hut
true, sights were backed up by a more or less reliable
picture of Father Gapon’s death. The pale, strangled
corpse, grotesquely twisted, grinned from the cheerless
page. An artist’s conception of the Madrid bomb out-
rage was served up hot and steaming on another
page. There was a lot of Blood on the street that
day. But in the pictures of the Ballina murder
the camera gloated with its star gloat. The face
of every one who had the remotest connection
with the butchery looked out proudly on an appreciative
world. The usual cross of the cheap, back-street tragedy
press located the place where one victim fell and the
letterpress proudly stated that Blood was found there. I
searched in vain for a big, black cross pointing out the
spot where the rival reporters died fighting for the pos-
session of a blood-stained shirt; but I presume that they
did it, and that the picture was omitted by pure accident.

Sydney potters away at a few small street
improvements now and again—that is to say, the
City Council does: the 43 outside municipalities are
hopeless, and seem likely to remain that way till
Greater Sydney comes to pass. There is more
sclieme than actual fulfilment about the Sydney
improvements—in fact, barring what was done at
Moore-street and Martin-place, it is difficult to re-
member that anything has really been finished
yet—but even schemes are better than nothing,
just as it is better to dream than to be dead. The
latest proposal is to widen Oxford-street, between
College and Bourke streets, from the present 60ft.
to 90ft. It isn’t a very big idea, for it will still
leave Oxford-street practically the only outlet
from the city eastward, and the improvement
can’t he carriprl far—at the host it rrmqf sten nt
can tbe earned tar at the best it must stop at
the boundary of the corpse-municipality of Pad-
dington—but still it shows that one of the 44
municipalities is threatened with intelligence.

“Y. ” : Re deaths under chloroform—
Melbourne had two in a week—no evidence seems
to be given at inquests regarding the quality ot
the chloroform used. In the last Melbourne case
(a death at St. Vincent’s Hospital) it was sug-
gested that the chloroform might have deterio-
rated from exposure to light, which produces
suffocating chlorine compounds. That, probably,
is a danger which cannot be afterwards traced.
Another question concerns the purity of the
anaesthetic. Chloroform comes on the market at
various prices. That of a certain firm is said by
surgeons to he absolutely reliable; at other
samples they squint more doubtfully. Why not
analyse the chloroform whenever misadventure
occurs—or, better still, analyse samples of all
chloroform sent into the market, and allow none
to go into use that is not of perfect purity ?
Observed at Sydney Water Police Court:
Act 1., Scene 11. in the Crick-Willis-Bath Police Court
formality, on Friday last, was more interesting by reason
of the setting than of the action proper. The business
began with the calling up and disposal of several ladies
who had had rum overnight. Then the male drunks were
postponed to leave the way clear for the celebrities.
W. N. Willis headed a procession, which included Charles
Bath, Barrister Lamb (defence), and Barrister Garland
(prosecution), and Lands Commission Secretary Houston,
but no Crick. Willis seated himself serenely and con-
templated the court with subdued curiosity. Lamb rose,
explained that Crick was on his way from bis office,
and argued that an adjournment was desirable to
enable Willis to digest a blue book that would have
made a respectable load for a goat. He held the volume
of printed evidence in one hand, and over-balanced to
the left. It was a reckless proceeding, and for the futjur e
counsel will take that mighty tome in both hands. The
strong-man exercise impressed the court san evidence
of the size of the document Willis had to g J through. A
fireside-sort of conversation ensued about a holiday on
Monday, a half-day on Wednesday, and part-heard cases
in between ; and when it was, after 25 minutes, agreed
that Thursday was the day to adjourn to, it was casually
remarked that Crick was still on his way from his office,
three cricket-ball throws distant. WithoutCrick.it seemed,
nothing could be fixed. The court was assured that Crick
was coming. So the court fined five drunks and then Crick
arrived. He went nimbly to Lamb’s side, got the hang'
of things in a second, and pointed out to the bench that
Tuesday wouldn’t do as Monday was a holiday and Crown
witnesses would want a day or two to recover their sober
senses. Thursday would suit to begin the month’s work
So Thursday was agreed upon, and the procession lef*,
and the court fined some more drunks.

The fact that Victorian Governor Ta'l-
bottle has presented Melbourne City Council wi th
a portrait of Lord Melbourne, after whom the
i city was named, recalls the fact that, before
a its final christening, Melbourne answered to
as many names as a lost dog. It came, m
fact, when anybody whistled to it. When
“the settlement,” or “the township,” was
recognised as inadequate, it became known
as Yarra Yarra or Yarrow Yarrow.
Later, it was styled Bearbrass or Bear-
grass (said to be a corruption of Bear-
purt, which was unreliably stated to
I be the native name for the locality).
Some of the residents from Tas-
mania took a hand by calling it
Batmania, after John Batman.
John Pascoe Fawkner, not to be
outdone, christened it Glenelg,
in honor of the Secretary for
the Colonies. Governor Bourlte squelched all
these names by officially making it Melbourne.

Sydney WATCHMAN is sometimes as funny
as a circus. T’other day it dug out a yarn of a
lady who had been proselytised at the R.C. pres-
bytery, Bathurst, imprisoned in the Tempeß.C.
Refuge, and otherwise maltreated by ‘Rome,
In reply, “Rome” brought evidence that the
lady was never proselytised and was never im-
prisoned by “ Rome,” though she had been twice
in a common secular State gaol, and that she
"’as quite an uninteresting individual, with no
claim to all this sudden prominence. IheWATCH-
man swallows its fairy tale with a grimace, and
then, in no wise abashed, gets back to the old
comminations about “Rome” :
In all this sad business there is only one fact which
affords us any satisfaction. The woman in her declara-
tion said she had been a Protestant, but had yielded to
priestly overtures, and had become a Roman Catholic.
There is no evidence to justify that Statement. To the
?aol authorities she gave her religion as Roman Catholic.
Rome has herself denied any imputation of-perversion,
Protestant she never was. She is A PRODUCT OF
ROME—in many respects only too typical a product—
and, as such, after the demands of justice have been satis-
fied, we hand her back to Rome, hoping they will show
her mercy. Thank God, Protestantism has no such types
furni ® h •• • Naturally the question arises, Was the con-
-11 “
The parsons who run the Watchman kind of
literature don’t blush worth a cent when their
tal o bursts, and their injured heroine proves to
be a very ordinary kind of gaol-bird. A spieler
°. r a bottle-oh may object to being proved a foolish
bar, but a certain variety of parson only looks the
more virtuous when he is shown up. Anyhow,
why was this uninteresting female dragged into
publicity ? Apparently she was a dreadfully corn-
monplace person with a bad habit of getting into
gaol, and why there should be columns of shriek
on the Watchman’s part because she was a rene-
gade Protestant, and then more columns because
she wasn’t, passes comprehension.
SINNING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Men sin against women in their hearts.”—Moan from
Salvarmyman M‘Kie.
Girls with cool, transparent blouses,
Chatting to us in your houses—
Sweet and winning ;
Dainty things of white and pink,
Sometimes while we look we think. . . .
Are we sinning?
Maidens in the ballroom seated,
Silken dresses, ruched and pleated,
Point lace tricktd ;
Gazing at your throats, our eyes
May betray a slight surmise—
Is it wicked ?
Man and woman once abode
In a garden where there strode
Angels haughty ;
When, amid the murmuring leaves,
Adam’s eyes looked into Eve’s—
Were they naughty ?
Dismal Army man, beware 1
Poets make of women fair
Half their story ;
Eyes grow tender, senses thrill,
And around us trembles still
Eden’s glory.
