The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Prime Minister Deakin, it is said, pooh-poohs
the idea of paying the States £9,000,000 or £10,000,000 for
transferred properties. His idea is that if the State which
transfers the smallest amount of property per head hands
over lands and buildings worth, say, £llos. per inhabi-
tant, then properties to the value of £llO per inhabitant
should be transferred to the Commonwealth by each State
free of charge, and only the surplus be paid for. Of course,
this idea is fair and logical enough ; for the people in their
Federal capacity to pay themselves in their State capa-
city for their own properties, except in so far as is
required to balance accounts, is quite unnecessary. But
it would be just as fair and logical for the Commonwealth
to make a settlement in full by taking over sufficient
State debt to cover the whole value of the transferred
properties, and anything that will make a beginning at
the transfer of the State debts to the Commonwealth is
highly desirable. In fact, this paper would gladly see
the Commonwealth pay 40s. in the £, and take over
£18,000,000 of State debts in payment for £9,000,000 of
State properties handed over, just to give the process of
debt transference an extra lift. It badly wants a lift.
m ♦ * *
N.S. Wales lias begun its new financial year
with a month’s revenue of £920,705, or no less than
£164,106 more than it had in July, 1905 ; and of that
£164,106 the railways supplied £57,885. The expenditure
charged against revenue was £936,661 ; so, in spite of the
enormous increase in railway earnings, the State went
£15,956 to the bad on the month’s transactions, without
taking into account the borrowed £124,786 which J. Car-
ruthers spent. The Julv revenue for the past four years
has beenl9o3, £898,300; 1904, £810,954 ; 1905,
£756,599 ; 1906, £920,705. So J. Carruthers got £lOO,OOO
more than the average of the previous three years, and
yet leaned on Cohen to the extent of nearly £125,000 —at
the rate of £1,500,000 a year. How much revenue does
the “ Reform ” Treasurer reckon N.S. Wales would have
to yield before it would be a fair thing to leave off bor-
rowing?
N.S.W. railways (apart from tramways) last
year earned £384,980, after paying working expenses and
interest; and, while bringing in a Bill to sack the Rail-
way Commissioners, Treasurer Carruthers collars this
surplus and wears it as a halo. Simultaneously he opens
the new Sydney railway station, which is about a mile
from the centre of the city, and more than that from
most of the wharves. Nobody knows what the old rail-
way station cost, but it Was probably £350,000 or
£400,000 ; and it is just now being torn up, and pulled
to pieces, and thrown on the rubbish heap. The cost of
removing it, along with the extra cost of carrying on the
traffic while it is being removed, will about counter-
balance anything that may be got for the old material.
According to every rule of honest finance, the cost of the
old rooted-up station, so far as it was provided out of
capital, should now come off the balance-sheet; in other
words, last year’s railway surplus ought to be used to
pay off that part of the old railway' debt which the old
station represented. To call the £384,980 profit, and use
it to make a surplus with, and encourage persons to come
along with demands for freight and other concessions,
while leaving the old station in the balance-sheet, is
financial fake and dishonesty.
Thomas Bent, of Victoria, having half a million
over from last year, announces that he will ship it to
England to pay off Bull Cohen to that extent; and the
man who can do that, while not borrowing to pay for new
works, could be forgiven even worse comic songs than T.
Bent sings. It is quite another thing to create a surplus
by deferring payment for works, or paying for them out
of loans. They do that in N.S. Wales and call it Reform ;
and the country is no forrader” than it was before—
or very' little. The result of Bent’s repayments will pro-
bably be that he will get money, which he will pre-
sently have to borrow to pay off old loans, at a much-
reduced rate of interest. Canada, which has a fine, sub-
stantial sinking fund, almost makes the fund provide its
own annual increments in this way ; the money it has
been able to save through the much-reduced rate of in-
terest at which Cohen is now prepared to lend to Canada
is almost equal to the sinking fund pay'ments. So Bent’s
£500,000 will probably be a very fine investment—even if,
as is likely, he borrows the greater part of his 1907
renewal loan of £4,000,000 in Australia.
The Victorian .loan of £4,000,000, which falls
due.on Ist July, 1907, was raised in 1881, and carries 4 per
cent. By the end of the year the State will have paid
26 years interest; and reckoning it as having paid com-
pound interest, Which is the only way the thing can
fairly' be reckoned, that £4,000,000 will have cost Victoria
£6,663,344 in interest alone. And Victoria will still
owe the original £4,000,000. On the other hand, if to his
renewal loan of £4,000,000 Bent attaches a provision for a
sinking fund of £lOO,OOO a year, invested at 3£ per cent.,
by the end of the next 25| years there will be just enough
accumulated to wipe out the whole loan and he done
with it.
YVestralia begins the financial year with a
deficit for July of £10,398 : revenue, £200,697 ; expendi-
ture, £211,095. About the same time as this was
announced, new Treasurer Wilson proposed a land-tax.
“ The falling-off in Commonwealth refunds in four years
had amounted to £352,084, and the interest and sinking-
fund had increased by £219,898, so the State was £571,000
worse off than when Federation was entered upon.”
In other words, mostly-absentee cos. were taking
out millions by skimming the cream off the country, and
there was a boom in consequence, and Westralia was im-
porting large quantities of luxuries in bottles and pots.
But instead of making those who were ravenously ex-
ploiting the country pay for the works necessary for the
exploitation, Westralia borrowed the money. Now most
of the cream has gone ; the bottled and potted luxuries
are not arriving in the old quantities; partly on that
account Customs have fallen off ; but the debt and the
interest bill remain. That this would happen might have
been easily foreseen, and almost as easily provided against.
The Government’s land : tax is not graduated ;it will be
no more than 2d.; hut there will be an extra 50 per
cent, on absentees. Its main object is to bring in
revenue; and the exemption stops at £250. It is
few of the things that a land-tax ought to be; but
Westralia has got into such a state that it must
do something, and this is a handy something. The com-
plete overhaul of the State’s public service might follow.
Westralia badly needs at its Treasury some plain, brown
man, such as Turner was and Kidston is, and Wilson has a
chance that rarely comes a Treasurer’s way.
In Treasurer Peake S. Australia has something
of a plain, brown man, too—only S. Australia is still
borrowing far too much. It had a surplus last year of
£112,474. It paid off £20,000 of Treasury bills during the
year; it did an amazingly honest thing, and wrote
£25,000 off the railways’ capital account; and now
£87'474 is available to redeem some more Treasury bills.
This £87,474 is presumably in addition to the ordinary
sinking-fund payments. S. Australia has two sinking
funds—one, created by ex-Treasurer Holder (now Speaker
of the Reps.) in 1896 ; J per cent, of the total debt has to
go to it yearly. That calls for about £60,000. The other
is a fund to wipe out deficiency bills, and calls for about
£20,000 a year—presumably the £20,000 previously men-
tioned.
Queensland received £118,106 more than it
spent in July—which is a handy contribution towards
the half-year’s interest bill. In July, 1905 the excess was
£94,638.
• * «- *
Outside Queensland, Fremantle (W.A.) is
the first municipality in the Commonwealth to run
its own tramways, but is setting a shocking example
in the matter of extravagant management. Instead
of having a committee of councillors, a Board of five
members has been constituted, and although the
duties are purely nominal the sum of £BOO per annum
is paid in fees —out of which the chairman collars
£2OO. The engineer-manager gets £750, and there
is a secretary and quite an army of clerks, messengers,
inspectors and other officials eating up the receipts at an
alarming rate. There are less than eight miles of tram
lines and only 14 cars constantly running. Perth Tram-
way Co. runs 45 much larger cars, and employs many
more hands, but has only a general manager and one
superintendent. Out of profits a sum of £3OOO to £4OOO
a year is handed over to the Perth City Council. Unless
Fremantle ratepayers look out they won’t have any
profits, but they will find themselves at the bottom of a
very deep hole. To pay interest on the loan for tram-
ways it has been found necessary to strike a fourpenny
rate, and next year the rate will be higher unless the
administration exes, are cut down. The trams are
popular enough, and under proper management ought to
yield a splendid profit, especially if they could be ex-
tended Perthwards. At present the only means of
crossing the Swan River at Fremantle is over an old
ramshackle wooden bridge erected by convicts nearlv
half a century ago. It impedes navigation terribly, and
ought to give place to a swing bridge of modern design.
According to a balance-sheet just published,
the Patriotic Fund, established six or seven years ago for
the relief of cohentingenters and their families, has still
to its credit a sum of £23,000, £20,500 of which has, within
the last few months, been placed on fixed deposit for two
years. Obviously, therefore, it is intended to continue to
hoard the money. There may be some excuse for hoard-
ing now that claims for assistance are probably neither
frequent nor urgent, but there was no excuse for hoard-
ing at an earlier stage, when applicants were often treated
in the most niggardly fashion. Writer remembers inter-
viewing the committee of the fund in those days on
behalf of a very large contributor, who was indignant at
the close-fisted policy that was being adopted, and who,
moreover, was in a position to make things very, very
uncomfortable. The committee was extremely angry at
its action being criticised, and asserted that it was doing
only what business men would do. It had to consider
the future, it said. Well, it has considered the future so
thoroughly that it has now £23,000 which it doesn’t know
what to do with. It is a repetition of the Bulli Fund
fiasco.

1905.. 11,587 14,294 1330 25 „ ~ 10,000 229,573
1906.. 12,270 13,193 1540 25 „ ~ 10,000 217,110
It is when you look at figures like these that you feel
inclined to break out into a little song of thanksgiving
that your own life-policy is in a mutual office. In five
years this co., which, of course, is issuing no new policies,
has paid its policy-holders £56,588 ; but it has paid itself
£50,000. In those five years the policy-holders have paid
in, in premiums, £17,018 ; therefore the co. has paid
them £39,570 net. That means that, out of the
funds which existed five years ago, the policy-holders
have had £39,570 ; the shareholders have had £50,000.
Yet the policy-holders provided the insurance fund, which
even now amounts to £183,807 ; all that the shareholders
ever put into the business was £40,000. And though the
shareholders have for years past been drawing nearly 25
per cent, per annum on their capital, there is no teiling
how much more there may be for them to divide in the
end. It looks as though it will be something handsome.
A new line added to the balance-sheet explains that “ the
liabilities under life policies have been reduced during
the year by £14,160, leaving a total liability of £128,160
on all policies, including bonus additions.” Apparently
that means, not that £128,160 is the present value of the
liabilities, but that that is the face value of all outstand-
ing policies. In that case the co. could pay off all its debts,
still have its own capital and £48,950 to spare, and
then pocket as profit all future premiums, less whatever
small amount may be taken from them and returned by
way of bonus additions to policies. This is assuming that
by “ total liability on all policies ” is meant what will have
to be ultimately paid ; if it means present value of liabili-
ties which have not yet matured, the assurance fund
would still show a surplus of £55,647. In the mutual
office that amount would be available for distribution
among policy-holders ; in this purely Private Enterprise
concern, the shareholders get it. The expenses of
management amount to 52 per cent, of the premium in-
come. But that is not the expense of doing the policy-
holders’ business; it is very largely the expense of ad-
ministering the large surplus which the shareholders
maintain in the business, but which they are making a
brave struggle to reduce by drawing this 25 per cent,
dividend once a year.

This is another insurance co. which apparently pays too
big a dividend to please the insurer, who naturally looks
for a big lay-by to protect him in the case of something
serious happening. Yet, in eight years, the reserves have
been trebled ; and, as the risks have been increased but
little meantime, the co.’s present position is much stronger
than it was, and it is far better worthy of confidence. Its
paid capital is £50,181; on that and the reserves the
year’s profits were at the very fine rate of just 19 per
cent., and, if it could keep that up, something ought to
happen soon to insurance rates. But, as a matter of fact,
this co. (in common with most others, until the last few
months) has been having a remarkable run of luck lately.
This shows it to some extent:—
Net Ex- Expense Loss
Premiums, penses. Losses. Rate. Rate.
1900 £82,217 £19,224 £46,406 23'4 p.c. 56‘4 p.c.
1904 83,216 20,456 39,500 24'6 „ 47'4 „
1905 82,276 22,544 36,710 27 ’4 „ 44'6 „
1906 83,535 23,589 36,782 28’2 „ 44'0 „
This co. i 6 re-insuring rather more freely than it formerly
did ; and that no doubt accounts for some of the fall in
the loss rate, and it may also account for a little of the
very marked growth in the expense rate. As to the
latter, part of the trouble with the office whose business
isn’t growing is that either its expenses must grow or
efficiency must suffer. Here, though net premiums have
hardly increased at all in six years, cost of manage-
ment and charges and expenses at head - office
and branches have risen from £19,080 to £23,589.
As against that there has possibly been an improvement
in selection ; but the downward course of the loss rate
hasn’t been quite so regular as the figures above would
suggest; and, anyhow* these loss-rate figures are subject
to = a great manv outside influences. Generali}' speaking,
the last two or three years it has paid people to keep
their premises from being burned down. The co. s in-
vestments returned £5165 last year; this was at the rate
of a rather poor £3 6s. per cent, upon the average hold-
ing. Of the investments £12,351 is real estate, taken in
at book value ; another £69,556 is on fixed deposit, and
£37,332 is in public stocks, while only £11,900 is on mort-
gage. The money, therefore, seems to be handled rather
with a view to safety than to secure a high return ; and
that is the right policy with insurance funds.

It looks as though this co. were half inclined to turn over
a new leaf, and, modifying its dividends, put by a bit
more for the better protection, not only of its insurers,
but of its shareholders as well. It had a straight
enough tip, anyway, that its only hope lay in doing some-
thing of the sort; for its premium income was steadily
falling. Even now there is no recovery in that respect,
probably because insurers are not satisfied that the re-
form is lasting ; and it doesn’t amount to much of a
reform, anyhow—the lay-by is only just over one-seventh
of the profits ; and that is a wretched proportion in an
insurance concern, which is liable to be struck by some-
thing extra heavy at any time, and can’t (epecially when
it is small) be sure of any one year’s premiums covering
that year’s losses. A comparison of recent results shows
this :
1900. 1905. 1906.
Premium income .. .. £11,324 £9,443 £9,124
Losses .. 6,387 4,429 3,829
Expenses 2,864 2,973 3,103
Losses p.c. of premiums .. 56'4 46’9 41 "9
Expenses p.c. of premiums .. 25 - 4 31 - 5 34"0
The underwriting profit in 1900 was thus 18’2 per cent,
of the premiums, and last year 24'1 per cent.—which
seems to show that it has paid very well to increase the
expenses, and, apparently, to more carefully select the
risks. All the same, that policy can be carried too far.
You can go on reducing the loss rate till you get it to
nil by the simple process of selecting your risks
till you accept nothing at all. The only' drawback
about that system is that, having no income,
your profits are also nil. There is some doubt,
however, whether this co.’s expense rate has mounted up
quite so fast as the figures show. It adopts the unintel-
ligible plan of lumping together commissions and re-
turned premiums ; and as there has been a slight increase
in this item, a small part of the jump in the expense rate
may be due to more premiums being returned. The co.’s
funds are principally on deposit (£15,534), public stocks
(£13,954), and real estate (£9684). The stocks are in at
cost; the estate at book value. On the mean holding of
the investments for the year the interest earnings were
at the rate of £3 12s. 6d. per cent. —about low enough to
suggest that the stocks and estate are, at any rate, not
undervalued.

The reserves are—lnsurance, £42,915 ; equalisation of
dividend, £10,699; and undivided profit, £495, which
passes to the equalisation of dividend fund. Apparently
depreciation is charged direct to profit and loss ; but no
profit and loss account is published, and the amount of
the standing charge probably wouldn’t be disclosed even
if one were published. The size of this charge upon gross
profits cannot therefore be even guessed at. If, however,
it has been a uniform percentage there has been a fine
spring in profits—though those of three years or so ago
were unusually low on account of the drought on the
North Coast. Further details

Five years ago the capital and reserves showed earnings at
the rate of £6 18s. per cent. ; now they are at the rate of
£7 45., including the unexplained additions to insurance
fund. These additions are, however, only contingent
profit; they are set apart to cover a risk which has partly
expired—the risk begins with the launching or purchase
of every boat. And as against the higher earnings shown
now there are greater risks : ship property has increased
£106,556 in five years, the money being provided partly
through increase of capital, but more through the
sale of freeholds (mostly taken over by the State) to the ex-
tent of £57,308. And, as before said, it is impossible to tell
how vigorously or otherwise this ship property, which is
open to heavier depreciation and danger, is being written
down out of revenue. When the co. stops to take breath,
and ceases buying up old lines and new vessels, some idea
of what it is writing off may be got; at present all that
can be said is that it appears to be easily earning its 8
per cent., and that its reserves could wipe out more than
one-fourth of the paper value of the fleet and still leave
capital intact.

The Newcastle and Hunter River Steam-
ship Co. (N.S.W.): —
Net To Re- Re-
Profit. Dividends. serves, serves.
Dec., 1904 .. £3092 6 p.c. = £3OOO £92 £8793
June, 1905 .. 2960 6 „ „ 3000 8753
Dec., 1905 .. 3385 6 „ „ 3000 385 9138
June, 1906 .. 3053 6 „ „ 3000 53 9191
This co., which has been in existence for 14J years,
doesn’t give its 400 shareholders any too much information
about its proceedings. It shows just about enough profit
each half-year to pay its 6 per cent. Yet that did not
prevent a shareholder, with apparently a good deal of
inside information, suggesting at a recent meeting that
the dividend should be increased. And the directors did
not point to the figures and say the thing was impossible ;
spokesman John See said “the steamers were written
down with the object of making provision for the pur-
chase of a new steamer. There was no increment in
steamers, and they must be replaced out of earnings.”
Which reply suggests that the management is wise and
prudent. There is no increment in railway stations
either, yet wasn’t it spokesman John See who was
Premier when the Owe’Sullivan conceived the idea of
building a vast new railway station, not out of earnings
but out of the same old loan moneys that the previous-
station had been built out of ? What provision is being
made in this way by the N. and H.R. Co. it is not easy
to say; but some idea can be formed. Take this com-
parison :
Dec., June, Dec., June,
1904. 1905. 1905. 1906.
Ship property .. £41.300 £40,050 £39,080 £40,595
Freeholds, &c. .. 7,175 6,390 5,855 5,308
Plant.. .. .. 2,730 2,998 2,881 3,069
Liquid assets .. 66,157 68,321 70,265 67,551
Debts to public .. 5,568 6,005 5,943 4,332
Deduct debts to public from liquid assets, and it will be
found that the latter increased from £60,589 to £64,322 in
1905—a growth of £3733. So, in addition to the amount
spent on repairs and on plant, £3733 apparently represents
the earnings in that year after paying the 6 per cent.
dividend—the earnings, that is, available tor writing off ;
and £3733 is equal to 8 per cent, on the total book value
of ships, freeholds, etc. The experience of the past half-
year cannot be followed so closely, because there has been
an increase in the book value of ship property, due pro-
bably to payments on account of a new purchase; but
there is no reason to suspect that the same very prudent
policy is not being followed. The liquid assets, repre-
senting over 60 per cent, of the capital, are nearly all on
bank deposit.

The co. maintains a paper item among its assets—
“ Parliamentary rights and concessions, £ls,ooo—dis-
regarded in the foregoing. The position of the reserves
is as follows :
Plant renewal account £15,500
Waterloo lease reserve account .. .. 5,500
Undivided profit 1,835
Total nominal reserves .. £22,835
Less paper asset 15,000
Balance £7,835
More or less, this balance is not genuine reserve either;
it is mostly a provision for the renewal of works which so
far have been kept in repair out of revenue. The whole
undertaking is in the balance-sheet at original cost; the
reserve accounts are equivalent, then, to an allowance
for depreciation. The one advantage about this system
is that it purports to set out the total amount written off
or provided for depreciation, although that can be done
just as effectively without inflating balance-sheet figures,
and by a plan which isn’t liable to give wrong impressions
as to the nature of the reserved accounts. But, so far,
no renewal has been paid for out of this account; if
renewals have been necessary (and presumably they have
been, for the co. is 16 years old), they have been paid for
out of revenue and classed as repairs. This suggests that
the reserves are better than they might look ; also, that the
stated profits(since they are already probably providing for
some renewals) are more genuine than they would be if no
such depreciation charges were coming out of profit and
loss. On the other hand, gross revenue isn’t what it was.
With assets shown at £133,205 in the first half of 1902,
£12,678 was earned ; last half-year £142,263 of assets
earned only £12,455 gross revenue. The co. is not likely
to pay more than 10 per cent. ; practically it is limited
to that by its Act. (It can pay more, but the City Coun-
cil must share equally in the extra.) But if it can make
concessions and so extend its business, it might, by-and-
bye, be able to find a very profitable investment for the
£75,000 of its uncalled or unissued capital; and these con-
cessions, combined with electrical competition, probably
account for the reduced earnings shown.

Brisbane Gas Co.

Profits. Dividend Tax. serves, serves.

Dec., 1903 .. 11,762 ll£ „ „ 8452 3310 26,194
June, 1904 .. 11,042 Ilf ~ „ 8452 - 2590 28,784
Dec., 1904 .. 11,634 Ilf „ ~ 8452 3182 31,966
June, 1905 .. 10,309 Ilf „ „ 8452 1857 33,823
£20,000 of Reserves transferred to Capital 13,823
Dec., 1905 .. 10,898 10 p.c. = 8400 2498 16,321
June, 1906 .. 10,768 10 „ „ 8400 2368 18,689
Twelve months ago £20,000 of reserves were transferred
to capital ; wherefore the dividend, represented as
10 per cent., is really £ll Bs. 6d. per cent, on the
paid capital. So the wind is tempered to the eye of
the shorn lamb the lamb in this case being the
man who buys the gas and provides the dividend —
even though the same old blast strikes the animal’s
pocket. Besides the reserves shown above there is an
item “Suspense account for dividend reserve, £5100”;
this is apparently the balance of the profit made on a
land transaction not yet completed. Depreciation
(amount unknown) is provided for out of revenue;
and, in addition, £378, which is about the same
as usual, was written off during the half-year
to provide for renewal of holders and retorts. At
June, 1903, after the sale of the land, the fixed invest-
ment (land, plant, etc.) was £149,000; it now amounts to
£153,978 ; and it looks as though a good deal more than
£4978 had been spent on extensions, new plant, etc.,
during the past three years. Whatever more (if any) has
been spent has been provided out of revenue, and serves
as something equal to a charge for wear and tear on the
undertaking. In any case, however, whether sufficient
has been provided to cover depreciation or not, the assets
have appreciated considerably in earning power ; in the
first half of 1903 the assets showed gross revenue at the
rate of £2llls. 6d. per cent, per annum ; now the gross
return is almost exactly 22 per cent.; and, so far as
shareholders are concerned, it doesn’t matter so very
much whether the improvement has come through
the hard writing down of assets or through a Labor-
governed Brisbane being now able to buy more gas—
it doesn’t matter, at least, provided the improve-
ment is permanent. The co. owes very little,
and is strengthening its financial position steadily.
Three years ago it had £31,373 of liquid assets in excess
of liabilities ; now it has £37,887. And, meantime, as
shown above, £4978 has also been put into plant, in addi-
tion to anything that may have been charged direct to
revenue. There are several ways in which a gas co. may
hide away its profits, and this payment for works out of
revenue is the easiest of them. There is hardly one co.
in Australia besides the Metropolitan (Melbourne) which
shows what it spends in that way; yet, unless those
figures are available, or can be estimated in some way,
the public has no means of telling whether it is paying a
fair price for its lighting or not.

Times seem to be too good for this co. to make very big
profits ; that is to say, there is a great deal of money
about which the Freetrade crowd won’t allow the owners
to find profitable use for, and the competition to lend it
is bad for the Mont de Piete. Also it affects the .co.
in other ways. In June, 1903, the advances totalled
£87,243; they' have now shrunk to £82,448. In
the early half of 1903 the gross interest earnings were at
the rate of £23 7s. Cd. percent, per annum on the total of
the advances ; now, after allowing for interest earned by
fixed deposits, they 7 are about £2l 4s. 6d. per cent. There
are, however, risks in the business which the foregoing
table doesn’t suggest; there is the moth that eats and
the rust that tarnishes, wherefore, in 1899-1900,, £7778
had to be written off. Among the reserves at present is
a contingency 7 fund (£3102), which is a special provision
for such shrinkages in value ; and, as a reserve, this is
therefore open to suspicion. In consequence of the re-
duced demand for money', the co. has paid off most of its
own fixed deposits ; three years ago they stoodat £11,215,
now they 7 stand at £5026 ; and the bank debit of £B5O has
been changed to a credit of £6646., Meantime, working
expenses have moved up from £3875 to £4398. It is still
a highly profitable business, nevertheless, yielding £8 15s.
2d. per cent.,per annum: on capital and reserves ; but,
compared with the “hard times” of a few years back,
some of the gilt is off it.

Victorian Butter Factories Co-operative
Co., Ltd. (Melbourne)
Dividend. Bonus Written Deficit
(10 p.c.) to clients, off. (Prelim.
Aug., Profit. Exes.)
1900 .. £163
April, Reserves.
1901 .. £914 £149 602
1902 .. 1603 164 £lOO6 1035
1903 .. 2513 191 1612 1745
1904 .. 3072 201 2593 £2O 2003
1905 .. 2882 205 2483 20 2177
190 G .. 4278 212 1947 571 3725
This is very remarkable growth. The co. started in 1900
with a Board comprising a managing director in Mel-
bourne, a butter-man of Tungamah, another of Kongwak,
and an accountant of Benalla. At first it only' went in
for co-operative marketing, the other sort of butter-mar-
keting being then under dark suspicion. Presently more
butter factories joined in, and in 1903 30 per cent, of the
commission paid by them was returned as bonus. In 1904
the commission itself was reduced h.v 1 per cent.; and a
butter-box factory' and a freezing works were secured ata
cost of £18,363. During the past year these works showed a
profit of £1551, orB| per cent, on the capital cost. This
is put forward as “ net profit; ” hut as the works are in
the balance-sheet at cost, it allows nothing for deprecia-
tion, and that is a big' item in such works—freezing works
especially 7. It isn’t merely (hat the thing wears out ;it
becomes obsolete, and has to be scrapped or one’s com-
petitor gets the business. The “ net profit” isn’t there-
fore what it looks. With the purchase of these works and
the rapid extension of business, debts have grown very
fast. In 1903 they amounted to £3445, and there was a
hank credit against that of £1957. Now they 7 amount to
£33.280, and there is no credit balance at all. There
are £41,284 of assets, most of them very perish-
able, many of them apparently involving very 7 con-
siderable risks (apart from foreign bills, “ sundry debtors”
amount to £16,483) ; and of that £41,284, the co.
..owns only £5845. It has to stand the risk of things
going wrong, not only with its own money, hut with six
times as much of the public’s money. Under these cir-
cumstances, a rapid accumulation of reserves is most
desirable ; the 10 per cent. div. meantime ought to be
cut down and something less extravagant in the way of
bonuses should be declared. If it doesn’t do these things,
the co. stands a very serious chance of presently finding
itself tied in a hard financial knot, which will mean sacri-
fice. And there are always plenty of people waiting to
help at the sacrifice of co-operative enterprises.

Though it has no bright prospects, Boulder
Main Reef (W.A.) is not dead yet. Co. has £12,000 in
cash and calls to go on with, and it is proposed to expend
£6OOO in renovating the plant, and the other £6OOO in
further testing the property. According to the new mana-
ger, Wauchope, there are 20,000 tons of Bdwt. ore in sight,
and it is expected that this will more than pay expenses
when the milling plant is overhauled. Other low-grade
ore will probably be found in the upper levels—stuff that
was passed over in the early days. The tailings' were sold
last August for £6OOO ; they proved worthless, and were
abandoned. But the sale gave new hope to the eo.

That calling-up of more capital in Hannan’s
Star (W.A.) doesn’t seem any better the more it is looked
into. The co., which has been in existence since 1895 and
lias never returned its shareholders a farthing, was
reconstructed in August last, when a 2s. assessment was
'made on the 210,165 shares issued ; and it was then said
that there were £3500 liquid assets in hand taken over
from the old co. As very little work has been done since,
it is absolutely impossible that the £24,500 working capi-
tal then provided could have been dissipated; and the
two calls of Is. each (due July 31 and August 31) look
very much as if they were made with the object of
squeezing out weak holders.
* * • *
Hidden Secret, Kalgoorlie, has, after spending
a lot of money, got on to a pay shoot 70ft. south at the
300 ft. level. The ore ranges from a few inches up to 2ft.
wide ; values vary from 7£dwt. to 50z., and average
32dwt. Owing to this development the ground to the
south has been re-pegged, and a good deal of activity is
being displayed. There is practically no pay mine from
this point to the Hainault, but this stretch of country
has been badly prospected, and may yet produce much
gold if capital is forthcoming to give it a thorough test.
Golden Pole, Davyhurst (W.A.), suspends
development work till the air-compressor plant is en-
larged. Yet, though this helps the finances, the long
talked-of div. still refuses to arrive, and it is a marvel
that the mine maintains its share value of £80,(100. On
February 1 ore reserves were reckoned as 9500 tons
blocked out, since when 7000 tons have been treated, and
the balance remaining above the 400 ft. level is very small.
Underfoot, below the fourth level, about 4000 tons are
guessed to exist. At this level, however, the gold shoot
is very flat, and dips to the north, and is being followed
by an inclined winze. An air winch is to be installed to
haul the ore up this winze, so this will mean heavy
working costs. Under the circumstances, the outlook of
the co. is very dreary.
» • * *
East Fingall, Day Dawn (W.A.), has hired the
Creme d’ Or mill on the adjoining lease, and started
crushing. Very gaudy reports are still published about
this very doubtful property. In a winze below the second
level the reef was reported to be sft. wide and worth
22dwt. At 413 ft. the shaft cut a reef “3ft. wide and
worth oldwt.” At the 500 ft. level values up to £27
a ton were recorded. In spite of all these fancy reports
the ss. shares continue to he quoted at 3s. 9d. or there-
abouts, and local residents still don’t rush them. The
hiring of the battery ought to soon set many doubts at
rest. • With a battery available, the co. surely won’t keep
£27 stone longer underground than is absolutely neces-
sary.
Cosmo Newbury Hills (W.A.), which Carr
Boyd has beep booming for months, has, been visited t by
Oarr Bovd, prospector,Cunningham and Gapt. Wearne on
behalf of a Kalgoorlie syndicate. The report is “ No good.”
The country is intersected by numerous leaders, some
of which run to ounces in patches, but they are too small
to be any use to a co. The syndicate, which consisted of
10 shares of £lO each, has been dissolved, as Cunning-
ham and Wearne were quite satisfied that prospects did
not warrant any expenditure of capital. A fresh syndi-
cate has now been formed to inspect another of Carr
Boyd’s fields, and Captain Wearne is to again go out.
The Narlarla Hills (W.A.) bubble has quite
burst. The £lO shares were dealt in up to £74, so some-
body, no doubt in Adelaide, must have scored con-
siderably. The proposal of the Adelaide syndicate
responsible for this wild cat, as well as for Grant’s Nor’-
West, to sink and test the alleged lodes • is probably a
piece of bluff. There is practically'nothing to test, and
the find, such as it was, is by no means new.
Collie Proprietary coal (W. A.), taken over from
the Zeb. Lane crowd in February last by electrician R. C.
Young, has now a certificated manager—Leitch, the man
who lost an arm in the Stanford Merythyr (N.S.W.) ex-
plosion ; and the concern will proceed to put up another
fight, under more favorable conditions than it has suffered
from lately'.
Bananaland badly wants a provision in its new
Mining Bill to permit mining on private property ; hut
it is feared Minister Blair’s measure will not give it. Not
only are large areas, supposed to be rich in minerals, held
as freehold, but the old survey marks have disappeared in
many places, while in others they' are erroneous, so that
the prospector is sometimes fired out by an irate owner—
who may be only a sham owner, despite his obliterating
fury—after he has opened up a show. This snag has been
struck against at Cloncurry; • so the Government has
arranged for a new survey there. But a new survey'
won’t get rid of the unworked freehold curse.
* * * *
Present position, as far as the Stock Exchange
is concerned, of most Queensland copper propositions is
as interesting to the jobber as it is dangerous to the
public. From O.K. in the north right through the State
to Mt. Morgan, Peak Downs, Glassford Creek, Mt. Hector,
and Mt. Cannindah, the hulk of the shares in every'
instance are in few hands. The few, up till lately, have
been sitting tight, and this position, in the hands of a
good operator and a willing public, ; means disaster
to the public. The willing public is now coming
in. Brisbane, usually morose, takes a hand, prin-
cipally in Central Queensland. Sydney' is likewise
putting some money into the same parts. Mel-
bourne is swallowing Cloncurry' almost holus-bolus.
One recent float was in 04,000 shares of 10s. each ; 30,000
paids went to vendors, 12,000 to contributors on the
latter paying ss. per share on 12,000 other shares, and
10,000 are in reserve. The prospectus said : “ The whole
of the contributing shares have been applied for previous
to the publication and issue of this prospectus, which is
published for public information only, and no person is
invjted to subscribe.” But the chances are that the
public will want to come into the co. that it wasn’t in-
vited to enter, the shares won’t be available, prices will
be forced up (and the public won’t see why'), and then no
doubt the vendors will be induced to part with a few of
their 30,000.
* * * *
The new co. which owns Peak Downs (Q.) has
started unwatering, and is hoping to find some high-
grade precipitate. Also, it is hoping that it will meet ore
straight away when it has the working's dry. Further,
it is lipping that copper will keep up until the blast
furnace is going.' So it is going ahead building its blast
furnace, though what it is going to put through the blast
furnace is not known, except it be the poor ore .already
at the surface; and it is doubtful Whether that will pay' to
smelt on its own. Still, with nothing done, Peak Downs
shares are forced tp £l.; It is .absurd. There is trouble
in store for someone ; for if the opinion of the man who
last saw the peak Downs workings may be relied upon, it
will take more money than ‘the present co.'has to effec-
tively locate the ore body 7, without opening it up and
equipping the property with plant. . That opinion may
be right or wrong ; The Bulletin merely says that
people who buy shares at present figures are, upon the
data available, taking ludicrous risks.
* * * *
An old copper miner sends this :—“ Peak Downs
copper mine is on the contact of the schist and granite
and, unfortunately, the underlay seems to be in the
direction of the hard granite, judging by' the small
amount of development that has been done. Previous
managers seem to have seen what that meant, and tried
to locate another shoot of ore longitudinally by driving
along the footwall. The ore body became yellow sul-
phides, somewhat irregular and narrow, below 180 ft. It
did not pay to handle this in the early days, but the rich
carbonates and oxides in the shallow ground paid hand-
somely'. Some of the copper was doubtless left in the old
furnace bottoms and slag dump ; it would he curious if
such were not the case, for there is always more or less
copper in these products. I remember that a bottle of
amalgam was found on a heap of old battery tailings near
the copper mine (for it is surrounded by old alluvial
workings and quartz reefs), but the mysterious ‘ assays
that have yet to come for precious metals ’ out of old
tumble-down furnace bottoms and slag dumps ( vide
official report) is too much for my digestion.”
* * * *
By way of counterblast, this comes from G. A.
Collin, of Brisbane, who had much to do with giving the
old mine another trial: —“Bulletin of July 19 harps
again on the effect of a probable slump in the copper
market. As the co. has a fine coal property a few miles
off, and actually gets coal delivered at 11s. per ton, and
pays for freight to Rockhampton only 17s. per ton for all
goods, it is evident the mine can be worked as cheaply as
any, and has less to fear a slump in the copper market
than most. Mr. Coates, the manager, writes in his last
report that bailing operations are in full swing, and
water is shifted at the rate of 24,000 gallons per hour. So
if ‘mournful Brisbane ’ arrives too late it won’t he Bris-
bane’s fault. As for the lode, Rand, Government geolo-
gist, stated in his report to Government in 1599 that it is
a true fissure lode ; and at the deepest level (240 ft.) it
showed a width of Bft. to 12ft., assaying from 6 to 12 per
cent., and going strong under foot ; and that is good
enough for most uersons.”
* * * *
“M.C.” : Mt. Morgan (Q.) reckons on treating
10,000 tons of copper ore per month, and shareholders
are beginning to ask what extra divs. they are likely' to
get. The copper development is discounted by the fall-
ing off in the grade of oxidised ore, by the building of
new plant, and repayment of £75,000 of debt, and the
creation of a necessary' reserve. The dividend lately paid
left little to spare, otherwise co. would not have had to
borrow. If the yearly output of copper is calculated on
a 3 per cent, basis from 120,000 tons of ore, it would
amount to 3600 tons, worth at £6O per ton £216,000,
enough to pay 3s. per annum in dividends and leave
£66,000 to cover depreciation and refining charges, which
are heavy items. Gold contents per ton of copper are
exceedingly high, and it is thought the gold value of
nearly £5O, if it can be maintained, will cover mining,
smelting and converting charges. Future depends upon
whether value of gold and copper extraction can be
maintained, and on the value of that oxidised ore which
has stood to the mine for so many years. A long future
seems assured ; but increased dividends will have to be
waited for, and it is easy to be too sanguine as to the
amount of ultimate increase. A decent reserve fund
and the payment of debts should absorb a year’s profit
out of copper.
Mt. Molloy (N.Q.) is a compact copper-mine, in
a hollow among the hills. Till more development work
is done no one can say whether the show will last. No.
4 level is, however, looking well, with about 6ft. of good
ore. Mine has been employing over 100 men. As usual,
there’s a big lump of shares; so each miner has to pro-
vide profit for over 1000 shares !
* # « *
The Etheridge (N.Q.) Railway Bill, which is
to validate the agreement entered into by the Queensland
Railways Commissioner, is being pushed along by Kid-
ston’s Government. First reading went through last
week. Bill gives the constructing co.—the Etheridge
Railway and Mines, Ltd. —the power, with the consent of
the Governor-in-Oouncii, to assign or transfer all
or any of its rights and powers, and . makes
provision for keeping the y'ellow laborer off
the job ; also for payment of current rates of
wages. Although the money for construction has
not loomed up yet, there are indications that with
the passing- of the validating Act, it will be available.
Certainly those wh.o have been left to nurse the Chfilagoe
infant deserve a chance to make a recovery, and it would
be wretchedly bad luck if the Etheridge, on which they
now rely, turned out to be a twin youngster.
* . * » *
After all,, it has fallen to the Queensland
Government itself to take out of the Dawson leases the
200 tons of coal required for the Admiralty test. Premier
Kidstoh lias earned the disfavor of about half-a-dozen
Rockhampton shareholders in the Dawson Co. for what
they term “confiscating their property.”, For two or
three years the co. has been monkeying with the ground,
and on .two occasions Kidston warned them that
unless they got to work things would hap-
pen. Co. even allowed its licenses to run out,
and the alleged act of confiscation was the Government’s
refusal to grant the co. a lease of 7000 acres of the Daw-
son Valley. If that modest request were granted, this
was promised;—(A co. in 25,000 £1 shares would be
formed, 4000 shares to go to the Dawson Co., 4000 to the
Dunstan Co., 8000 to the public, 4000 to the under-
writer (who in this case was G. C. Willcocks), 5000
reserved. What Kidston wanted was 200 tons of
coal, not a monopolistic company ; and he decided
that the Government should sail in and get it, for too
much time and State money has already been spent in
vainly encouraging the Dawson crowd to do something
practical. Rankin, the managing director of the Queens-
land Collieries, has been appointed to carry out the
work ; and there appears no reason why it should not he
carried out effectively, and promptly.
* * * *
Among the speculators who have put their
money into the nest of resurrected shows south of the
main Broken Hill line of lode there seem to be some who
implicitly believe in the miraculous. They apparently
expect .a lode to be cut that will be twin-sister, to the
bonanza worked by the B.H. Prop. Nothing has yet been
met to justify su,ch faith. In all the shows that have
sprung up on the broad White Leads plain, there is not a
single indication of anything greater than the ordinary
small vein long familiar to those' connected with “out-
side shows.” The Rising Sun hen has laid the latest egg,,
and is cackling tremendously'. But it all boils down to
some veins of galena, which were frequently met for the
whole length of the western crosscut; and at 30ft. from
the shaft they happened to bunch and be of better quality.
The lode, what there may he of it, will be found further
west.
* » * *
The Junction, Broken Hill, is putting down its
main shaft (Browne’s) another 200 ft..—and waiting for
something to turn up. One of the things waited for is
the repair of the Junction North shaft, a work not-yet even
begun. The completion of this work will herald the un-
watering: of the lower level of the Junction North, and
incidentally the draining of the winze below the Junc-
tion’s 775 ft. level, which will allow the Junction to get
at a rich shoot of ore, cut just before the outbreak of the
fire. Meantime, if the directors wake up sufficiently,
some of the ancient machinery on the. mine iqay be over-
hauled/arid a condenser put in. The overhauling and
also the condenser are badly needed.
The De Bavay zinc process has had a good trial
run at the B.H. North mine. The concentrate assayed 45£
percent, zine, but it was obtained from one unit of the
plant only (seven tables), and the question of how the
process is going to act on a large scale has yet to be
answered. The material for treatment over the De
Bavay tables requires to be prepared as carefully as
baby’s food, which means high working expenses. The
plant has been idle since the experimental run. But
Melbourne has been very busy gambling in the Process
shares.
The tin shows about Euriowie and Waukeroo
(far west N.S.W.) ought to get a move on now if ever.
A tin expert from Tasmania (R. Purdue, manager of
Federation and Dalrvmpie) has made an inspection, and
pronounced the fields good. The tin. outcrops, which
have hitherto been regarded with a doubtful eye as mere
surface veins and pockets, are to make into one big lode
at depth ; and the expert has talked learnedly to that
effect, as experts did 16 years ago 1 o J. S. Reid and others,
and induced them to put in some tens of thousands,
which they dropped. There have been spasmodic efforts
to work the deposits since then ; hut the micaceous
character of the gangue has stood in the way of a satis-
factory recovery. A good deal of alluvial tin has lately
been found near Yanco Glen, on the west side of the
Tarrawingee railway line.

Mount Boppy Blocks (N.S.W.), lately visited
by the Sydney directors and G. H. iSlakemore, wisely
decided to abandon all work in present shaft and sink a
new one to water level to catch the underlay on a huge
gossan lode. Lode in old shaft carries small values in
gold, silver, and lead ; but until something more than
80ft. is touched (the present depth of the shaft) there will
not be enough to build a prediction upon.
* * * *
“Thurla”: On July 30 the miners at the
Conrad Stannite mines, Howell (N.S.W.), struck against
the introduction of the Gympie or single-hand system of
working. Manager Parker said both systems, double and
single hand, would be used ; but the miners suspected
that it was the thin end of the wedge, and came out—loo
of them, counting in truckers and timber men. Some of
the smelters and other surface hands also turned out.
The mill hands decided not to quit work ; still individuals
have been falling out, and, anyhow, the mill can’t
keep going without? the mine. The objection miners have
to the single-hand system in connection with the Conrad
is that it would he very dangerous to work alone in the
face, owing to the treacherous nature of the walls and the
lode. They allege that the stope south of the plat at 250
feet, where it was proposed to introduce the system, is
not safe for such work, as in about 800 ft. of sloping
there are only two man-ways, and often the men cannot
use these, but have to go by the passes that may be
empty. The Inspector of Mines has been inspecting the
mine ; and something may come of that.
* * * *
Another factor in reducing the mining costs,
especially in large mines, is the general use of “Little
Wonder” Hand Hammer and “One Man” Compressed
Air Drills. Broken Hill mines use them extensively. The
managers favor these drills for their great strength and
simplicity having only one working part; also for
economy in air consumption as they require only about
25ft. free air per minute. Miners prefer them, as work is
made much easier; for tributors, “Wonder” drills are
the means of bringing in larger returns. Messrs. W. and
J. Lempriere, of Melbourne and Sydney, have de-
scriptive catalogues for those interested, and attention
is drawn to an exceedingly simple and ingenious patent
water attachment for allaying dust.*

The New,Havillah Gold Mining Company has
just installed a high lift pump to make all necessary' pro-
vision against any increase in the' volume of water now
that they are opening up large areas of wash dirt. This
installation is in addition to the large three’ throw pump
which has given such high efficiency' and, such constant
service since its inception. The new pdmp is of the high
lift turbine type, electrically driven, and. is capable of
lifting 2,010,000 gallons of water in 24 hours against a
total head of 350 feet. The -mechanical efficiency' of the
pump is guaranteed at 71'5 percent, and all repairs and
replacements apart from undue wear are likewise
guaranteed for twelve months. The pump has four
runners and four vanes made from best bronze and the
shaft is attached by means of a flexible coupling to a
Ganz three phase motor 440 volts pressure, 60 cycles
capable of giving 207 li.p. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have executed this coil-
tract. This Company lias had the manufacture of most
of the big volume deep alluvial lead pumping schemes in
Victoria and has met in each with unqualified success.* [a]

Aboriginalities.
“ Walfre ” sends a faint buzz from the far
interior of N.S.W. :
There is a peculiar state of affairs out West just now.
Although there has been a very fair amount of cold
weather this winter there are still a noticeable number
of blowflies about. None of the oldest inhabitants (there
are 12 oldest inhabitants here, and each one is older than
the others) can remember being troubled by flies after
heavy frosts. The most feasible explanation is that,
owing to the number of poisoned rabbits lying around,
flies have increased far beyond the average. ' It is pretty
safe to predict that next summer will be about the worst
on record, and that the West will be almost a solid ram-
part of fly' and buzz-z-z-z-z-z.

“ Brisbane ” : “ Elmore ” need not go any
further than Australia for an answer to his ques-
tion as to musical birds of prey. The magpie
fills the bill. During the late drought I have
often seen these birds picking out the eyes of
sheep that were still alive but too weak to stand.
It was also common to see two or three crows and
a couple of magpies feasting off a newly-deceased
carcase. And a magpie’s carol is song.
“ Whana ” : The Maoris have peculiar
methods of determining distances, not one in a
hundred being
able to tell you
the mileage from
his pah to the
next pah or town-
ship. Rode from
Napier to Wairoa
once. Was ap-
proaching a pah
and met an old
Maori. Asked
him, “How far
the next place ? ”
“ How far Nu-
haka'! ” said he,
and stood looking
along i..u road
fully two minutes.
Finally he sat
down. “ How far
Nuhaka,” he re-
peated as soon as
he was quite com-
fortable. “I think
good horse fifteen
miles; bad horse
—aw —long time.”
Seeing a younger
native a few yards
away, I put the
same question.
He looked my
horse up and
down, under and
over, for a second or so and then answered, “I
think two hour that horse.” I did the journey just
under two hours, and found the distance was
about 16 miles. The Maori has a fixed belief that
a place may be further off with one horse than
with another.
* * * *
Geraldton, in Queensland, claims that it
can easily beat the rest of Australia in wetness.
In 1902, when pretty well all the continent was
wilting, it had 191 inches, and the year before
that it had 202 inches —17ft. all but a couple of
inches; and they probably fell, but slopped over
when the gauge was more tired than usual. From
January to March the Geraldton people can
reckon upon having 4yds. of rain ; when there is
only 3yds. it is a drought, and a row awaits the
local joss, who, by the way, is the most respect-
able-looking joss in Queensland. There are never
more than 29 wet days in February ; but the fall
averages just under an inch and a half whether it
is a wet day or not. Yet they don’t, as a rule,
have to swim around Geraldton; half-an-hour
after the rain has stopped the surface is reason-
ably dry, which probably accounts for the fact
that if it doesn’t rain for three weeks there is
grief among the bananas (Geraldton is the place
where the bananas come from), while if it doesn’t
rain for six weeks there is positive disaster.

“ Fraulein ” : Amusing that anyone should
think it odd to catch snakes in rabbi t-traps.
That’s the way we mostly kill them here. Towards
the end of the summer, when water is scarce, tiger
snakes get about the house, living for days be-
neath the floors. When one has been seen a few
times going under the house, a saucer of milk and
a rabbit trap are set for his snakeship. The trap
is set with the opening of the jaws along, not
across, the path of the snake. Result —a snake
securely caught within twenty-four hours. Wells
here are good snake-traps. After a sultry night,
two or three are down most of the wells in the
district. Once down, they can’t get up, though
they live for days in the water, and can live for
half a day at least under water. I’ve kept one
under myself—l mean I’ve kept one under the
water. (Cruel woman !)
The Council-General of New Caledonia has,
after a heated discussion, decided that lepers shall
not be isolated. The voting was very close —eight
against isolation, seven for. The Governor, to his
credit, voted with the minority. It seems
almost incredible that such a law needed any
discussion whatever, but strange things
happen in Fresh Caledonia. The fact
is there are lepers amongst the inhabi- -
tants of Noumea, but nobody seems to
worry about them. Leprosy was
brought into New Caledonia, some
20 years ago, by a Chinaman, and
since then the disease has spread
like wildfire. There must be at
least suo white and 2000 kanaka
lepers to-day. The Government
has done its foolish best to give
the pest a fair chance. Some
years back, it formed a leper-
station on Belep Island, the
Belep natives being removed to
the mainland. The expense
proving too great, the lepers
were taken off the island and
sent back to their various
tribes on the mainland, and
the Belep “boys” went home
again to their tainted isle.
As a result, the mainland boys
are spreading the disease among
their people, and the Belep boys
are catching it fast. It seems probable that,
unless some drastic measures are adopted,
New‘Caledonia will, in another 20 years, become
one vast lazarette. The tainted Kanaka mixes
with his friends, eats with them and sleeps with
them. The friends don’t object, so why should
the leper object ?
“ Werrimee ” : Re wild passion-fruit.
“Ossian” (B. 26/7/’O6) says: “Wild passion-
fruit are plentiful on the northern rivers of
N.S.W. In appearance they greatly resemble
the ordinary fruit; the only difference is that the
leaves are a shade thinner and the fruit deadly
poisonous.” I lived five years alongside a passion-
fruit scrub (Woram, Richmond River), and I have
bought much of the seedy fruit in Sydney (which,
I presume, is the tame variety), and if there is
any difference between the two, I have not been
able to discover it. Wild passion-fruit—the very
wildest sort you can find—are not only eaten off
the vine, but people on the Richmond, Clarence
and Tweed Rivers make preserves, tarts and pies
with them ; and hawkers, plying up and down
the Richmond in boats, gather cases of them every
trip for sale in Coraki, Woodburn and Ballina.
Other excellent fruits plentiful on those rivers are
gooseberries (a rich, yellow fruit, enclosed in a
white husk when ripe), blackberries (a wild cur-
rant), raspberries, tomatoes, figs, cherries (which
formerly used to hang over the water in blood-red
masses, and fall in showers on the steamers’
decks), lillipillies, lemons, limes (two varieties),
yams (which fruit is a potato), quondongs, gee-
bungs, strawberries, and the tamarind—a splendid
nut. Sarsaparilla vines (as sold in Paddy’s Mar-
ket) are also plentiful on the Richmond ; and one
frequently comes upon dense clumps of peach
trees (gone wild), and acres of pie-melons, not to
mention numerous lien-fruit, deposited by the
descendants of fowl that deserted the barnyard
for the jungle. These fowl are quite expert on the
wing, and often lay in hollow trees.
* * * *
N.S.W. Government Botanist Maiden
makes clear some points about which “Abo.”
contributors are bushed :
“ Ossian,” on gooseberries and raspberries, isn’t quite
clear. The fruit to which he refers is the Cape goose-
berry (know to botanists as Physalis). It came to us
from South America, via Cape Colony. There are a
number of Physalises in the United States, where they
call them ground cherries. Our Cape gooseberry is a
sub-tropical plant, flourishing in our warmer coast
brushes. The common gooseberry of northern Europe
will only flourish in elevated situations in N.S. Wales,being
a cold-loving plant. If it is going to be elbowed out of its
ancient name of gooseberry by people who are too lazy to
call the other fruit Cape gooseberry, then it will have
to fall back upon its slang name of goosegog.
The reason why Cape gooseberries spring up everywhere
after a clearing, as referred to by “ Ossian ” and “ Gip,”
is not easy to explain. Certain birds eat the fruit
greedily and void the seeds, which are evidently capable
of lying dormant for a long time, years perhaps, till the
opening out of the brush affords the necessary foothold
and light for the Cape gooseberry to germinate and
spread.
As to native raspberries. “Ossian,” in speaking of
their exuberance on the North Coast, evidently refers to
the Australian species of raspberry. They are true rasp-
berries, but of different species from the common rasp-
berry of Northern Europe, which only flourishes in cold
situations. Our native species of raspberry ( Rubus ), of
which we have four, have engaged the attention of fruit-
growers, with a view to their improvement, but so far
without practical results. One of them (Rubus Muorei)
is, in my view, the worst “ lawyer ” of the coast districts.
Re wild passion-fruit, which “ Ossian ” speaks of as
“ deadly poisonous.” Here we again haveen introduced
passion-fruit and native passion-fruit. The common
passion-fruit that one sees in the shops (Pasxiflora
edulis) is not a native, but has spread enormously in the
warmer coast districts. We have in Australia about half-
a-dozen native species of Passijlora, some of them with
beautiful flowers. Their fruit is very poor eating, even
when fully ripe, but their poisonous nature only exists in
the imagination.

SLAVE O’ THE SHOVEL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When first the saffron cockfight flashes up the clouded
east,
And mirroring the dawn, the windy sky is golden-
fleeced ;
When, chirking down the gully at the bend of Marble
Creek,
The grey hawks gather where, red-splashed, the dressing
gallows reek ;
When the bower-birds wake up skittish in the holly-grove
behind, »
I also wake and potter out to face my daily grind :
And, as 1 slide into my boots and reach the raw meat
down,
(), how I wish I hadn’t left my' clerking job in town !
The loud, melodious chanting of the wind among the
gums
Makes melancholy music in my soul, when evening comes,
For then it is I wander back and lounge into the scrub,
To drag my bit o’ timber in and cook my bit o’ grub -
For then I find my lonely fork and make my lonely tea,
And think how sad this fife is for a clever chap like me ;
O, when I haul the red steak down and pound it once
again,
I’d give one-half my life to swap my shovel for a pen.
When, in the sweltering pub. at night, the rowdies plunge
and shout,
When, tumbling through the angry air, the bottles
fly about,
When dusty heads bump, cursing, in the road outside
the door,
And, drunken-deaf, the “dead-house” bunch lies tangled
on the floor,
When the dice-box bangs upon the bar, and the drinks
flow long and brown,
O, then I want to go back south to see my girl in town ;
O, then, when battling in the crowd, I hear the blue-rum
speak, .
1 want to be a clerk again at twenty bob a week.
But when, week-ends, I breast the rise to reach the office
shack,
Where Bullface, throned upon a box of fraeteur, hands
me back
My honest sweat in yellow gold, I look at him and smile
To see his fretful chiv., and guess I’ll chance the toil
awhile ;
O, then I clash the tawny coins within my horny hand,
And bless the kindly ways iof this, my good old native
land ;
Sure, then I love my lot, and as I swing full-chested down
The hill—O, I don’t wish I were a clerk again in town !
vie.
FRANK HENTY.

“Agricola”: Magpies are on the war-
path early this year, and are already embellishing
the vocabulary of the out-backer. Last week 1
was biking near Wagga, and, about a quarter of a
mile ahead, saw a wildly gyrating figure waving
a stick round his head in large capital S’s. 1
concluded that his business was snakes, and
bustled along to be in at the death. He turned
out to be a runaway German sailor on his way to the
shearing sheds. He was much nettled, and spat
out frightful German adjectives at an old man
magpie, who wiped his beak contemptuously on
an overhead limb, and aimed some visible scorn
at the German’s cap. The foreigner inquired
the name of the fearsome bird, and opined, “He
von tain sheeky bird alretty, by tarn.” He shook
his fist at old man Mag, and hurled Government
metal at him with the prodigality of an unna-
turalised foreigner. Then his patriotism came
out through all his woes : “Ach, Himmel! Soosh
sheeky birds ve not in Shermany have godt,
by tarn!” I left him to the company of
the “sheeky” bird, and, as I topped the rise,
looked back to mark the imported one’s progress.
Just as he lifted his bluey on his back, Mag
swooped at him and knocked his cap off.. In
terror he stamped both feet on the ground like a
startled buck rabbit, and made more S’s with
his stick, while old man Mag sailed off home to
make the missus and kids laugh with the story.
The squarehead didn’t mount his swag again, but
dragged it after him with one hand, and kept his
weather eye fixed on the bird till he got out of
range. It was the triumph of the native-born
over the imported article.

The vagaries of the Australian climate were
strikingly illustrated on July 26 when the ther-
mometer registered 25 degrees and under at places
like Bourke, Hay, White Cliffs, Hillston and
Walgett. At Coonabarabran the temperature got
down to 16, equal to 16 degrees of frost. A sum-
mer temperature of 118 has been registered at
Coonabarabran, a cheerful variation of 100 degrees.

“D.H.R.”: I am with “Elmore” (B.
12/7/’O6) all the way about protecting bees with a
close season. The way that thb hives are
robbed in the early summer months, by
idiots who don’t get enough to repay them
for labor done, is ridiculous j and the
way that others are destroyed in mid-
winter deserves the attention of the
Animals’ Comfort Society, or some-
thing. If the hives were looted,
as “Elmore” suggests, between
September and January, the
looters would find a good deal of
honey, while there would remain
ample time for the insect which
“improves each shining hour”
to lay in a winter’G supply. Of
course, the months flamed would
not suit all parts of the Common-
wealth, but they could be varied
in different parts. The native bee
has considerably decreased in
numbers in the otiter-back, and
there can be no doubt that the
decrease is due to' this wholesale
and indiscriminate plundering of
his commissariat waggons.
■* * *
“ J.S.” : Can you give me the
address of some place in Australia
that hasn’t a great future before it? I
don’t want places with futures behind
them or on top of them—l just want one without
a future at all. Recently a lot of Governors and
Ministers and M’s.P. and others have been tour-
ing this continent, and after devouring the fatted
turkey and drinking the fizzy wine they have
always told the local people that the place had a
wonderful future before it. The “wonderful
future” mostly depended on the local “vast
resources.” Surely there ought to be one place in
Australia where a man can be at peace—where
the rolling of the wheels of the stupendous future
won’t disturb his sleep, where there are no “ great
natural resources,” where there are no “pro-
spects,” no huge, bloated “ destiny ’’—nothing
but rest!
* * * *
“ Uckle ” : Be “ A.P.R.” (B. 5/7/’O6) and
his comments on the lack of firewood for rabbiters,
and the annoyance occasioned thereby. Whole
towns are likewise affected in the West, where
squatters monopolise the ground on all sides. The
squatter burns every stick of wood on his run, con-
sidering it as rabbit harbor. Instead of going but
a few yards for firewood now, the unfortunate man
in the town has to go, in many cases, eight or ten
miles out. When that lot is finished—good Lord
deliver us !
“Agricola”: “The Prooshan’s” crow-
slaying par. seems to have set things humming in
the crow line. But all the competitors have
neglected to mention the simplest method, prac-
tised by the juvenile with his untrusty '22. On
killing-day the carcase is left hanging all night to
cool, and the crows, who superintend the
slaughter from the neighboring gum-trees, call
round pretty early in the morning to sample the
quality of the beef, and to carry off the scrag-ends,
and the bulk of the kidney-fat. They don’t bother
to look in at the window, and caw, either —they’d
simply hate to interfere with the family slumbers.
But when the shadow of Scalp Day is hovering in
the middle distance, young Bill is up long before
the crows, and safely planted under the hide, with
the muzzle of the '22 bearing on the carcase.
The nose of old Bottly-neclc is too full of meat-
smell to notice the perfume of the boy, and as he
settles on the carcase, in the place where the tail
was yesterday, he softly chortles “caw.” That
is the last remark he makes in this world, for
young Bill conveys the corpse to the wood-heap
and decapitates it.
* » * *
“F.H.R.”: I’ll leave it to any jury of
bushmen that this is the only genuine “Wagga
rug.” Take three wheat or corn sacks and sew
them together with a packing-ijeedle and twine,
side to side. Nothing "more is needed, and if
they be ripped up the rug loses its most valuable
asset —weight. I have seen them made thus in
camps all over Australia, but have never yet seen
a man carry one in his swag. They are generally
made in the beginning of winter and discarded,
or turned into bag tent-flys, in the summer.
Made this way, they are just the right length and
width for a single bunk, and I know of nothing
that defies cold and frost like them. I put in a
winter in Gippsland with the aid of one, so I
ought to know.
* * * *
“ Pylock ” writes indignantly, evidently
under the impression that there is only one Night
Well and that he has the loan of it: —
Me night-well, who has been pulling “ Wurrym’s” leg?
It is quite evident from his letter that he has never seen
the well. Now, it is not east of Wagin, nor is it 30 or 40
miles from there, but it is situated in the bed of a creek,
on the Albany-Ravensthorpe Road, about 80 miles from
Albany and 100 from Ravensthorpe. It is eight or nine
miles from Mungup Station, on the Pallunip River. It is
a cleft in a big flat rock, and fresh water rises in this cleft
a* - irregular intervals and disappears, sometimes remain-
ing in nearly all day and sometimes only an hour or two.
It mostly comes in between 10 and 11 at night; some-
times not till three or four in the morning, and occasion-
ally not at all—once not for two weeks. I cannot offer
any explanation of the phenomenon, but fancy it is due
to some tidal action. It is a well-known camping place.
In sandalwood times I have seen 20 and 30 horses watered
there at a time, and it seemed to have no effect on the
supply. I, myself, have camped there scores of times,
and my father has known of the place for 40 years. It’s
native name is Caipuaru-Wimbric, which is rather long
for general conversation. Shall be glad to hear from
“ Wurrym ” again, and, if possible, show him the place
provided he is not a N.S.W. land agent.
GIPPSLAND CATTLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With bells the road tinkles
As home the mob passes
To the milk-cans and sheds,
And the long and lush grasses.
Whips crack at the muster,
Men shout and hoofs rattle
They smell lucerne paddocks
Wild, winter scrub cattle.
For food long they’ve foraged,
Turned out from snows’ dazzle
In the blanket-wood scrub
And the wild oats and hazel.
Driven back to the bails
At turn o’ the seed-time,
Proud mothers with calves
For the milk-time and feed-time.
'Cross creek and round cutting,
Up mountain side pushing,
Flanks steaming, horns gleaming—
They’re roaring and rushing.
And the shouts and bells clanging
Break louder and clearer,
Down bush tracks to Neerim
Still nearer and nearer.
Till the road rings and rattles
As home the mob passes
To the sliprails and sheds
And the sweet, springing grasses.
VIC.
LOUIN ESSON.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
A 'o notice (except of a hostile character) will he taken o f
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-

Pigsty: You seem to take more interest in
sectarian problems than this paper does. You say, “It is
a universal fact that the R.C. poor are the poorest of the
poor.” Dunno! Among European nations the ones
showing least wealth per inhabitant are Russia and Servia
(mostly Greek Church) and Turkey. (Roland, which is
Catholic, is the richest part of Russia.) For individual
poverty you won’t beat the East End of London by much.
Then you say that, with one exception, “ R.C. nations are
decadent.” Dunno ! Among the nations which have
nearly bossed Europe at different times, and which are
now nothing in particular, are Sweden and Holland (Pro-
testant), Spain and Rortugal (R.C.) and Turkey (Moham-
medan). Among conspicuously rising nations to-day are
Italy (it was going downhill before Christianity was in-
vented and is just beginning to look up), Argentina and,
to some extent, Mexico (all R.C.); Germany, U.S.,
Australia and Canada (which are from three-
fifths to four-fifths Protestant); and Japan (Bud-
dh.st). One thing, however, tnis paper does know.
In Australia most Orangemen seem to be attached to the
Freetrade party and to howl for foreign imports and attack
Australian industries—from Freetrader Rarkes with Ins
Kiama Ghost down to Freetrader Wilks with his Walls of
Derry. And in JN.S.W. every "candidate who is put up
now on the Orange ticket is a declared enemy of
Australian manufactures. That ought to make every
good Australian sick of the Walls of Derry

PIOUS REFLECTIONS.


“ The turning of the flood waters of the Gouiburn
River into Waranga Basin (Victoria) next week will
raise the water in the basin by 6ft. There are two
houses below what will then be water-level. One .Is
occupied by Mrs. Thomas Cleary, who announces that
unless she receives compensation for the loss of the house she wlil
stay in the old home until the Government drowns her. The Govern-
ment nas given her notice to quit, and has declined to recognise
claim to compensation."
Mr. Bent is here seen trying to persuade
Mrs. C. to “Come out o’ that.”
• see your Government wrecked A
first 1 ” says she.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Davy Gaunson, in Vie. Assembly: “ I
believe with all the great physicians that a man
should get drunk once a month.”
One of Bananaland’s new M’s.L.C. sank to
sleep twice during the opening ceremony. He
seems to have summed up the duties of the Upper
House pretty swiftly.
Labor member Charles McDonald, the new
Chairman of Committees in the House of Reps.,
is an artist among other things. Two of his paint-
ings, “Young Blue Gums, Victoria,” and “Bou-
gainvillea,” were shown at the recent annual
exhibition of the Queensland Art Society, held in
Brisbane.
Parson Hughes is getting on in the material
world of Melbourne, getting on in society, getting
on everywhere that money and an agreeable
anecdotal manner can get anybody on in this
world. He drives a fast motor, is a recognised first
nighter, and a frequenter of the cafes and high-
class dining places in the city.
At the age of 84 years, Joseph Taggert
went over last week, at Quirindi (N.S.W.).
Seventy years ago he arrived in Sydney with
his parents. As mailman' he opened up the route
between Murrurundi and Walgett when there
were but three white men at each end, and four
white women on the whole route.
Veteran Melbourne journalist James
Smith is bestirring himself in spiritualism again.
He has just circulated a pamphlet explanatory of
the cult, accompanied by a long report of a speech
said to have been made to a medium by the spook
of Richard Seddon. The speech is almost long
enough to be genine, for Seddon was dreadfully
voluminous in his later years.
The boundary rider’s find of 257 sovereigns
in a kerosene tin on the Molong (N.S.W.) station
of Kater, M.L.C., recalls a loss made by the same
Kater long ago, when a clerk in a Mudgee bank.
Kater was given some £6OOO in notes to be taken
to the head office as exchanges, and, at Cherry
Tree Hill on the way down, was relieved of his
handbag by a pair of bushies. One of the rangers
was arrested and returned his share, but the other
was shot dead by Sergeant Stephenson and the
hiding place of his £3OOO was never discovered.
A BIG CLIMB.
The anti-Bosh monkey after the cocoa-nut vote.
“Lil”: Be Melbourne police-magistrate
Panton (B. 19/7/’O6). My rabbit-vendor is candid
enough to confess that he has been forced under
the notice of that P.M. more than once. He gave
me the sum of his experience thus: “Panton’s
right enough in some ways, but when you’re up
before ’im. Gawd ’elp yer if y’ ’ave big ears. ’E
’as one look at yer ears, and you’re done ! ”
The proposal to present Mrs. Seddon with
her late husband’s Ministerial residence and
£lO,OOO is not likely to pass in Maoriland without
considerable opposition in the press, if not in the
House itself. Statements are being printed that,
apart from the real estate and personality of her
late husband, Mrs. Seddon is a fairly wealthy
woman in her own right.
Ex-Railway Commissioner John Mathieson,
some years ago autocrat of Queensland and then of
Victorian railways, was on two occasions within a
signature or two of securing railway control in
N.S.W. He was an able man in his line, but,
while threatening with dismissal any one under
him who solicited political influence, he himself
constantly courted it for his own advancement.
He cultivated prominent politicians as an industry,
quite irrespective of what State they belonged
to. Accordingly, when a hitch occurred any-
where, Premiers and leaders of Opposition thought
first of Mr. John Mathieson. His trouble lay
with the men under him. N.S.W. railway men
who threatened fight under Eddy and Oliver,
would have fought outright with Mathieson.
The appointment
of Acting Equity-
Judge Street (N.S.
Wales) will relieve
the congestion of

tered jurisdiction.

The Acting - Judge-

a “ stuff ” gown, but
has been moving up
