No. 3 J ury Court to say so.
S. Australia’s loud-voiced ex-Works Minister
Richard Witty Foster, who was once a full-fledged
Methodist parson—almost everybody is or has
been a Methodist parson in 8. A. —is to be one of
the Ass candidates for Senator. Once Foster
followed in the footsteps of Kingston, but associa-
tion with Jenkins affected his political morals,
and now his bray about the horrors of progress is
heard in and out of season.
The oath in Court is usually administered
by a glib and frequently illiterate tipstaff.
Judge Murray is the only N.S. Wales'Judge who
personally administers the oath. Burton, who
officiated in 1832-44, always administered the oath,
and made witness repeat the words after him.
That Judge was a stickler for punctuality ; on one
occasion he fined himself £5 for being late, but
before the Court rose remitted the fine on account
of his own previous good character.
Some years ago, when applying for ad-
mission to the Melbourne Cricket Club, a certain
Victorian M.P. was blackballed. He was what
was known as a pro-Boer, which was a dire
offence in those days. Since then Chinese labor
has been introduced into South Africa; also, the
M.P. in question has become a Minister of the
Crown, and was incidentally appointed a trustee
of the Melbourne (or Metropolitan) Cricket
Ground quite recently by the Government. Then,
when the recent cricket dispute was at its height,
Samson, in the person of the new trustee, rose
up and delivered a knock-out blow that put the
grab-all M.C.C. out of court. And, behind it all,
the M.C.C. sees a pro-Boer M.P. who was black-
balled by the club some years age.
Everett Digges la Touche, 8.A.,
spouting throughout Victoria against Home
Rule in the wake of the Irish delegates,
Devlin and Donovan, is said to have been
brought out from England by Simian
Phraser and others of the Orange clan.
Everett Digges la Touche is a small young
man of somewhat the same physical
type as the Wesleyan Judkins. So far
the very existence of Everett Digges la
Touche is hardly suspected by most
people.
“ Wang ” remembers an
apostle :
Reading- of Dowie, Judkins, and other fanatics, reminds
me of one I knew a few years ago. He hung out at
Oowies’ Creek, near Geelong, on the Anakie road. Every
Sunday, clad in white from shoes to headpiece, and
carrying a cross made of a pitchfork handle, to which
was attached a white cloth, he would station himself
alongside the road and wait for victims. The stock
market was held on Mondays at Geelong, and remote
stock owners sent in their animals on Sundays. The first
time I met old Snowflake I was following up a flock of
sheep on foot, leading my horse, with the reins slung
carelessly over my shoulder. Then suddenly a vision in
white hurled itself over an adjacent stone wall and bore
straight down on me, with the cross held aloft and the
cloth flapping in the breeze. The sheep spread them-
selves out all over the landscape, and my horse bolted.
The old pale evangelist cleared his throat for an exhorta-
tion ; but from his surprised look as he listened to my
remarks, he evidently came to the conclusion that I was
beyond his reclamatory powers. He contented himself
with wavjng the pitchfork-handle cross over my head a
couple of times. Then he went away to watch for the
next sinner. Last time I saw old Snowflake he was flying
for his life from an incensed Hindu hawker, whose gods
he had aspersed. I didn’t interfere.
Bishop Langley, of Bendigo, who died of
paralysis on Sunday, was born in 1840 at Dun-
garvon (County Cork, Ireland), and came to Aus-
tralia in the first 50’s. His early story in Ireland
mostly tells how he lived at some place beginning
with “Kil,” or moved to some place beginning
with “Bally.” He started to take care of the
immortal soul in N.S.W. at the early age of 25,
and in succeeding years he was two curates, four
incumbents, three archdeacons, and one bishop.
Bendigo only rose to the dignity of owning a
bishop in 1902, and Mr. Langley was the first
holder of the office. He leaves 12 children.
Mr. Sam Hordern, in the N.S.W. Arbitra-
tion Court, made some interesting remarks re-
specting apprenticeships; he does not believe in
them, and gave one instance as an illustration.
His father, Anthony Hordern, had apprenticed
to him a youth named William Bede Dailey, who
did not appear likely to make much of a draper,
but whom Anthony Hordern thought might make
something of a lawyer. Accordingly he released
the boy Dailey from his indentures, with results
widely known. It was generally imagined in
Sydney that it was to Mr. John Hordern, of
Nottingham House, corner George and Grosvenor
streets, that Dailey was apprenticed.
William Stuart Forbes, baronet, who died
in Maoriland lately, was a striking person-
ality, standing 6ft. sin. He was the ninth
baronet, and came into the unprofitable title some
years back. Stated that deceased derived the
income wherewith he bought plug tobacco
for his declining years in a rather curious
way. He had been one of four old cronies who
used to gamble at cards regularly, and the under-
standing was that all winnings should be banked
on the tontine principle, the last survivor of the
lot to take the pool. Baronet William saw the
others under the turf, and gathered in the shekels.
The shekels weren’t very numerous, but they
came in handy.

AUSTRALIA : “ Another loely advertisement for State rights .”
The pomp of Willie-Motor Kelly was
illustrated the day Bent gave Parson Worrall the
famous advertisement. Willie, with Fisher,
Thomas, McWilliams, Chanter, Hughes, Spence,
Salmon, Phillips and Maloney of the Reps., found
himself in the lobby of the State House. All
the seats inside were occupied by people who
had rushed to see the show. So William-Motor
asked Bent to allow them the back benches ot the
chamber, and Bent moved accordingly ; and gave
tho names of the Federal members who were to be
admitted. Willie’s name was far down the list,
but large odds were hurriedly offered in the Press
gallery that lie would be the first to enter. And
he was. He had shoved himself into the van, and
he led the way with all the pomp and self-
consciousness of a school girl rising in her new
white frock on exam-day to state that the boy
stood on the combustible upper storey of the ship.
Belmore, the Earl who clawed at his pay
more keenly than any other Governor N.S.W.
ever had, and after whom many things about Syd-
ney are named—including the malodorous markets
alongside which the Owe-’Sulli van railway-station
is built —is still living. Nowadays, at 71, he is
senior representative peer of Ireland, having been
elected, for life, half a century ago. His wife was
a niece of W. F. Gladstone’s. It was said that he
only accepted the post in N.S.W. while the
estates in the North of Ireland were being
nursed back to solvency. Besides Governoring,
he grew cabbages in Australia, and scraped in
every penny he could. He even claimed a day’s
pay for one day spent on board ship in Sydney
Harbor. “Who’s Who ” says that he owns about
15,000 acres, and is also heir to an estate in Kent
“ now enjoyed by his mother.” One would think
the lady must be too old for any wild enjoyment
in Kent.
“ Quan ” : John Macarthur may have
been the first in Australia to object to being tried
by a man who had borrowed money from him,
and this may have happened on the occasion of
his being cited to appear on a charge of having
smuggled a private still into Sydney ; but that was
not the occasion upon which he objected to Judge-
Advocate Atkin trying him, nor was the objec-
tion that stated (B. 19/7/’O6). Says Sutherland’s
“History of Australia” (which calls him Atkin,
and not Atkins): —
The determining cause (of Bligh’s deposition) was sup-
plied by a guarrel which took place between Mr. Mac-
arthur and Mr. Atkin, the new Judge-Advocate of the
colony. Mr. Macarthur was condemned to pay a heavy
fine for neglect, in having permitted a convict to escape
in a vessel of which he was partly the owner. He refused
to pay, and was summoned before the court of which
Atkin was the president. He declined to appear, on the
ground that Atkin was his personal enemy.
Finney’s “History of the Australian Colonies”
gives the same account.
Taken by-and-large, American millionaires
are a mean-souled lot, and perhaps the meanest
was the just-deceased Russell Sage. “Uncle
Russell,” like Rockefeller, has been praised as an
unremittingly industrious, churchgoing and am-
bitious man, but his meanness was a characteristic
which blotted out all the rest. Even when he was
handling millions of his own, and was one of the
biggest operators on New York Stock Exchange,
he was daily to be seen haggling with a street
apple-woman over the number of apples she
should give him for a penny* or growing tearful
in the effort to get a fly-specked cake of chocolate
at half-price. When apples were what he called
dear he lunched off half of one. Sage never gave
money away. A collector for a deserving cause
pointed out that Mrs. Sage (who had money in
her own right) appeared as a giver on his list, and
invited her husband to be another. He thought
he had scored when Uncle Russell wrote some-
thing on the paper, but on investigation it turned
out that he had merely sought for some cheap
credit by writing “Mr. and” before his wife’s
“Mrs. Russell Sage.” By such exercise of
“thrift’’this shocking example of millionairism
managed to leave sixteen million pounds, and
started for eternity without a penny in his pocket.
The silly censuring of Rev. Thingummy
at the Bar of the Victorian Assembly reminds
one that a bogus “dignity” of the British
Parliament was maintained for many years
after Cromwell’s time by the unresisted
authority of the Speaker. The penalty of being
“named” by the Speaker was vaguely under-
stood to be the most terrible thing thatcould happen
to a member of the Commons. If an inquisitive
young politician asked a veteran to explain the
effect of the Speaker’s naming, the veteran would
solemnly reply “God knows,” and that ended the
inquiry. Nobody dared to test the power of the
penalty. At last Hr. Kenealy, the champion of
the Tichborne Claimant, got into violent conflict
with the Chair. “ I call upon the honorable
member to resume his seat,” said Mr. Speaker.
The rowdy doctor of laws continued to perform
until the man in the wig had repeated his request
several times. Then came the “naming.” In
his loudest possible voice, the Speaker cried
"Dr. Kenealy”—and the House of Commons
held its breath. The hon. member had been
addressed as no other hon. member had been
addressed within the memory of man. The
mysterious meaning of the penalty was about to
come to light. Men gazed upon the victim as at
one whose minutes are numbered. The Speaker
completed his sentence, “Dr. Kenealy— sit
down!” Since then a good many members have
endured the “ naming ” without a shudder, and,
as a rule, they refused to sit down under it.
“jSTum” discourses about people who are
called to the Bar without any idea of supplying
them with liquid refreshment: —
The first man called to the Bar of the Vic. Assembly
was Turner, a Ballarat editor, who had offended the
majesty of Parliament by an article published in ’6l. The
proceedings were just as farcical as those of last week, and
having been admonished, Turner went away to sin some
more. Dill, the publisher of The Argus, was struck with
the same public feather a few months later. The next
man to appear at the Bar was Dill’s successor, Hugh
George, who was sent to the spot known as “ the leads ’’
where he lived like a belligerent rooster for
some weeks at State expense. Syme was the ensuin''
visitor in’67, and, in the course of the proceedings, Davi§
gave Parliament such a bad time that it was glad to let
him go. In the same year, Hargreaves, the gold dis-
coverer, appeared for having made statements about
certain alleged smellful transactions implicating a num-
ber of leading citizens of the day. The affair was allowed
to fizzle out, apparently in the interests of those who
desired to let the sleeping dogs remain horizontal. In
’6B one Andrew’Tobin, a wild Irishman of sixteen stone,
was brought up for threatening Minister Longmore with
stoush and not supplying it, and, the following year, Glass
and Quarteman appeared to answer charges of corruption.
In their case, the usual practice of sending prisoners to
“ the leads ” W’as not followed. They were committed to
Melbourne Gaol, and remained there unti) the end of the
session, which is the longest sentence Parliament has
power to impose. Over 20 years elapsed before
Clark, the editor of one of the Ballarat papers
was brought up. The Speaker bellowed forth
his censure, and shook the Mace ; and that pressman,
who w'as as deaf as a post, stood at the Bar wonder-
ing whether he was being complimented on his
vigorous English or sentenced to death. Parson
Edgar, of the Wesley Church—prominent last week in the
glorification of the Rev. Worrall—was the next offender.
Edgar had said that Parliament contained liars and
profligates, but he sadly lacked the grit of his brother,
Worrall. At the Bar, he did the most abject trawl ever
seen outside Japan. Elmslie and Packer, publishers of
the Argus and Age, were each fined £lOO in ’99 for print-
ing a report on a subject that was sub judice, and Wor-
rall’s is the first case since then. There is a general im-
pression that the late Jimmy M’Kean, the giant Mel-
bourne solicitor, was called to the Bar; but a different
procedure was adopted in the two cases in which he was
concerned. M’Kean was a member for years, and, in '76,
he remarked, in Collingwood court, that members of
Parliament were a lot of drunken scoundrels. The same
night the matter was referred to in the Assembly, a com-
mittee of enquiry was appointed, and when Parliament
met next day the first business was the expulsion of
M’Kean from the House. The- other case, which hap-
pened a few months previously, occurred through
M’Kean’s defiance of the Chair, lie was “ named ”in his
place, and, as he still scoffed at the piece of furniture, he
was committed to “ the leads,” where he remained a
prisoner for three weeks. M’Kean may be said to have
led a stormy life in politics.
Parson Won-all's accusing screech at
Gillott, and its comic-opera consequences, sopped
up all the available limelight, and left Brudder
Judkins in the cold, wet darkness. But Judkins,
who has a paper as well as a pulpit to advertise,
wasn’t the sort to stand that. He arose at first
opportunity and loudly proclaimed, not merely
Gillott, but the whole Vic. Parliament asM’Leod’s
murderers. Then Judkins anxiously awaited the
sudden blaze of light. But it didn’t coine -only a
thicker blackness. Bent had suddenly found sense
and had recognised that there is no profit in taking
serious notice of the hysterics of women and
parsons.

On the 19th June a memorial service was
held at St. Paul’s Cathedral, London, as a last
tribute of respect to the late Richard Seddon.
All English officialdom, from the King to the
Salvation Army, was represented; every Aus-
tralian State sent its Agent-General ; the Cape
and Natal were there. Even the British South
Africa Co. sent its secretary. The Commonwealth
alone was conspicuous in being unrepresented.
Matters must have reached a pretty pass if politi-
cal jealousies in his Cabinet are to prevent Deakin
from daring to provide Australia with a represen-
tative in England for even a funeral service.
THE DIFFERENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
T am a man led by the holy spirit. —Parson W. H
W orrall, at Bendigo.
Of old the grim Jehovah ruled
His peoples with an iron rod ;
By flood and famine they were schooled
To fear the heavy hand of God.
He smote the froward ones of earth
With fire and pestilence and sword ;
By open grave and blackened hearth
They bowed themselves before the Lord.
But now the puny priestlings seek
In vain to sway a careless world ;
The voice of thunder waxes weak,
The bolts are impotently hurled.
The ancient glory groweth dim,
And all the splendid strength is spent;
For Judkins is the voice of Him,
And Worrall is His instrument.
VIC.
R.P.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, August 7, 1906.
My Dear Arini,—
A large bunch of orange blossoms: Miss J eanette
Osborne, one of the Ben. Osbornes, of Hopewood,
Bowral, is going to stand under the floral
wedding-bell with Mr. Arthur Peck, who is in
the civil service of some Malay region. They
met on the premises of Jessop, the cricketer, in
England. She is one of the best-looking of the
great army of her name. Mr. Duncan Osborne,
one of the Aleck Osborne group, is engaged to
Miss Maddrell, of Braidwood. A sister of hers
married a beautiful, brass-bound person named
Hussey-Cooper, of the P. and 0. line. Bride-
groom-elect (Duncan) has a cherubic baby face,
and both he and his Choice are well-off. Also
Miss Minnie Richards, of Rand wick, is engaged
to Mr. Willie Badgery, of Taralga.
In musical and wool coteries the engagement of
interest is that of Mr. George Grimm, the well-
known vocalist, to Miss Kate Durham, of Rich-
mond. She is a confessedly beautiful girl with a
little voice—just enough to blend with George’s.
The Durhams, who have a ripping house at Rich-
mond, are related to the Wentworths, Hills,
Badgerys, and Dalgarnos.
Miss Lizzie Hogue, second daughter of the
Chief Secretary, is another about to face the
three-barred love-honor-and-obey gate. Bride-
groom-elect is named Holmes.
A son of the Chief Secretary has been engaged
by Mr. J.C. W. for the Repertory Co.
A last-mail letter from London says that Melba
is much stouter than when Australia last gazed
upon her, and now presents the real “straight-
front ” figure to her admiring audiences. But as
long as Fat doesn’t get into that Voice nobody
but Melba and her dressmakers will mind.
One, who asserts that she knows , tells me that
Court presentation gowns may be hired for five or
ten guineas—and that even the expense of “the
rotten sixty guinea gown ” may be avoided. Also,
this Knower says that a Somebody holding
a lofty billet within shadow of King Edward
told a certain Agent-General that presentations
are made easy for Australians and Canadians,
especially for Australians. “WE”—notice the
size of the pronoun—“ recognise the slight class
distinctions in Australia and its democratic spirit,
and in consequence any Australian woman, pro-
vided she be reputable, is eligible for presentation
to her Majesty. Even the fact that she may be
working for her living is not a bar.” (There’s
generosity for you ! Imagine the Royal charity
being wide enough to cover such a sin as that of
getting your own living!) “WE” went on to
assure his hearers that this tolerance and let-’em-
all-eome courtesy was not extended to the English,
Irish, or Scotch woman. For her the Lord Cham-
berlain’s statutes are not eased any more than
in the late Victoria’s time. The Countess of
Crewe and Mrs. Coghlan seem to be sponsoring
the majority of Australians. If King Edward
would only say something democratic, such as “I
hope that every Australian girl, even though
she comes to my Kingdom as a stewardess,
will make it convenient to be presented to US,”
he would make a great number of persons glad, or
mad.
A romantic advt. from Melbourne Argus :
■p ECLUSE, beyond want, occupying little, low-roofed
- L|J bungalow, on idyllic river' bend, weary of iso-
lated solitude, desires to correspond with home-
loving woman, not averse to the holy bond. Sylvan
Dell, Argus Office.
Doubtless a tall, thin, elderly gentleman, with
that stoop that the peerage gets through bowing
to royalty at the Court of St. James, or at
Almacks, or the Tower of London, or somewhere.
He has a short, pointed beard (Van Dyck pattern),
and a tendency to linger with long white hands
over rare books and engravings, and he personally
breathes on his valuable collection of cameos, and
polishes them with his handkerchief. In the
low-roofed bungalow he ponders on his ancestors
who came in with the Conqueror, or the devil, or
some other remote personage. And this elevated
hermit now feels the want of a hermitess, who is
tall enough to live up to his dignity, and not so
tall as to knock her head against the lid of the
bungalow ; hence this advertisement, replies to
be addressed to Sylvan Dell —not “ Briggs.”
Amongst ancient Sydney mansions now on sale
is “Tusculum,” out Potts’ Point way. It was
built by a wine merchant named Young when
there were very few houses in Macleay-street,
and was afterwards occupied for many years by
William Long, wine and spirit merchant and
semi-millionaire, father of Mrs. Martin (the wife
of Chief Justice Martin) and Mrs. W. B. Dailey.
After the widow Long’s death, it was for a time
occupied by the only son, William Alexander
Long, M.L.C., of racing fame—commonly called
“Bill.” After him the house had a variety of
occupants, amongst them the W. P. Mannings.
Now much of the ground has been cut up
and sold, and the old mansion no longer
fronts Macleay-street, but hides itself away at
the back.
Mr. Frank Coffee and daughter Holly go for a
trip to the Land of the Canned by the Sierra on
Monday week. Mr. W. J. Spruson (of the firm of
Hepburn and Spruson, patent agents) also goes on
the same trip.
Miss Lute Mackenzie, daughter of Colonel
Mackeiizie, who commands something or someone
in Hobart, and niece of Francis Suttor, of the
Beautiful Calves, left yesterday with the Collier
Company for America, where she will make her
first appearance, perhaps carrying a banner. She
is a pretty, graceful youngster, and her military
parents must be quite day-after-to-morrow in
advancement to smile upon and sanction their
lamb’s sortie.
The smart florist girl, who runs her business
under the name of Florance and Co., is about to
be married to a Viennese resident in Sydney, one
of a firm of Austrian merchants.
Saw a just-finished linen frock lately that had
one of the small embroidered side pockets for a
hanky. It was in the form of an envelope with
straps at either side, and fastened with a hook in
the belt.

A Smart Luncheon*.
(Mrs. Newmoon, a smart Sydney hostess, stands survey-
ing her smart luncheon table with the eyes, of a grazing
coxy looking at the next chew of grass. Three smart
maids stand a little behind her, respectfully sympathetic
in her satisfaction. The lady is smartly dressed. Her
hair, ever so slightly brightened by art, gleams as brilliantly
as the silver on the table. Her environment is luxu-
rious, but the pictures are not in the best taste.
She is quite young, and her complexion exquisite. A
whirring sound is audible from without, and, with a loud
sough, a smart automobile stops at the door. Mrs. New-
moon’s large brown eyes take on the look of a cow
expecting the evening bran. Enter a blaste debutante of
eighteen, wearing a tailor-made of violet cloth. She kisses
Mrs. Newmoon, who addresses her as Bouncie).
Mrs. Newmoon : Bouncie, it’s just dear of you to come
so early. What do you think of mv table ?
Bouncie (after a prolonged scrutiny) : It’s simply
ripping, Belle 1 Those two shades of pale green silk fixed
up to look like the petals of a flower—and the Neapolitan
and Russian violets. AVhere did you get it from ?
Mrs. Newmoon : It is 1113* own idea.
Bouncie : Oh, Belle, you little liar ! You know you
could no more have ideas than freckles.
Mrs. Newmoon (with a pleased side glance towards
the mirror): I mean I used the idea I saw in a fashion
paper. I wanted to have everything awfully nice, as it’s
lanthe’s last unmarried luncheon.
Bouncie : “ Last unmarried luncheon ! ” No wonder
you married so well, Belle, you do blunder so fasci-
natingly.
(They go into thedrawing-room and the blasie debutante
robs three chairs of their cushions to make her own more
comfortable).
Bouncie : lantlie is a hundred and two, isn’t she ?
Mrs. Newmoon : No. She's just thirty-two. Auntie is
her godmother you know*, and
Bouncie : And your aunt is just the woman to keep
other women up-to-date in the matter of a girl’s age—
especially if it’s verging on old age 1
Mrs Newmoon : Bouncie ! you'cat. Did you come half-
an-hour too early just to hand me acid-drops ?
Bouncie (after a pause): Well, dear, to tell you the
truth, 1 came early to warn you to set me next lanthe.
To-day she’s just an old maid of no particu'ar import-
ance, but by this time to-morrow she’ll be a smart young
married woman. 1 want her to persuade her ridiculous
husband to buy our motor-car. You know it cost £5OO.
We’ll sell for £4OO. Only three months in use. I want
the money for a flutter in Egypt with the Lambswools.
Who’s coming, Belle?
Mrs. Newmoon : The Lambswools and
Bouncie (interrupting): Isn’t Ren6 Lambswool getting
sharp ami hard-faced ? Really, her reflection in a mirror
is enough to scratch the quicksilver off the back. Go on
Belle.
Mrs. Newmoon : Mrs. Buster-Cross
Bouncie : The Greasy Merino.
Mrs. Newmoon : And Mrs. Margaux and Pearl
Bouncie : The vineyard people. Well, don't sit them
near the Lambswools. Wool and wine ivon’t mix. Yes?
Mrs. Newmoon : And Mrs. Foozler
Bouncie : I’m always glad of Rachel. She’s got the
freshest scandal made up on the spot—her spot. Ido
hope she’ll wear her leaning-tower-of-Pisa hat—the red
one that says out loud, “ This way', ladies ; this way' to the
bargain counter.” Go on, Belle.
Mrs. Newmoon : Miss Platto and
Bouncie : If old Platto’s in a bad humor she'll be amus-
ing. But “Menu, not Men,” is her motto. Anyone
else ?
Mrs. Newmoon: Mary Kittenly. She’s back from her
wedding-tour, you know ; and the Scoffsby' girl, and, of
course, lanthe. That’s all.
Bouncie : And enough 1 Ten human Black Thursdays !
I know I’ll be bored unless lanthe promises to buy' the car.
(Within the next ten minutes the ten guests arrive.
Some are in golf or hockey clothes, the others are smartly
frocked and furred, and wear violets. They follow their
hostess into the dining-room, their voices pitched high,
their movements energetic and confident. Only Mrs.'
Buster-Cross moves as if her legs were made of unjointed
wood. She smiles broadly from ear to ear, and from eye-
brows to treble chin. All seat themselves, talking as one
about the artistry of the table—all save Miss Platto, who
is intently reading the menu. The first course is served.)
lanthe (in reply to something the blasie debutante has
been urging upon her) : I’d love a motor, of course, but I
don’t think Billy'’s means will run to a car as well as the
horses and a brougham ; of the two I’d prefer the brougham ;
I don’t want to hide the light of my hair under a motor-
veil. Billy* says—
Mrs. Buster-Cross (a little oily about the lips and
chin): But isn’t it awful to think that a young man of his
age can’t marry without his mother’s section ?
The Scoffsby Girl (leaning eagerly forward) : Her what,
dear Mrs. Buster-Cross ?
Mrs. Buster-Cross (beaming): We’re talking of Hal
Smooker’s engagement, dearie. His father left it after
him in the will that Hal couldn’t marry without his
mother’s section.
(A group of the guests shriek with merriment. The
hostess colors faintly. Mrs. Buster-Cross looks offended,
and, amidst some confusion, Rene Lambswool holds out
a post-card.)
Miss Lambswool : It’s all through this, Mrs. Buster-
Cross.
Mrs. Buster-Cross (eagerly): Is it a funny one ? Send
it here. (She studies the card, which shows a draped
Grecian figure holding aloft a lamp. Then her jaw
falls) : Well, where’s what you laughed at ?
The Scoffsby Girl : Oh, just look at the length of the
waist of the girl. If you had a waist line that, Mrs.
Buster-Cross, we’d laugh at you. (Mrs. Buster-Cross,
whose 33-inch waist is near her armpits, looks pleased.)
Mrs. Lambswool : When I got your cheque, Mrs.
Buster-Cross, for the Infants’ Bib Fund, I got as well a
delightful shock, and- it took me ten minutes to realise
that the third figure of the amount was not a mistake.
(Mrs. Buster-Cross, flushing radiantly, imparts to the
wool and wine ladies the terms of the late Buster’s will.
Also, those of the still later Cross’s testament.)
Mrs. Foozler (in earnest rumble-toned conversation
with the Scoffsby girl) : It’s not the slightest use in the
Pastepotters trying to get into Society—for Society
won’t have them at any price
Miss Platto (interrupting): Yes, it would, only they
are not willing to pay the price. She can’t, or won’t,
give luncheons and dinners like Mrs. Newmoon, and her
husband won’t have an always-open billiard-room and
free whisky and wine.
Mrs. Foozler : I think that the block is that ante-
nuptial scandal, and
Mrs. Newmoon (to Miss Platto): Don’t say “No” to
that Huviard Amerieaine Mattie.
Miss Platto : I’ve said “ Yes ” twice to it.
Mrs. Marsaux (looking at Rachel Foozler): Ante-
nuptial scandal! Nothing of the kind ! Why, Sydney’s
too busy winking at the post-nuptial flirtings. It’s
Mrs. Pastepotter’s hats Society won’t tolerate. They' are
a cross between the helmet of Navarre and that millinery
calamity that Nellie Stewart wears in “ Sweet Nell.”
Mrs. Newmoon : It takes a tall, large, beautiful woman
like Mrs. Crosby to wear such hats.
(All eyes turn expectantly towards Miss Platto.)
Miss Platto (dropping her fork audibly and looking at
her hostess with angry eyes): “A beautiful woman like
Mrs. Crosby ! ” Belle, you used to be a girl of such refined
taste. Mrs. Crosby is tall and large, I grant you—so is a
giraffe and an elephant. With her snapping black eyes,
her strong black hair, her white face, thin painted mouth
and great, thick, round throat with those two awful lines
running around it, and
The Kittkslt Bride (interrupting): Du Maurier called
them “ the collar of Venus.”
Miss Platto (continuing) : She’s wise always to wear
black, for all she wants is a scarlet frock to look the
typical Frisker of Babylon.
(Everybody laughs, and Miss Platto clutches the menu
afresh. Mrs. Lambswool murmurs to Mrs. Buster-Cross
that Mattie Platto is privileged “ because she has sixty
years and sixty thousand.”)
Rene Lambswool (to the table): Have you seen The
Woman at Home for this—
lanthe: Of course we haven’t. No one has. The woman
is never at home.
Miss Platto (looking sourly at the Missuses present):
No, she’s too busy scuttling over to the other woman’s
house Bridge-playing, or racing on board warships and
liners, or pursuing the remains of old knapsacks at the
Barracks, looking for Platonic affairs.
Rene Lambswool : I mean the magazine with the por-
traits of Australian beauties in it?
Bouncie (jumping into the conversation) : Is any one
of us in it?
Rene Lambswool : No.
Chorus : Then what a collection of frights they must be !
Mrs. Buster-Cross : The new millinery is something
lovely. Yesterday Madame Lousiana gave me a show all
to myself, and I bought the heavenliest Gobbling blue
toque. When Madame took it out of its box 1 let a
screech of joy out of me and nearly kissed her——
Bouncie (shuddering, and aside to lanthe): What a
horribly narrow escape for Madame ! Why, if the kiss had
come off she’d have had to go to the wool-scourer’s.
Mrs. Newmoon (to Mrs. Buster-Cross): I wish you’d
worn it to-day, dear.
Mrs. Buster-Cross : Well, I would only I was afraid
some of youse might get one like it before I’d had a chanst
to wear it at the Saumarez-Smith’s Garden Party.
The Scoffsby Girl (aside to her neighbor): Isn’t the
Greasy Merino a whole Tivoli programme ?
Mrs. Foozler (a sugar factory in her voice): You
should have bought a river hat as well, Mrs. Buster-Cross.
One with brodene Anglaise and some bebe ribbon, and a
touch of a pastel shade. It would suit you.
Mrs. Buster-Cross : So I think, but Madame Lousiana
put me off it. “Mrs. Buster-Cross,” she says, “leave
river 'ats to them as suits them.” This is going to be a
dark woman’s season, and if there’s one thing I am it’s
dark ; so I’ll have a wide pick amongst the soft yellows
and wine shades and over-blown rose ones.
* * * *
They talk more millinery, and more about people, and a
little about the theatres.
» * * *
Three hours from the start of the smart luncheon the
blasie debutante, with the Lambswool beside her in the
motor, is whirring off the Newmoon premises.
Bouncie : Belle Newmoon has a perfect genius for
getting the wrong people together. 1 wonder if she
thinks tee enjoyed sitting there fora hundred years just
because lanthe has at last yarded a man ?
Rene Lambswool : Isn’t lanthe going to buy this gaso-
line cart ?
Bouncie : No. She gave me to understand that she and
her newly-acquired Billy would have to boil on tuppence
a week. llow I hate poor people ! (Yawns.)
Another enormous crowd, variously estimated
at between 60,000 and 80,000 people gathered
about Parramatta River to watch the Ladies’
Double Sculling Championship of N.S.W., con-
tested by the six crews that the previous
Wednesday’s preliminary racing had left in the
final. Originally 20 ladies formed 10 crews, and
the districts represented were North Sydney,
Abbotsford, Stockton (Newcastle), Glebe Point,
Manly (2), Mayfield (Newcastle), Port Hacking,
Port Macquarie, and Darlinghurst. The winning
pair were Mesdames Wood bridge and Hyde, of
Stockton, near Newcastle, who wore light blue
sashes and caps, and pulled marvellously for women.
I was told that they acquired their strength and
skill through pulling from Mosquito Island to
Newcastle every morning with the milk. The
Herbert girls, of Port Macquarie, in gold sashes
and caps, made a succession of brilliant
efforts to overtake the winners, and, had
they—-the winners—been ordinary women, would
have beaten them. But they were not ordi-
nary women—they were machines in skirts.
A fearsome row has broken out in Rose Bay
women’s golfing circles ! The committee of the
R. Club blackballed a nice girl, whose grand-
fathers wore evening dress, and whose grand-
mothers wore their clothes cut low after sunset,
what time the committee’s grandpas were going
about in shirt-sleeves and eating with their
knives. The only reason for the pilling was,
“We don’t know her.” Which is a rash and
insufficient sort of reason, seeing that the club
isn’t personally acquainted with the Czar or the
Shah of Persia.
The trousseau talk of the hour wanders in
circles around the bridal array of Miss Muriel
Marks, who marries Dr. Watson next month.
Miss Esme Sly, one of the legal family of that
name, was voted belle of the University Sports’
Club ball. She belled in cornflower blue chiffon
and silver.
Two things that will stand out—or up, like
Kosciusko—in the memory of those who attended
the reception to Dolores at Town Hall were (1)
the Hannibal-like generalship of Mr. Southwell
in organising and leading to the Guest’s hand-
clasp over three thousand excited women, and
(2) the emphasis of the Dolores kiss. Hannibal
S. brought in the forces five deep, and when
Dolores saw a face that belonged to a friend she
kissed it, forte. A rhapsody in B major is the
Dolores salute. “Your name, please?” asked
Hannibal of a lady awaiting presentation. “I
didn’t speak,” was the surprised reply. Another
to whom the same query was put gave him the
Polar eye and remarked, “I haven’t been intro-
duced to you.” Dolores is the most fascinating
feminine musical personality that has struck Aus-
tralia, within the recollection of anybody alive
or only moderately dead, but she has no
more taste in frocks than a rock-cod. She
turned up at the Ella Caspers concert
in a rig reminiscent of Maggie Papakura leading
the tourist about Rotorua and taking long, wild
leaps over boiling springs. She spoke the golden
word about the little contralto—" Genius.” Pos-
sibly there wasn’t a dissentient opinion in the
audience. The girl has a beautiful voice, and
brains. Her “Abide With Me” dropped note
by note a benediction, and seemed to exhale
purity and peace.
If that liner-load of women that is coming to
Australia under the wing of the Immigration
League to marry Superfluous Man—that isn’t the
ostensible reason, but not even the affidavits of a
car-load of angels would convince me that it isn’t
the true one—should hear that from the Randwick
Bachelors’ Dance last week 30 nice girls went home
early because there was no possibility of their getting
partners, it—the liner-load—may cancel its col-
lective passage. The bachelors of Randwiek are
clearly road-metalled in the matter of feeling. A
steam-roller could roll over the most delicate
blossom of sentiment in their composition, and
the only result would be to cripple the machine
through it rebounding off the blossom. They
can do worse than live in single wickedness —and
they did it when they so heartlessly under-manned
their ball.
Actor William Collier left not many personal
regrets behind. His manner was cold and for-
bidding. With his company he was always top
dog, and the only dog. No one else was allowed
a ray of the limelight. At his farewell night in
Melbourne, the ladies of the co. weren’t allowed
to receive over the footlights the flowers sent to
them by admirers of their art. But that didn’t
prevent the actor man himself, on his last night in
Sydney, taking a gift across the footlights. The
gift was a puppy, and it wasn’t meant sarcastic
either, though it might have been.
Just died, the widow of J. B. Rundle, M.L.C.,
a well-known figure out Potts Point and Elizabeth
Bay way. The deceased lady met her death in a
peculiar fashion. It had been her custom for a
long time to patronise a certain George-street
shop. Going thither, as usual, her coachman was
blocked by other vehicles, and had to draw up a
door or two away. The poor old lady, not noticing
that she was at the wrong door, walked straight
into a flight of steps leading to a basement, and
fell to the bottom.
Wonder if we shall have the new eye-shade in
Australia by the time the many Spring meetings
are in full neigh ? A woman invented it, and
called it “The Maisette,” and it has caught on
abroad as a preventive of sun headache and eye-
screwing. The Maisette is a bar of tortoise-shell
or gold, lined with green silk and mounted on a
folding stick. When you wish to look at the
flannelled fool at the wicket, or the bogged oaf at
the goal, or watch your horse chasing the field
before it, you rest the bar against your forehead,
and the great natural laws which govern the uni-
verse do the rest.
Artist Percy Spence has set up in London as a
painter of portraits. Two hours in accomplish-
ment and ten guineas. He did several water-
color portraits of pretty women whilst here in that
time, and at that rate ; but, though the pictures
were beautifully worked up as regards hair and
dress accessories,, the friend of the original of the
portrait in some cases would exclaim at first glance
“Who’s that?” His tendency was to idealise
the faces—-to make them all perfect ovals.
The Players, so long regarded as Sydney’s
tip-top amateur combination, will have to look to
their laurels, judging by the way the Sydney
Muffs played “The Amateur Burglar” and “The
New Boy” at Sydney Criterion Theatre in aid-of
the United Dental Hospital, which will serve the
double purpose of giving students experience and
fixing up the teeth of those who are too poor to
pay for their treatment. The versatile. Donald
Smith and energetic Stanley McKay practically
ran the show, and the Muffs played both
pieces in a manner that did them proud. In “ The
New Boy ” the company was quite at home in an
atmosphere of rollicking humor ; the Muffs
appear to be a genial, pleasure-loving crowd. The
two women parts were excellently done, Mrs.
Rennick being dressed and played admirably by
Miss Vera Wallis, while the part of Nancy Roach
was well filled by Miss Maud Crothers. Miss
Wallis’s dresses were beautiful, and the name of
the builder thereof would be a secret worth
knowing.
My Brisbane correspondent writes
The engagement is announced here of Miss Edith
Griffith, daughter of the Chief Justice of Australia, to
Mr. Doyle, of station fame, and well endowed.
Brisbane is filling up for the Show, and by every train
arriving from the West there tumbles forth a crowd of
country cousins—pa, ma, the quiver (very) full, and
bundles.
At the recent Naval and Military Ball, where their
Exes. Chelmsford, the Admiral and Lady Fawkes, bonny
Miss Du Cane, pretty Mrs. Braithwaite, and a host of
minor stars shone over the assembled throng-, the most des-
perate yet unsuccessful efforts were made to put the
official set through without mistake. Two aides, Bertram
Brooke and the Admiral’s own, were stationed close by, and
as the opening bars were played they whispered detailed
explanations to their respective chiefs. But, alas ! The
outer and inner chain was too much for them, and, after
two or three frantic dives into the struggling mass of
spurs and flounces, these would-be marshals just leant
against the wall and subsided into hopeless laughter.
They had to do that or else weep.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, August 7, 1906.
My Dear Yarrie,—
The fattest charmer in this capital is called
“Babs.” An older battler is playfully labelled
“Babe,” and the very large, plump, though quick-
witted wife, of a prominent Federalist answers to
the name of “ Tiny.” There’s one Society chick
front-named “Dove,” and another “Bee.” After
all these, plain Nora Power—the blunt label of
the prettiest Power among them all—strikes one
as wholesome and sane.
Countess Linlithgow has a frank horror of this
‘roo-land. Some drifter from these parts asked
the chilly dame whether she had seen a certain
event chronicled in the Australian papers. “No,”
said the countess, coldly, tightening her lips; “I
never wish to see an Australian paper as long as I
live.” (Her taste, they say, when out here, was
the Pink TJn.)
The humors of the Divorce Court occasionally
obtrude. A distressed plaintiff in a suit told the
Bench, the other day, that her particular brute
was the most evil-tempered man “she had ever
lived with.” Perhaps she didn’t exactly mean
that. If she did, it was an awkward place to own
to a plurality of pasts. By the way, it’s unusual
for the much-loved woman to talk Man. It’s the
spinster who doesn’t know him, and the baffled
dame who married him and still doesn’t under-
stand him, who talk, year in and year out, of him
and his villainies and weaknesses and vices and
sins.! Hear an unloved Christian Endeavorer on
the sins alone !
A well-meaning bachelor, of giddy habits, sent
a bride a box of light-colored silk stockings as a
souvenir, the other day. They were the latest
thing from Paris, but the ’groom objected so vio-
lently that the girl posted the scented packet
back, with a request for a clock or a cruet (if he
would send something). She delicately intimated
that she desired a souvenir which would last, and
which the donor could view for himself now and
then. So he forwarded a silver box of powder—
it was evidently the only feeble revenge he could
think of.
Justice Cussen is slowly but surely being drawn
from fireside reveries into the glitter of Vanity
Fair. His dame is being-drawn likewise. At a
recent hop she mothered 32 fledgelings. Thirty-
two ! And she scouted for them with any amount of
red-hot zeal. Men fled from her appealing eye.
Never did a duenna fight so desperately for her
charges. “ I must introduce you to my girls,” she
told every male she could lasso. Escape being
impossible, he turned to face the flock, expecting
three at the most. Thirty-two girleens smiled
upon him. In a dazed state ho saw 32 flapping
programmes. Ih a nightmare he felt 64 eager
eyes watching him. And Mrs. Cussen has the
humor to laugh now at the awful experiences of
her victims.
One satisfactory opinion from a wanderer,
gathering the roses while she may in the Thames
vicinity, is to the effect that Australian frockers
show up well in juxtaposition to the English
misses. It came as a surprise' to this frill devotee,
who had felt qualms at appearing at an Anglo-
Australian affair of much lustre, to find that she
and dozens of other ’Roo-landers were quite in
the picture, and that charmers like Miss Ruby
Madden, Miss Muriel Cumming, Miss Langtree,
Mrs. : H. Levy, and the Misses Anderson (of
Sydney) show with distinction against a back-
ground of limp and bony Londoners. The more
blue-blooded the Vere de Vere, the bonier she is.
Fat is a crime in the eyes of the splinter of
nobility. Of course a fat monarch is another
matter. There is a majesty that doth hedge
the avoirdupois of Royal Edward.
One sees the name of Mr. George Armstrong
constantly in the lists of “those present” in the
Big Smoke files. Melba’s son takes kindly to the
tempestuous pettings of his mama. Report says
that no one has a hope of the Diva’s enthusiastic
attention now that son George is housed with
her. What a pity, in a way, it’s not a Georgina,
to wallow in feminine luxuries, and sample a
season in the great axle of the universe under the
wing of the first canary in the world. After all,
a youth can only be gay to satiety. He gets no
excessive satisfaction out of the pinking of his
nails, the set of his coat-tails, the size of his shoe,
the shape of his eyebrow, and the dozen and one
effects the second sex joys in. It’s a good thing
to be feminine and deliciously foolish over the
quality of one’s hanky, and even smaller details.
A man can never know to the full the raptures to
be got out of toilette effects.
The Willie Manifolds are enjoying a great time
in London. In a word the sporting pair are going
big. The dame is an energetic social battler with
a cheery black eye and a figure sufficiently cor-
setted to save it from being buxom. The polo
champion himself is a typical socially-indifferent
squatter who is content, when away from the
sheep-run, to give his mate the reins and to be
quite docile. He’ll go to a frilly shivoo with the
patient look of an uncomplaining ox, but there his
social activity ends. He can’t be induced to
flutter. As well expect a harvesting machine to
two-step over the crops. The one Manifold chick
en tour will be given all the shining hours that a
devoted, wealthy and knowing rnommer can fit
into a season.
A pair of Bananalanders, also going big in the
Mecca of ambitious wanderers, are the Cunliffes.
They have taken the Countess of Something’s
house in Surrey, and go a-motoring in the most
up-to-date goggles and things. Of course, the
machine of the hour has a new smell. If yours
has an old out-of-date smell, there is more disgrace
on your head than if you had slain an inefficient
cook or bolted with your neighbor’s wife.
Mrs. Harry Levy acquaints her sister —Mrs. I.
Isaacs—with bright occurrences in Britain. The
Sydney charmer (I hear from other sources) has
been haunting Henley o’ Sundays, when myriads
of the gayest pole and punt upon the river. Also,
she has been testing the splendors of Ascot, and
comparing the glories of its meetings with Rand-
wick and Flemington Cup rallies, where the stage
management, at least, is more effective.
Mr. and Mrs. Ernest Brookes are due from
their big jaunt shortly. Toorak will be glad to
get the pretty trifler back again. Just at present
the prevalence of To-let signs among the Man-
sions of the Blest has a depressing effect upon the
stay-at-homes. Most eyes are mentally turned
on the distant capital, where the absentees spend
their good Australian money. No one is enter-
taining here. Nothing is doing. Half the
fashionables prepare for the 1907 gad, and the
other half sink their sorrows in Bridge.
An unconventional personage of great cash
tonnage appeared at a swagger function recently.
As he hadn’t sacrificed to the God of Graces (but
rather otherwise) for some time, a she-acquaint-
ance twitted him on his attendance this day.
“Well, I haven’t deserted respectable society
altogether,” he assured her. And he hasn’t.
More than that, Toorak, which pretends to be so
shocked at his unorthodox way of life, swarmed
round him like so many hungry bees.
The ambitious dame of one seasoned Federalist
is crowding out her husband’s library with books
on etiquette. Whether she should leave one
pasteboard or two after the party, or go in bereave-
ment rig to make a visit of sympathy, or use her
kerchief before or after meals, or manipulate an
orange with a small pick and shovel or a pair of
fingers, is the worry of her stylish hour. In vain
her loving spouse emits a grunt and says
“Bunkum!” to all her cares concerning these
punctilious points. Dishonor would appear th
this socially-yearning soul better than a slip in
etiquette. So she gathers together all the avail-
able books of polite conduct and correct behavior,
and hustles out philosophy and the poets from the
library shelves. The maids of the house, who
suffer by reason of the jam and the French
mustard being locked away, relate the story to
other Mary Anns, and so it circulates briskly.
Now the mischievous are looking for an excuse to
set the etiquette fiend tripping.
An awfully important skating expert is at pre-
sent agitating the mind and pulse of the Glacia-
rium dears. He’s fresh from the ice at Prince’s
Court, and returns thither in a month or two.
Professor Chambers has a graceful figure, and all
the appearance—with more humanity thrown in
—of ex-Private Secretary Fitzpatrick. His voice
has been cultivated up to a noble pitch, owing to
his pilotage o’er the ice of England’s fair aris-
tocracy. The local girl is paying all her pin and
powder money away to go a-glide with him.
Some superior literary extracts :
She drew his throat down and fitted her flower-mouth
to the sunburnt hollow.
He felt the necessity to grip on to something stronger
than himself.
“ You small sweet fool! ” he said, his voice so deep and
tender that it thrilled all through her.
These are from a book of the hour which I picked
up idly in a South Yarra drawing-room the other
day. It bore the name of the fashionable library
of Collins-street. lam told there’s a rush for it,
some passages being so devilled as to stimulate
the most blase feminine taste. Here is another
bit:—
And the moonlight was in her eyes, and the scent of
lavender in his blood, so he kissed her again on her bare
burning lips, his own on fire ; kissed her on her eyes, her
chin, her threat; kissed her till
No, the excitement is too great. One thing, the
chin-kiss has a smack of novelty. The chin has
been neglected. That feature (unless it has a
dimple) has so far passed amorous notice. But
“bare lips” suggest a query. Does the sex
generally wear clothes on its mouth ?
A Glaciarium carnival shortly calls for motley
wear and ornate rigs and fantastic disguises, and
the skittish who are versed in ice frolics are falling
over themselves in anticipation of the show.
Two dozen Brighton charmers are tired of wait-
ing for something festive or otherwise to happen ;
so they take matters into their own hands and
start ahead with a hop, to which they have asked
every eligible in the neighborhood, and every
good-looking Johnnie in this city.
Mrs. Otto Wunderlich and her medico-spouse
have been abiding at Menzies. The young Syd-
ney dame has a continental style of frocking that
captures the Melbourne eye. One day she
stepped forth in a gay crimson garb with lively
white plumes nodding from her Parisian hat.
Another time she knocked the Menzies dwellers
and subsequently the Glaciarium crowd with a
silver grey rig, fitting so closely that she seemed
to have been melted and poured into it out of a
jug, and a marvellous top-piece a-droop over a
classic profile and secured with a chin-scarf of
grey chiffon. No feminine has successfully worn
her chin in,a chiffon sling for years, but the Har-
bor charmer has a nose of such beauty that any
style of millinery invented would agree with it.
The doctor himself went a-skating regularly, and
the picturesque dame saw that no harm came to
him.
Many rushed to view the £lOO lace dress
flaunted by Mrs. R. G. Knowles. One day in the
green-room I counted fifteen females prying into
the beauties of the St. Petersburg mesh. The
owner, by the way, is a bright, knowing, cultured
dazzler. The French residents here went crazy
over her perfect Parisian accent and her know-
ledge of French literature. Apart from that, Mrs.
Knowles is the originator of the audacious show.
She urged R. G. to it and so helps him on to a
fortune.
Mrs. Ross-Soden distributes her Tyson wealth
lavishly. The other night the Cream rallied to
a call and disported gaily in the profusely-lighted
hall. (The dweller likes her mansion referred
to as “The Hall.”) Music and supper on a
banquet scale made the evening go swiftly.
Two of the most uncommonly pretty and chic
foreigners in this city are off to Sydney to startle
that capital with a beauty boudoir on an extensive
and expensive scale.
The student who leaves a girl behind him when
he goes on the grand gad is a rare specimen of the
first sex if he remains true to her for any length
of time. He almost always deserts. There
has been a recent instance of a pair tearing them-
selves apart on the ocean-liner —he to take a sad
countenance to Europe ; she to return home and
to mourn as David for his last girl. A big, bright
ring was the outward seal of their engaged
affections. It reminded other men that the girl
was booked. Travel, however, cleared the clouds
from his brow, and the Big Smoke turned him
suddenly gay. He was so lost to the sense of
decency that he used to pour his perfidious soul
out to the waiting Griselda in ecstasies over the
other girl. Then there were two sirens in his
ken. Then three, over whom he enthused with a
full, free and unthinking heart. At this stage
the fiancee checked his flow of eloquence, and re-
turned the gold circle.
Another Toorak warbler gets a help to Europe,
Mr. Charles Rose, the tuneful serenader who has
been hampered in his career from the start by a
pane in his eye. This glass is dropped in moments
of song, but it obstructs his countenance at other
times and worries the public. It also leads the
misguided artist to make grimaces in extracting
the top C, and this, too, is a worry to the onlooker.
A woman stalked down Bourke-street, the other
Saturday morning, tenderly carrying a small dog
of nondescript breed. Several tufts of the
quadruped’s hair were carefully done up in curling
pins:—evidently in preparation for some important
engagement later in the day.
Mrs. Oswald Watt is again in this city. The
charmer has thinned and lost some of her bloom
in Sydney.
Two smart sisters have open and public squabbles
over their gewgaws and such trifles, and become
enraged if one finds t’other coiling her tresses in
an imitative fashion. One will not stand being
copied by t’other. That’sdeplorable, and droll. But
there’s a more pathetic instance of sister rivalry
in the case of a specimen of the brute sex trans-
ferring his affections from one to t'other. He had
plighted his troth to the first angel befqre he met
tlie second. When he did encounter the cherub,
he immediately plighted some more, and the
spurned one is slowly starving herself to death,
while the inother of the rivals mingles salt tears
with her prayers that the brute may slip fatally
on a banana-skin and leave the family alone.
Sister-rivalry is a horrible thing.
Charles Rose managed—off his own bat—to
score a record attendance at his off-to-Europe
concert on Thursday. The serenader always had
the sympathy of the Tooraketty set, and for once
that set was as good as it promised. The show
resulted in the dressiest concert gathering since
the Melba time. Mrs. Jack Fisken, with a bush-
fire garb of vivid flashes, struck tlie boldest chord.
Miss Arrnytage was keyed (in a chiffon sense) up
to ball-room magnificence. The Bowes-Kelly
contingent and hosts of the most moneyed clans
sported raiment the like of which has never been
flourished in the Masonic Hall before—that is, for
a mere bill of strum and song.
The farewell Rose concert is given out solemnly
to be his last for many a songful season. The
story goes that Charles is bidden Bullwards to
clasp to his nervous bosom certain relatives of
classy and moneyed note. But the farewell show
is a movable feast and an abiding infliction. One
warbler in this city has farewelled and blubbered
between strains four or five times, and she still
goes briskly—or plumply, rather—on her way,
and looks good for a few more “ finals ” before
stepping aboard with the accumulated silver.
A reconciliation has been effected between an
estranged young couple, and their re-engagement
is announced. In the first place, the girl, who
was burdened with money, said Yes to Edwin at
her debutante hop. After the excitement of the
dance and the consequent congratulations had
died down, Angelina guessed she had rushed
things overmuch. So she returned the gold band
of hope to Edwin. After a season and a half of
pirouetting and several colds contracted by sitting
in the draughts for bijl-and-cooing purposes,
Angelina decided that the “yes’’ murmured
softly to her Edwin at the debutante hop was not
a mistaken monosyllable, and, the young man
being adjacent, negotiations were re-opened. Now
Edwin is rushing the heiress into matrimony 6re
she can change her mind once again.
My Adelaide correspondent writes
The Rymill-Blue marriage is billed to make a society
earthquake in Tortoise Town on September 25th. The
bridal raiment is to be of the gossamery chiffony
Parisian kind that makes you think the girl will be visible
through it, but she never is, and the trousseau, as a whole,
will be a never-to-be-forgotten joy. Adelaide’s girly ele-
ment will turn out in full force to catch a glimpse of the
show. Bride and groomlet will dwell somewhere between
Woodville and Seaton, an uninspiring neighborhood that
possesses only one attraction —it is near the golf links.
Both are wild golfers, and himself is secretary of the club.
So he will be able to keep an eye on the premises, and
see that nobody steals away the clubhouse, or the trees,
or anything.
The “just returned from Europe ” state seems to be a
permanent one with the local product that has once
learned to make a costly noise in furrin parts. Notice
(8., 26/7/’O6) that Miss Elsie Hamilton, the Adelaide
pianist, has “ just returned from study in Europe.”
May I be permitted to point out, with all due difference
—I mean deference—to Miss Tryphena that the damsel
was in exactly the same interesting position at least two
years ago. But that was in Adelaide. She may move on
to the Harbor in another two years, and be still just re-
turned from Europe. Anyway, it’s a good old advertise-
ment gag. Reminds me of the warbler who wanted to
make some cash. She had been living in another State
for some years, so she hastened back to her birth-place,
looked out some stray relatives, and got them to boom
her; and gave it out that she was going to Europe to
study. Her State arose and did its duty nobly, and she
gave a concert that brought in £lOO. Then she went
back to the parental dwelling, and there she remains.
That’s nearly two years ago, but never a sign of her has
poor old Europe seen ; and its disappointment in this
matter is the chief cause of the disturbances in Russia
and other troubles.
Another Tortoise Town warbler has been gathering in
laurels and bay-leaves in the Fog Village. Ethel Hancke,
studying at the Royal College of Music, London, has just
gained the Anne Lloyd Exhibition for her top note
against all comers. The damsel has a fine voice, and there
are plenty of parental shekels behind her, so she ought
to do well. She held a scholarship at the Elder Conserva-
torium (Adelaide) for three years before going Fogwards.
Also, she was engaged to warble at the State concert
when the Yorks (afterwards the YValeses) were starring in
these parts ; and most of Adelaide’s warbling profession
was very annoyed about it. The idea of allowing a mere
amateur to yodel to a York or wail to a Wales was said by
some to be adjacent to disloyalty. But the Yorks heard
her and survived.
Said that one Government House official was much
annoyed, because when walking down the street the
police did not salute him. A complaint to this effect was
sent to the Commissioner of Police, and now all the
Johns have been presented with photos, of this especial
flunkey so that they may touch their hats to the Truly
Great Man. A salute order, with photo, attached, has
been issued to the beadles at the Post Office, Town Hall
and other Government offices, so the outraged gentleman
will have a sufficient supply of grovel, abasement and
salaam in future.
Yours Affectionately,

dress and at the same time totally neglect their com-
plexions ; or, what is infinitely worse, cover a rough, coarse
skin with injurious paint and powder—a subterfuge
apparent to all but the most vulgar ! In times gone-by
such foolish methods were excusable ; but science has
made such strides in recent years that ladies troubled
with wrinkles, pimples, freckles or any facial blemish
need no longer worry. They have now a most reliable
and dainty cure in Patti’s Beauty Cream, which has added
three years of the highest . Australasian reputation to a
quarter of a century of the most distinguished European
appreciation as the only perfect skin food and complexion
beautifier. It is found on the toilet tables of every lady
of refinement, because it is absolutely pure, harmless,
and a perfectly natural and never-failing remedy for ali
complexion troubles, no matter how bad they may be. It
instantly clears the skin of all pimples, rash, &c., caused
by injurious concoctions and imitations against which
ladies are cautioned. All chemists and stores, price 3s. 6d.
and 6s. per pot.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
Bent, in moving the second reading of the
new Vic. Licensing Bill, mentioned a characteris-
tic clause in the political gospel of Duncan
Gillies. Seems that Duncan long ago warned him
“never to touch a Licensing Bill or a tariff.”
The wisdom of the Tory place-hunter lay in his
determination to have nothing to do with
meashres calculated to touch the pocket of the
fatman importer or the bloated beer-trader.
THE SHIP INDUSTRY. IN DISTRESS.—
THE INADEQUATE LIGUT.
“ I have no hesitation in saying that I and
my party are out for the abolition of the Legisla-
tive Council.”— Commendable statement by Labor-
leader Archibald, of s.A.
Formal question and answer regarding the
Butter Commissioirput in Victorian Parliament
Was Mr. Croker paid for his services ?
Mr. Croker was paid.
Our oath ! How could Mr. Croker buy his corsets
if he were not paid ?
Sydney Telegraph has had a new line
over its Foreign-trade and other leaders lately—
“ Daily Telegraph Stories.” That puts it
mildly, of course ; still, it shows some perception
of the position.
“ G. H. Reid,” complains a Queenslander,
“is too artful a campaigner. We’d have given
him a warm time at his meetings, have greeted
him for the clown that he is, but he always had
his wife and daughter;there! We had to be de-
cently civil to him for their sakes.”
The Federal penny post represents a con-
cession to the richer classes—the mercantile com-
munity especially— at the expense of the general
taxpayer, which should not be made until the
financial situation is clearer.
Apparently the solitary decision arrived
at by the N.S. W. Government, so far, on Justice
Owen’s Land Scandals report is that Under-
Sec. MacFarlane shall not return to his billet in
the Lands. Robert McDonald, one of the
Western Lands Commissioners, is at present
“doubling"’ the two parts. Thus does Mac
follow on the heels of Mac.
Arthur Griffith, in N. S. Wales Parlia-
ment, revives his Bill to assure to the widow and
children of a testator maintenance out of his
estate. A living man’s property must maintain
his wife and children; it is absurd, as well as
unjust, that a dead man’s estate should be relieved
of that liability if he is hunks enough to will all
his property away from his family.
The gabble on Rananaland’s Address-in-
Reply fizzled out soon enough to make it almost
a record for brevity. Last year the farce ran into
five or six weeks; this year it lasted two days—
two wasted days. Hungry Forsyth was mainly
responsible for showing up the ridiculousness of
the whole business. For four hours he insisted on
saying that the present prosperity of Queensland
was due to Queensland being prosperous ; and
after he had repeated that in 217 variations, the
House went out to yarn ; so when the Speaker put
the question there was no response, and the farce
was over. Even Forsyth has his uses, impossible
as it might appear.
Premier Kidston’s land monopoly tax pro-
posal for Bananaland is so painfully modest that
it, is hard to conceive any serious opposition—
except on the ground of its painful modesty. It
is only to apply to estates required for closer
settlement; and the money thus raised is to be
handed to the shire councils for road construction
through subdivided areas. Some members of
the old Morganite party got such a fright from
their constituents last year, after their support
of the Land Monopoly Tax Bill, that Kidston
has decided to go slow; and his present
scheme is to show the farmers that the tax won’t
touch them, but only the men who are blocking
the farmers. As an object-lesson in the effect of
a tax to make land available for the farmers, even
this poor little measure ought to be helpful in a
poor little way.
Vic. M.L.A. Outtrim re-introduces his
slaughtered innocent of last session—the Juvenile
Smoking Prevention Bill, which prohibits the
selling, giving or supplying of tobacco in any
form to children under 16. It is a simple enough
measure, and the only persons at all likely to
oppose it won’t have a vote for some years, and
shouldn’t be considered even if they had ; where-
fore it is a fearsome commentary on the ways of
legislation that a Bill of this sort should have to
come before Parliament three or four times before
it is passed.
The suggestion that the Commonwealth
should appoint its own J’s.P. is likely to bear
fruit. Before, however, any official
step is taken, the form and feature of
the creature should be determined. If
he is to be the State J.P. invested
with Federal power, he will be a
worse affliction than he now is. The
freak will simply have a wider
sphere, and be more bumptious and
insufferable. If the Commonwealth
makes its own appointments, but
follows State precedent in its methods,
the Australian J.P. and the State
J.P. will be an ignoble pair of
brothers. The only way to avoid ex-
tending the sphere of the fraud and
fool, or creating a new batch of frauds
and fools, is for the Australian Par-
liament to deliberately fix the duties
the persons are to perform and
designate the kind of men suited to
discharge the duties. If the reason
for appointing J’s.P. be that they
may witness signatures in out-of-the-
way places, then men appointed for
that purpose should have no authority
to go on the Bench. And any mem-
ber of the Federal Parliament who
solicits the Government to appoint
his creature to a Federal J.P.-ship
should be named (disrespectfully) by
the Speaker.
“If a man is willing to work,
and cannot find employment, then
the State should either have to find
him work or give him sufficent money
to enable his wife and children to
live. That sounds like revolution, I
know, but if you think it out care-
fully, I would like to know what
Christian argument you can bring
against it.”— Swinburne, Vic. Minister for Water
Supply.
Though Australia has only a theoretical
interest in the question of the establishment of an
Irish Parliament, about which question two Irish
envoys are now holding forth in this country, it
is probably safe to say that three-fourths of the
Australian people have sympathies in that direc-
tion. As things stand now, it looks as if not only
Ireland, but England and Scotland also, must soon
have local Parliaments; the great, unwieldy
Legislature at Westminister is bogged in a mul-
titude of details, and, as one of its own members
recently remarked with bitterness, the old, worn-
out, overworked machine is only fit for the scrap-
heap. And it is certainly time that Ireland had
a chance to try if, as regards some of' the details
of government, it can’t do better for itself than
England has done for it. England has put up in
Ireland this amazing record, that it has made it
the one white man’s country on earth that doesn’t
seem worth living in. It is the only white man’s
country where a vast and continuous process of
depopulation is going on, and, if that result is
anything to go by, British rule in Ireland is worse
than Russian rule in Poland, or Spanish domin-
ance in Cuba. And, if English rule in Ireland
should not be judged by its capacity for making
the country a desert, it is up to England to
explain what it should be judged by.
SETTLED!
I am prepared to admit that Protection is the settled policy.— GKORGE REID.
AUSTRALIA: “ It is not properly settled yet, George."
GEORGE: “ Well , let me at it with that axe, and it soon will he.”
. New Customs and Excise duties on spirits
were brought to the Federal Parliament with a
sudden rush—to prevent the revenue being preju-
diced by clearances to avoid the higher duty.
They represent adequate—or nearly adequate—
Protection to the Australian spirit-distilling in-
dustry, and will, no doubt, very soon make the
general Australian nip a thing of local production.
The Government should deal gs courageously and
as promptly with the machinery section of the
Tariff Commission’s report—shortly to be pre-
sented.
“ Hamer’s ” complaint:—
After Bunker— l mean Bruntnell—had got in for Surry
Hills, on a small majority, a pleased individual came up
to me and said it was a great victory for Freetrade. He
said that the fact that Bruntnell was a “good” man and
a teetotaller make it a victory for Freetrade. He told
me that Protection was ruining the country, and asked
me to look at the unemployed and himself. So I looked
at himself. The man was wearing a suit of English cloth,
an American hat, American hoots and Brummagem
jewellery. He was smoking a Manila cigar, which he lit
with Japanese matches, and his tie was of Chinese silk.
He invited me to have a Scotch whisky or an English
beer to celebrate the approach of good times and the vic-
tory of Freetrade.
The Owe’Sullivan appeared in a new
guise last week as leader of a protest in the
TV XKT i. • ,1
JN.b.W. Parliament against the
changing over from the old to the
new Sydney railway-station on
Sunday—the most suitable of all
days, since the traffic is then
smallest and the danger least.
Perhaps the Owe’Sullivan was
concerned because an unobtrusive
Sunday opening would stand in
the way of an official blare, with
champagne and an Owe’Sullivan
oration. Perhaps he was only
making friends with the Mam-
mon of Holy Joeism in view of
the result of the Surry Hills elec-
tion. Whatever his object, it
was a poor sort of posturing for
a man who once tried to bulk
large in the Democratic crowd.
And it was well matched by
Joseph Carruthers’ attitude in
reply—a pitiful yes-no uttered in
the tone of a worm weeping on a
clod. The changing over from
one station to another must be done one day. To
give Sunday the honor might have minimised the
inconvenience and the danger to human life.
Sydney courts are becoming so choked
with libel actions, many of them mere advertise-
ments, that it would be a fair thing for Parliament
to charge so much a day for the use of the J udge
and the premises.
Iceberg Irvine, of Victoria, in his capacity
as a prospective Federal legislator, thinks the
election of Senators by the people should be
abolished and the matter entrusted to the State
Parliaments. Which is one of the reasons why
Iceberg Irvine is neither use nor ornament—only
a sloppy, melting, thawing, dripping circum-
stance.
From the Press gallery of the Reps. :
A large, fat figure entered the House of Representatives
last Thursday (August 1) and rolled slowly round the
seats with a round, gleaming piece of glass stuck in its
eye. It marched from man to man, shaking hands, and
talking in high, audible tones, and taking no notice of
anybody who happened to be speaking. It moved
heavily from the room, to be interviewed by a frenzied
Argus reporter. It came back to the chamber, to
create more local disturbance while other men
were speaking, and Knox, Joe Cook, and Dugald
Thomson gathered round ic lovingly. Pumpkin
Smith—alas !—was away, By-and-bye the huge, spheri-
cal figure rose, and began'to talk. It had refused
to listen to anybody else, but was insulted to find that no
quorum was present to hearken to its weighty words, so
it called for a quorum. Then it remarked (among other
things) that Australian legislation was a By-word among
the Civilised Nations; that we were tottering beside a
Precipice of Socialism; that the cry of “Australia for
the Australians ” was wicked and selfish ; that we had a
Mother Land; and that the Labor party' was a Tiger,
holding the Executioner’s Whip over the head of the
Ministry. By the time the massive, rotund Shape had
made three speeches, and had renewed these statements
in each speech, it was realised by all present that Reid
had returned for a day.
Sydney Evening News, after a brief spell
of comparatively liberal tendencies, has gone
back to its old parochial wallowings. Last week
it rose in an indignant leading article to denounce
the proposal of the Australian Government to
vote £50,000 for the encouragement of immigra-
tion as tending to destroy N.S.W.’s “identity ”
and place it in “fetters.” It is amazing in what
unlikely places theparochialistcan find “fetters”
lying about.
“Z.Z.”: I played the looker-on at the
recent Surry Hills (Sydney) contest, and feel sad.
Expected to hear some statesmanlike utterances
on finance, legislation, and administration.
Heard, instead, one candidate promise to have the
local tram-fare reduced to a penny. Another
exhibited a woman he had bailed out 26 times.
Another told the publicans he was out to give
them fits. Such were a few of the candidates’
claims to be elected. Among the crowd I heard
one candidate accused of writing for a sectarian
paper. A barracker stated his man was an Irish-
straliancatholic, while the other reptile was
backed by the Protestant mob. If the above is a
fair sample of State politics, I think we could do
without a State Parliament.

The shepherd roaming o’er the plain,
The stockman on his lonely run,
The teamster with his bullock wain,
The sportsman out with rod or gun,
The digger on gold-bearing reef—
While firm to duty’s call they stand—
For coughs and colds they find relief
With Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure at hand.*

NOT ENTIRELY INCORRECT!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. G. If. Reid denies that he has any idea of bringing
pauper labor into Australia, and says the statement is e
slander.
Let’s consider the position
For a moment, Mr. Reid !
You’re an agile politician—
That is cheerfully ai reed ;
Yet one thinks, with hesitation,
But with total disrespect,
This offensive allegation
Is not wholly incorrect.
: Not that you’d bring pauper labor
To Australia, not at all 1
If you drummed upon that tabor
Who would hearken to the call ?
One must grant that such a slander
Is, as stated, foul and mean ;
But, one asks, in simple candor,
What your policy has been ?
Oh, we grant you, portly neighbor,
That you are most wrongly charged
With a wish to import labor !
The indictment is enlarged !
For your policy has ever
(Has it not, now ?—Yes or No)
Been a strenuous endeavor
To enrich the foreign foe !
Though you’d not bring pauper labor
(That’s too foolish to discuss),
You’ve a policy, my neighbor,
1 ‘ ZNT ON PAUPERISING US !
v ,iich is why, with hesitation,
Yet with perfect disrespect,
I submit the allegation
Is not wholly incorrect!
N.S. W.
W. T. GOODGE.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Veterans’ Race on Parramatta River
on Saturday' was an event that no State save N.S.W.
could have put on exhibition—three world champions,
one of them unbeaten ; together with five others of the
world’s very best, who onl.v missed the premiership of the
planet by’ a hair’s breadth, and beat all comers till the
world-champion himself put them down, and did it with
difficulty'. The afternoon was lovely’, and the course,
from Abbotsford to Mortlake picnic grounds, was like a
sheet of glass. Sydney' turned out en masse to see its old-
time mighty' scullers in their last great race of one-and-a-
half miles,* and took an enthusiastic farewell when
the navy’ blue jerse.v of Peter Kemp led
the way home, with * the blue and white of
Elias Laycock behind, and the black and yellow of
William Beach third. They started beautifully, Richard
Green, burdened with the weight of 70 years, receiving a
minute’s handicap. Then Michael Rush shot away with
25secs. handicap, followed by Elias Laycock with 12.
Reynolds had eight seconds, and Pearce six. Kemp, the
winner, Beach, and M’Lean were on scratch. Green
pulled wonderfully’ for a man of his age, but lost his
bearings, and went too far over to the opposite shore.
Kemp and Beach came away with a rush worthy
of much younger men. But old man Green kept
in front to the gasworks, and then Kemp headed
him, with the rest in a bunch close behind.
Towards the finish Laycock began to close up, and
the excitement boiled over, causing the watching boats
to press close to the course and get mixed up with the
scullers. The Government steamer fouled Laycock, and
threw him out of his swing altogether. The scullers
scrambled out of it somehow and resumed the race, which
Kemp won by half a length from Laycock, with Beach
three or four lengths behind. The display of stamina
was remarkable. Grandpapa Green finished second last.
Somewhere about 80,000 people were scattered along the
banks, and on steamers and miscellaneous craft. The
official steamers were well patronised, and the takings go
to swell the fund for distribution among the veterans.

famous Australian veteran scullers. The one at
