Sydney Metropolitan Rugby competition started
on its double-barrelled second round on Saturday. In A
division Glebe kicked St. George to the tune ofjlB to nine.
Balmain and South Sydney wiped the floor with each
other impartially, and finished a drawn game with three
each. Newtown danced on Manly, inflicting 19 to three.
In B division Sydney slew Eastern Suburbs needlessly dead,
nine to nil, Western Suburbs stamped on North Sydneyby
12tp three, and University had the bye. When this mix-u’p
was sorted out Glebe was found to be still at the head of
the poll by an increased margin, with a credit of 21
points. The second place is occupied by no less than foui-
teams abreast—Eastern Suburbs, South- Sydney, Univer-
sity, and Newtown, with 17 points each. Sydney is
third, alone, with 15 points; Balmain, fourth, with *l3 ;
Western Suburbs next, with 11 ; and thence to the tail
single figures,
! * * *, *
Baimjain has shown an improvement that is
simply amazing, and against South Sydney the forward
division played a beautiful game, dribbling perfectly in
spite of hard ground, scrimmaging with sound judgment
and tackling with certainty and effect. The back
division also worked admirably, but its combination
was not jewelled in as many holes as that of the for-
ward crowd. South Sydney forwards were completely
out-classed, but the back division was good, or else it had
been a day of terrific downfall. Balmain opened with a
five man charge, which was only stopped close up by
the last man being thrown into touch. South next
worked down the touch-line till Balmain heeled and set
going another charge that was carried right over by Wil-
cox after a run in which he dodged like an excited taran-
tula. The shot at goal failed. Then South did some
beautiful kicking, and by first-class generalship got
within 12 yards of the goal, right in front. Before it could
gtrike, Balmain heeled out to another counter-charge
that was only brought to grass by the swift-footed
Douglas overtaking Balmainer Whitting and downing him.
A terrific struggle took place right on the line, Balmain
getting across four times only to be thrown out again
before it could fall on the ball. Then somebody
kicked clear to the Balmain full-back on the 25. He
promptly’ kicked for the goal, but it flew low, and South
Sy'dney worked it out to half-way, and gasped for breath.
Balmain had also felt the strain of that effort, and while
the water-side suburb was taking a deep whiff of
oxy'gen, South man Fry suddenly rushed through, to be
brought down two y'ards from the line. Then another
struggle took place, on Balmain’s line, and South Sydney
got across by sheer force twice, only' to be rushed out
again before it could fall down on the leather. Balmain
worked back to half way, where both parties held their
hearts and gasped. Next South pack heeled. Three-
quarter M'Cabe got hold, and showed Balmain how to
dodge. He slipped through them like cash through a
gambler’s fingers, and headed straight for the full back
with a support running on the outside. Tackled he
passed like a flash, and the scores were equalled. The
second spell showed much hard work done but no scores.
* * * *
Sydney opened its match against Eastern
Suburbs (B Division) with a series of passing charges
that came to grief mostly through forward passing.
THE VETERAN SCULLERS,
who formed Saturday's unique exhibition
on Parramatta River.
Photo, by Eden Studios, George-st., Sydney.
Play was bright, but marred by unscrupulous conduct on
thepart of Sydney, a few of whose players need to be
passed out for reckless kicking. For Suburbs, Messenger
played a great game, and sent Sydney back again and
again by cool, accurate line kicking. Then Suburbs
charged, Messenger taking the ball himself three times
between the two 25’s. But Sydney managed to secure
and punt out. From a mix-up on the corner of the 25,
Sydney suddenly potted a beautiful goal just on half-time.
Score, four to nil. The second half was very fast indeed,
Eastern Suburbs playing desperately to equalise things.
A rapid succession of furious charges caused Sydney to
force four times in five minutes. Some astounding marks
also helped,to save Sydney’s hogflesh, and a sudden rush
took the leather right to Suburbs’ line, where it was
deliberately taken out of the scrum and whipped across
the line. The extra points were added, and the game
ended in a victory for Sydney by nine to nil. Eastern
Suburbs had at least the consolation of knowing that,
badly as Sydney bad lickqd it, it had “ done ” the referee
much worse.
All that is best in the Australian game of foot-
ball was on display at Sydney Cricket Ground on Satur-
day afternoon, when the St. Kilda (Melb.) team met an
18 drawn from the combined teams of Sydney League’s
first grade clubs. The Sydney team was merely a chop-
ping block on which the visitors demonstrated the fine
art to which they have brought their game. They pass
with their feet, the ball travelling right across the
ground in a succession of short,-accurate kicks, executed
with amazing quickness and skill. No matter how
high a ball soars a St. Ilildean seems to be able
to jump high enough to get it. The Australian
game, as these Melbournians play it, certainly is foot-
ball. The hand plays a very minor part in it.
Truly, they do juggle with it somewhat; but it is the
foot that is relied on for the serious business of the game.
Still, looking at it with an impartial eye, this writer fails
to see that it is one scrap less dangerous than Rugby,
and the jumping for glorious high marks is most
risky. Nevertheless, played properly, it is an inter-
esting thing to watch. It partakes more of the nature of
human draughts or chess, whereas Rugby is a miniature
battle • and the instincts of a thousand years of close
order and charging en masse probably explain why the
Rugby game appeals more to of the spectators.
The Vic. Football League, which has a match
arranged with Ballarat this coming Saturday, perceiving
that any old team will meet all likely demands, decided
not to lose a good “gate,” and postponed two of last
week’s games for seven days. This has grievously vexed
the Association, which thought its match with South
Australia was going to be the sole attraction for that
Saturday. Consequently the two bodies are niore un-
friendly than ever; also the S.A. Association is being
drawn into the quarrel. The trouble is of course the old
human weakness that makes the stronger man want the
bigger half of the loaf. There being thus only two League
games last week-end, a 40,000 crowd distributed
itself between the ’Wood v. Essendon and South v .
Geelong tussles, and the former’s ground was densely
packed. The crowd got its money’s worth in the finest
and fastest game seen this season, played with sufficient
vim and sufficient luck to make it interesting up to 10
minutes before the bell rang. ’Wood started well by
kicking two goals, but for the rest of the first quarter
shot after shot just missed the posts, the score then
being two goals 10 behinds to Essendon s one behind.
Then the ’Dons woke up, and half-time saw them 23
points to 35. They kept battling steadily thereafter, but
'Wood had got its system going to perfection-
no man holding the ball longer than was necessary
to pass it on by a short, low kick or a punch,
to the handiest man in front—and the pace finally found
the opponents’ weak spots. In the last 10 minutes the
Essendons were played to a standstill, and ’Wood put on
three goals more, just to make sure, the tally being
’Wood nine goals 15 behinds to five goals seven behinds.
South Melbourne, flushed with last week’s victory over
Carlton, played the same 18, and walloped Geelong
handily by 101 points to 3S. In the Association games
West met and ran over the weak Ports by 132 points to
27 ; North maintained its lead by' scoring 31 points to
Footscray’s 27, after a hard, good game ; and Richmond
had the better of its argument with Williamstown by'37
to 25, after a most interesting game, in the third quarter of
which the discussion became so unfriendly that the
umpire had to stop play and caution several players
against mistaking their opponents for the ball. Just how
accidents happen to players of the Australian game is
explained by a couple of mishaps that recently put
two men temporarily on the sick list. An
Essendon man, springing for a high
mark, drove his chest so
violently against
a mate’s knee
§ that he was badly
I knocked out, and,
after the match,
was spitting blood;
while a Carlton
man, doing a simi-
lar leap, twisted
and sprained an
ankle in coming
down. There was
no suspicion of
rough play about
these disasters, but
they invalided the
men for quite a
while, just the same.

Saturday was an important day in Sydney
British Association circles. If Glebe could beat Pyrmont
it meant that Balmain had a chance of winning the
premiership. But Glebe had never a hope, although it
started with a rush, and pressed home a well sus-
tained attack. Pyrmont put up an invincible de-
fence, and kept its eye open for a counter attack.
The opportunity came suddenly, and was snapped
at once. Undismayed by its bad luck Glebe attacked
once more, but lost control of the leather, and
before it could recover itself, found that its own goal was
in danger. Pyrmont broke through a second time just
as the spell ended. The second half consisted of a series
of attacks by Glebe, which were beaten back and followed
up. Only once did Glebe get through, and on that occa-
sion the Pyrmont goal-keeper mulled badly, missing the
ball altogether. The game ended in victory for Pyrmont
by six to one. Pyrmont’s score in the competition is 12
wins, one lost, one drawn, equal to 25 competition points.
Balmain is next with 10 wins, two lost, and none drawn,
and 20 competition points. It has two matches still to
play. Rozelle has one match yet to play, and is also the
proud possessor of 20 competition points, so it was not
very much interested in the defeat of Pyrmont. But it
meant a lot to Balmain. Other matches played on
Saturday were Sydney against South Sydney, won by the
latter with four goals to one ; Rozelle’s victory over
Ultimo by three to nil; and Balmain’s defeat of Granville
by two to nil. Now the premiership is decided, the con-
test for the Gardiner Cup will begin.
* * • *
The Yic. Full Court last week unanimously up-
held the appeal of bookmaker Oolman against Justice
A’Beckett’s decision that the V.R.C. was within its rights
in refusing the privilege of bookmaking at Flemington,
or in charging a fee for the privilege. Last Cup meeting
the club decided to raise Ikev Mo’s fee, for the paddock,
from £5O to £75. Ikev paid, but shortened his odds.
Colman, however, decided to fight, and three Judges have
now decided that “as the Y.R.C., under its Act of ’7l,
is permitted to hold the land for the purpose of main-
taining and using it as a public racecourse, it has no
manner of right to make fish of one and flesh of
another. Betting is not unlawful, and the club has
no power to make by-laws restricting a lawful act.”
Which means that if the Y.R.C. wants a fee from Ikey
for betting, it must call it an “admission fee,” and it
must charge the same fee to everybody, backers as well
as layers ; also, it means, incidentally, that the £9OOO it
collected last year in fees was gathered in illegally, and
may have to be refunded. In any case, there will be no
£9OOO, or even £9, this year. It is a frightful situation—
for the public as well as the V.R.C. For if everybody
who pays half-a-guinea can go into the paddock and
bet —and “balance” and welsh—there’ll be riotous times
at Flemington, and Judkins and Worrall will have some-
thing really worth raving about.
• • * •
Wade’sN.S. W. anti-gamblingßill closesall race-
courses with a running track of less than six furlongs in
circumference. That, of course, means six furlongs
round the outside edge, and the animal that gets
the inside running travels less than five-and-a-half
furlongs. Six furlongs, or three-quarters of a mile,
is altogether too short a course for any but the weedy
gallopers of the present-day racing decadence. Hart
an inside circumference of a mile been insisted on it
would have shut out one or two more of the minor race-
courses which ought to be closed in the interests of sport
and of the public.
* * » *
Realm must have been a mighty good thing for
the V.R.C. Grand National Hurdle, seeing that he was
able to win the corresponding event at Caulfield last
Saturday with another 241bs. on his back. For a moment
it looked as though top-weight Florin, meeting Realm on
231bs. better terms than before, would just manage to
turn the tables on him. They ran neck and neck at the
final jump. Florin was the first over, but in the run home
the Flemington hero wore him down and won by nearly a
length. The time (6.50) has onl.v once been beaten, by a
9-stone horse (Good Day). Considering that Realm
carried 11.1, the performance was distinctly the best on
record for the Caulfield race. York, last year, took 3sec.
longer in doing the same job with the same weight.
York was a “ rod in pickle ” for the double event. Realm
was even better pickled than the Adelaide champion who-
has since gone to England.
* * * *
Perhaps The General will reward his followers
for their loyalty' by taking the Caulfield G.N. Steeple-
chase next Saturday. He isn’t the safest jumper in the
field, but he has been round the stiffer course at Fleming-
ton, and it is “up to him” to win another big event.
With Beilis going rather badly on the track, The General
and The Cardinal look promising. The latter finished
second to Airly in a slow-run race two years ago.

Things were very much mixed over a race at
Rosebery Park (Sydney) pony meeting last Friday. Miss
Alma finished first in the big handicap, but before passing
the post her lead-bag (which made up the necessary load)
fell from the saddle, to be subsequently picked up and
placed on the scales with the jockey. The responsible
official declared “ weight ” and Mo paid out, only to find
that a mistake had been made—allowing the lead-bag in
the circumstances was decidedly wrong. The committee
then placed the second nag, Mona Lily, first, whereupon
Mo kicked vigorously, and swore by the beards of his
ancestors that he wouldn’t unload over two winners.
Once more the wise men controlling matters discussed
the situation, and, this time, ruled that Miss Alma’s
backers must be paid (in face of the fact that, through
finishing short weight, she should have been disqualified
altogether), and gave the prize to Mona Lily.
* * * *
Saturday’s Caulfield G.N. Hurdles’ winner,
Realm, was a profitable spec, for present owner, who paid
650 gs. for the great lepper, after it annexed V.R.O. G.N.
Steeple a few years ago, and has already won £9OO in
prize-money, independent of bets.

■ Plaister. Perhaps it is an obscure royal joke, though !

The final of the Dunlop Inter-Club premiership
under the N.S.W. Union was runoff on Saturday, on the
Homebush road. The competing teams were those re-
presenting ‘ the Enfield and Eastern Suburbs Clubs,
and consisted of four riders each. The road was
dusty and hard, and the blue metal cut the tyres griev-
ously, causing many punctures. Nevertheless it was a
great race, the ,40 miles being covered in lh. 56min.
12 l-ssee. The competitors were paced by selected riders,
and kept together wonderfully well. At the finish
three of each team Were all bunched together, and
there was a great sprint for home. Enfield rider Maxwell
just beat Thompson, of the Eastern Suburbs, with Blake,
also of E.S., close behind. Then came Gannon, of En-
field, followed by Nicholson, of Enfield. After that there
was another Enfield man sandwiched between two
Easterners. Enfield won by two points, and as this is its
second win, the shield now becomes the club’s own pro-
perty. Balmain has also a shield of its own, having won
the championship in 1902 and 1904.

Made Possible to All by the
Pianola Piano
THE PIANO THAT EVERYBODY GAN PLAY.
This wonderful instrument can be played
artistically and easily by anyone, even by those
music-lovers who Have no knowledge of piano-
playing, or who may not know one note from
another. Its repertoire includes all the music
♦here is, from a Strauss Walt* to a Beethoven
Symphony.
The principal numbers from all the current light opera and musical
■comedy successes of the day are at the instant command of the owner of a
PIANOLA PIANO.
Or, if more serious pleasures are desired, the whole world of music is
equally available. More than 15,000 Pianola Music Rolls are already
catalogued, including from this enchanted realm the works of all the
modern and classic masters.
THE PIANOLA PIANO is a high-grade standard upright
piano, into the case of which is built a Metrost.yle Pianola, the standard
player. The first complete piano, playable either by the non-musician by
means of perforated music rolls, or by the pianist by hand, at will.
THE METROSTYLE, while enabling one to render any com-
position in accordance with an authoritative interpretation, allows, with
equal ease, individual expression. It is also invaluable in that it enables
anyone to play artistically an unfamiliar composition at first sight, even
without any knowledge of music. Found only in the Pianola and the
Pianola Piano.
All makes of pianos taken in exchange at a fair valuation. A small

Witham had bad luck in Saturday's monthly
race for the Chamberlain Cup. (living to his exhaust
valve breaking, he never saw the way the race went.
Saunders also had troubles, his ignition machinery going
wrong and letting him down with a bump. Forest
Finlay, who won the first race and ran third in the
second, again came home two behind the winner. That
fortunate being was F. Daniels. Jarman, who won the
second race, ran second on Saturday, so the appearance
of Daniels is something like an irruption or a strange
bovine in the paddock.
* * * *
The coming of South African champion, Mike
Williams, to Australia was something of a surprise, and
Wren—who stands the expense—doesn’t know what to
■do with Michael, now he’s got him. When the chief engi-
neer of the tote wired for O’Brien of America, he thought
that the arrangement with Williams of Africa was off,
and neglected to cancel his offer to the latter. Such offer
was a purse and expenses to come and fight Squires.
When the fixture with O’Brien was reduced to documents,
a cable message fell in on Wren, announcing Williams’
acceptance of the other engagement, and stating that
Williams had taken to the water, bound for the Common-
wealth. Now Williams says he didn’t come here to fight
small fry, and it seems that Wren will have to keep him to
match with the winner of Squires v. O’Brien. There are
efforts to arrange a biffing bee for Mike with Peter Kling,
and Ed. Williams wants a cut at his namesake. This
proves Ed.’s advisers to be rather unwise. The small man
who sets up against slogging heavy-weights usually gets
heart trouble as a result of too much thumping, and is
afflicted with ring fright for ever after.
* * * *
That Peter Felix is losing the ability to hit a
knock-out blow is very evident by the way Peter Kling
survived the pounding that the scientific black gave him
under meat-yard rules at Sydney National Sporting Club
last week. It wasn’t a fight—it was a rough-and-tumble,
and should have been stopped under the Queensbery
regulations. From the first, it was evident that any
science that was going to be shown would be supplied bv
Felix. Kling was merely a leaping wild animal, whom
anybody with the strength to hit one real, sledge-hammer
blow would have obliterated in short order. Felix landed
him full in the face again and again with bis dainty
left—but it was too dainty. Ivling’s head always
got back to its original position after being knocked
half way down his hack, and he would get in close and
chop, chop. They got hold of one another, and tried to
hold each other’s fists while bashing with their elbows.
They continued to twist and struggle long after the gong
had sounded, and the referee had to get into the ring and
haul them apart, and narrowly escaped getting punched
by both. In the second round they roared at one another
like bulls, and Felix’s wild whoops, while he held Kling
against the ropes and bashed him over the back, the
neck, head and sides, shook the building. Kling was
utterly beaten, and the referee should have stopped the
fight and declared the black the winner. But Kling
got to his corner, and recuperated, and came back to a
round that was simply indescribable. When Kling
knocked Felix down the referee had to hold him back
from the prostrate man while he counted. After that,
Felix went to the floor repeatedly. In the fourth round
Kling’s face was never free of the black fist while they
-were within distance. But the white got in close, and
banged away with both hands on the black body,
•while Felix grabbed his head and tried to screw it right
round. Kling got loose somehow, and beat the black man
to the boards twice, after which the referee suddenly
realised that he had some authority, and declared Kling
winner. There are no points about that method of
-fighting. It is interesting and amusing for the fellows
■outside the ropes, hut it is foolishness for the ones inside.
In the majority of cases it would have been stopped.
Kling ought to have been disqualified several times in the
first round and a half. Kling has plenty of pluck, and if
he looks after himself may yet do well; hut if he tries
last week’s methods on some cool-headed, hard-hitting
■opponent, he will probably be brushed away in the middle
■of his first roar.
* * * *
The bruiser who, “to oblige the club,” meets a
man who is obviously much lighter than himself, has an
unpleasant row to hoe. If he knocks the little man out
in short order, a refined and sympathetic audience calls
nun a “ cow,” or something even more inelegant. If he
lets the little fellow down lightly, the chances are that a
sympathetic referee will reckon he ought to have made a
better showing- against so light an opponent, and perhaps
award the fight to the limiter man “on points.” Then
the hard-jowled persons, who. laid odds on the big fellow,
howl for his blood, and the enthusiastic crowd hoots
him as a ninny and a busted Goliath. This was
the predicament in which Charles Griffin, late of
Maoriland, found himself when he met Ted Green
of Balmain, at Sydney Gaiety, on Saturday night.’
Mr. Griffin was obviously much heavier—he looked
17ib. or 181 b. weightier than the Sydneyite. The an-
nounced weights made the difference a stone. Mr. Green
had evidently studied the tactics of the fate Mr! Napoleon
Bonaparte, and tried to defend himself by putting Mr.
Griffin on the defensive. The Maorilander did not realise
that he was being made a fool of for about a minute, and
then he inserted a few uppercuts that had a singularly
disquieting effect. In the second round Mr. Griffin
settled down to business—be was not there to show
chivalrous consideration, or’to prolong the agony in any
way. The man in front of him was there to be smit,
and Mr. Griffin proceeded to smite. His thoroughness
roused the anger of the crowd, which asked the Almighty
to sky if He had ever seen anything like it. Receiving no
satisfaction, it took to alternately cursing and hootin'*
Griffin. He fell on Green like a landslip, hi's blows hailed”,
and Green fled from the downpour as cattle flee before a
blizzard. Griffin pursued, and bashed and banged with
energy. The crowd hooted and called on its gods, forget-
ful that Green could end it all by the simple expedient of
hoisting the towel. Griffin cared little for their blame or
praise—they had paid their money before they came in, and
that was all the interest he took in them. When the vic-
tim came up for the third round he attacked Griffin with
considerable suddenness, driving home a couple of weighty
shocks that made the cyclonic Maorilander rock. But it
was only momentary—then Green hit the floor with a
bang, and stayed there listening dimly to the roars and
hoots with which the sympathetic but fool audience
irreeted his conqueror.
* * * *
Lightweight “Monty” Andrews, of Sydney,
dealt out a scientific hiding over twenty rounds to “.Ted”
Whiting, at the Cyclorama building (Melb.) t’other night
for a £6O purse. Andrews weighed in some few lb. the
lighter of the pair, but Whiting’s slight advantage in
weight and height did not counterbalance the knowledge
displayed by the Sydney boy, who made his opponent
look a mere novice. Proceedings opened bv “Monty”
shifting about and using both hands with aptitude and
effect. Whiting, out-classed in the legitimate game,
resorted to rushing and wrestling tactics, and endeavored
to beat his man in the in-fighting, but “ Monty ” covered
and stopped so well that the blows counted for nothing.
In the fourth round Whiting, with a bull-like rush,
pushed the Sydneyite in a corner against the ropes, and
rained a shower of blows, mostly on the body. If the
required power had been behind them, Andrews, Esq.,
would have been in a delicate state. Several times
Whiting went to the hoards on his own account,
but referee Stokesbury requested him to box on. The
referee’s decision (after the full 20 rounds) was a foregone
conclusion.

With the advent of August Australia starts
what is officially known as the “ new racing season.” Con-
sidering that the crowd which follows the stiffened moke
and the steed that is in the hag can attend a race meeting
of some sort or another almost every day of the week all
the year round, why start a new season ? Why not just
let the old season go right on ?

Victorian Owners’ and Trainers’ Association
last week passed a resolution.favoring the legalisation of
the totalisator. It took the common or horse-sense
view that the suppression of gambling- was impracticable,
and the talk about it just so much waste of the time
which is money. Therefore the aim of those who control
racing should be to give punters an honest run for their
money, and to eliminate from the gambling business the
unhealthy elements of welshing, balancing, swearing,
drinking, cursing, pulling horses, running stiff, and
other needless and unpleasant circumstances. Reed, the
chairman of the Association, reckoned that bookmakers’
profits could not be less than £140,000 a year. He quoted
the case of one book who cleared out to England with
£3,5,000, leaving behind rows of houses and clusters of
pubs, from which he still draws a corpulent income. G.
G. Stead, the Maoriland owner, was brought up as an
advocate of the honest public tote. Indeed, every re-
spectable owner and trainer favors it. He gets better
odds and the machine doesn't get at his boys and hire
them to pull a favorite. The racing clubs’ themselves
naturally want it. The V.R.O. is crying for it, and
as far as punters are concerned the success of John Wren
and the Oollingwood tote is sufficient evidence to con-
vince any sensible man as to whether the betting public
piefers tire bookie or the machine. Unfortunately in
Victoria just now every parson with a loud voice is
shrieking at the top thereof for pro’hibitiori of betting—
“the stamping out of the curse of gambling.” Bent’s
blundering’ in the Worrall. case lias made the reverend
personages feel that they are the power that can move
the Legislature. Bent has introduced a Drink Bill and is
about to introduce a Gambling Bill, and this legislation,
though Bent declares that he is doing it off his
own bat, is regarded by the Judkinses of Victoria
as the actual fruit of their raucous agitation. To
mention the legalisation of the tote just now is to call
down such violent indignation from the church that
many men prefer to drop the subject for fear the church
should burst in its excitement. Meanwhile the book-
makers cordially approve of Judkins and Worrall. The
only thing they are really afraid of is the tote, and 1 hey
reckon that Judkins and Worrall stand between them
and that ealamit}’.
* * * *
The crortk horse-owner sees trouble ahead of
him in Maoriland. The executive of the Racing Confer-
ence has been empowered to provide new machinery, so
that suspected eases of malpractice may be investigated
and dealt with by a permanent tribunal. The primary
idea is to license owners as well as trainers and jockeys, and
the possibility of the license being refused on the annual
renewal-day is expected to prove a very effective
check on the unscrupulous. The idea is a noble one, and
the individual who only has a holiday win when the price
suits will find a much bigger snag in his road than the
’ordinary namby-pamby club stewards who lire so prone
to return a verdict Of “ explanation satisfactory.” The
awful fear of having his license refused will haunt the
cronk owner’s dreams. Loss of his little document
would be tantamount to being warned off the turf, the
oidy difference being this : The old idea is that every
horse-owner has a right to be on the turf, and can only be
dragged off when his character is proved to be awful.
The new idea is that the whole boiling of horse-owners
are only on the turf upon sufferance, arid even if an owner
doesn’t seem bad enough to be directly warned off the
grass his license can he dropped on the ground that lie
isn’t good enough to be actively shoved on to the grass.
During last April 1763 immigrants were
refused admittance at New York—the front door
of that country which Bruce Sniff tells us welcomes
every body with open arms.
The plaints of a certain voluble crowd
about the sinfulness of opening the new Sydney
railway station on Sunday caused Joe Carruthers
to back down, as a matter of course. Joe always
backs down. The proposed Sunday opening
simply meant that the trains which, on any other
Sunday, would have rim into the old station,
would this Sunday run into the new one instead.
There was to be no opening ceremony gist the
usual number of trains. In fact, when a huge job
of that kind is on hand, there is no ti me for an open-
ing ceremony. Possibly, seeing that it was Owe’-
Sullivan who objected most loudly to the Sunday
opening, the trouble was more over the lack of
opening ceremony than over the Sabbath.
Anyhow, the plans were altered; the trains
began to run into the new station on
Saturday, before things were anything like
ready; some voluble people got a chance
to shoot off their mouths at great length;:
and, owing to the premature nature of things,
there was chaos. The people who must
blow off their loose eloquence when anything—
even a new dog-kennel—is Opened, have a lot to
answer for.
“ 0.K.” : If, as some have suggested,
Donald M’Leocl, late of Flemington racecourse,
died of fright he wasn’t the first human
of low grade whose fear of death killed
him before his neck was broken. There is
at least one well-authenticated case of a dead
man being hanged at Melbourne Gaol. He was
a Chow. As he stood on the drop-bridge he took a
melancholy survey of himself, and looked to the
right and left of him, and said, “Not to-mollow—
no more to-mollow.’’ As a matter of fact, there
was no more to-day for the poor wretch. The
hangman could feel him die as the white cap was
pulled over his face, and it was a lifeless form that
dropped into space when the lever was pulled.
All the spectators had seen the Chow shudder off
his mortal coil before he fell. If condemned sol-
diers confronted by a firing party have been
scared into “ dying before the gun goes off,” there
is fair ground for the supposition that “Big
Mick” MtLeod, a soft fellow with no heart for
work, gave up the ghost in a moment of mortal
funk.
The much-needed Chinese Employment
Bill, which aims chiefly to protect the white
cabinet-maker, has been re-introduced for the
manyth time in the Victorian Assembly. It
mainly provides that, after Ist January, 1907, no.
unlicensed Chow shall work in a factory ; licenses
to be annual and non transferable ; onus of proof
that any Chow found working in a factory is
licensed, to lie on such Chow ; a Chow employer
shall not board or lodge an employee, or be re-
gistered as a factory occupier unless licensed ; no
factory occupier shall employ an unlicensed Chow,
Licenses may be cancelled after third conviction.
REFORM WITHOUT REVOLUTION.—The great
and particular merit of Hunyadi Janos is that it
relieves without discomfort; in other words, it effects
reform without revolution. This is because it is the
best and safest natural aperient.*
Mount Gambier, where S. Australia grows
its potatoes, continues to be torn to the depths of
its Blue Lake; and almost every local resident
knows for sure that that is bottomless. When
Labor Premier and local preacher Tom Price was
down there weeks ago,, the W.C.T.U. gave a re-
ception to his wife ; and Thomas said some things
in the local-preacher manner about frivolous
females. Mount Gambler didn’t worry over it
much till the Tory wire-pullers pointed out that
this Labor person had imputed 72 deadly vices to
its wife and daughter. Every potato-grower in
the South was therefore urged to throw his biggest
and rottenest spud at the base calumniator. They
held a meeting, demanded an apology, and called
upon the Institute committee to wipe the name of
Price off its foundation stone. Price hasn’t been
able'to see anything to apologise for, and the
Institute trustees say they are not a political body
and are not going to be made into political tools.
The stone was therefore secretly removed; and later
on it was plastered over with tar and grease. The
Po'ato Union says it didn’t do it; but, all the
same, some of the members are quite furious
enough to do worse. Livingstone, the local’
M.LIA., said, at one meeting, that he was proud
of the Ml Gambier people because they did
not tear Price Iq pieces on the platform of the
railway station as he went away. A side-show is
provided by the W.C.T.U., which has pulled
its bonnet-strings to rags taking violent sides in
the controversy.
Shall hacking cough my rest destroy,
And all my pleasure here alloy?
Are pains that cut me like a knife
To make a misery of life?
Shall bronchial troubles wear me out ?
No—never, all are put to rout
By best of medicines, simple, pure,
W. E. Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.*
Native representation in the Maoriland
Legislature is an absurd anomaly, considering that
the sale of liquor to natives is prohibited in the
King country. The Maoris are treated as a race
of children. Kids return no members to Parlia-
ment. If the brown, native is never a man, accord-
ing to social regulation, he isn’t entitled to share
the white man’s political rights, yet he figures as
an infant at the pub and a free and independent
adult at the hustings.

Legal Notice.
PURSUANT to an Order of the Chancery
Division of the High Court of Justice made
In the matter of the Trusts of an Indenture of
Settlement dated the 7th day of August 1876 and
made between Silvain Eugene Trefond and others
and In the matter of the Estate of Sarah Franklin
deceased and In the matter of the Estate of
Elizabeth Jane Trefond deceased Trefond v.
Boustead 1900 T. No. 1118 and dated the 3rd
March 1902.
It was (inter alia) ordered that the following
Enquiries should be made namely.
An Enquiry who were the persons entitled by
virtue of or according to the statutes of distribu-
tion of Intestate estates or otherwise to the
estate of the above named Elizabeth Jane Trefond
living at the time of her death and whether any
of them are since dead and if so who are their
respective legal personal representatives.
An Enquiry who was the heir at law of the said
Elizabeth Jane Trefond at the time of her death
and whether such heir at law is living or dead and
if dead who by devise descent or otherwise is
entitled to such real estate (if any) of the said
Elizabeth Jane Trefond as descended to such heir
at law.
Now the persons claiming to be such next of kin
or the legal personal representatives of such of
the said next of kin as are now dead and heir at
law as aforesaid of the said Elizabeth Jane Trefond
late of 53 St. John’s Wood Terrace Regent’s
Park in the County of Middlesex (wife of the said
Silvain Eugene Trefond) who died on or about
the 20th day of October 1898 at Brighton in the
County of Sussex are by their Solicitors on or be-
fore the Ist day of November 1906 to come in and
prove their claims at the Chambers of Mr. Justice
Kekewich and Mr. Justice Joyce at the Royal
Courts of Justice Strand London England or in
default thereof they will be peremptorily excluded
from the benefit of the said order.
Thursday the Bth day of November 1906 at 12
of the clock at noon at Room 696 in the said
Chambers is appointed for hearing and adjudi-
cating upon the claims.
Dated this 25th dav of June, 1906.
RICHD. JOHN VILLIERS,
Master.
WALTER ADAM BROWN,
27 Lincoln’s Inn Fields,
London, England,
Plaintiff’s Solicitor.
Note. —The said Elizabeth Jane Trefond was a
daughter of James Naunton late of St. John’s
Wood, London.

Almost impossible to find in the House of
Reps, a Freetrader who is a Freetrader when his
own constituents are affected. On the Bounties
Bill, Freetrader Lonsdale suggested a bounty to the
farmer who lost his crops, Freetrade-Laborite
Brown suggested a bounty for developing rust-
resisting wheat, Freetrader Fuller (from the 111a-
warra dairy district) asked for the bounty on
condensed milk to be increased. And Freetrader
Wilks ie still anxious that the ships of the new
mail line should be built and repaired in his.
electorate, whether that represents the cheapest,
market or not.
The S.M. Herald barracks more and more
loudly for the importation of a Chief Railway-
Commissioner, from either England or America,
at an unlimited salary, on the grounds that an
Australian does not know what competition is
doing outside the Commonwealth to advance rail-
way management. Mr. James, on this reasoning,
ought to place his paper in charge of a Londoner
or New' Yorker used to journalistic competition
in those cities —a man who would be profoundly
ignorant of Australian politics and politicians,
and Australian conditions and aspirations. Why
doesn’t he?
The Foreign-trade push, with its usual
bitter hostility to all Australian manufactures,
has raised a shriek against the giving of bounties,
(under the Bounties Bill) for such articles as
ginned cotton, sweetened and condensed milk,
powdered milk, canned fish, flax fibres, and so on.
The shriek was based on the other shriek that
a huge outlay would be necessary in order to.
provide plant for these industries, and that conse-
quently all the bounty would go to rich mono-
polists, and never reach the farmer or producer,
for whose benefit it was intended. Minister
Lyne replies by a memorandum, which shows that
the plant required for the articles mentioned
would cost something like this Ginned cotton,
£2OOO ; sweetened condensed milk, £lOOO to £5000;.
powdered milk, £2OO ; vegetable oils, £2OO ; tinned
fish, about £200; fibres, the same or less. Obvi-
ously, most of these amounts are within the means,
of a single grower ; all are well within the capacity
of a farmers’ or fishermen’s co-operative company.
So it was just the Foreign-trade lie once more.
Goldsbrough, Mort and Co., report: A quiet
week has been experienced in the local wool market, few
transactions taking place. Advices respecting the new
clip are coming forward daily, shearing having commenced
in many of the early districts. Latest reports from
Bradford show that manufacturers and spinners, although
well employed and having orders on hand for months
ahead, are operating with great caution, preferring only
to buy to meet their immediate requirements. The “ top ”
market is quiet, super GO’s. being quoted at 271 d., and
common 60’s, at 261 d. These prices, however, are in
advance of those ruling at this time last year, and present
indications in consequence point to satisfactory prices
being obtained during the coming season.-*
N.S.W. Premier Carrufchersshould admin-
ister a tonic to that aged and damaged body, the
Public Service Board. The Attorney-General
says
It is impossible for the board to complete the work of
regrading for some little time yet. 1
But this grading of the Public Service is not a
matter which has been suddenly sprung upon the
P.S. Board. It is a statutory obligation imposed
upon it once every five years. Five years is a
long time for a man to wait for what is due to
him, and the Board, if it hadn’t been quite so
gone in the knees, might have contrived to do the
work it is paid for at its due date. The Board is
supposed to see to the efficiency of the Public
Service, but it looks as if many an office cat in the
service could give the Board points in efficiency.
The proposed Australian penny post
is an expensive luxury, which might well
wait until Australia has secured other pressing
reforms. Old Age Pensions, for instance, are far
more urgent, while a scheme of adequate defence
is a crying necessity. Or the money it is pro-
posed to fool away on penny postage would just
about enable the Government to take over the
Northern Territory and start the Transcon-
tinental railway. Until Australia is properly
defended a penny post won’t help it much.
The still-born Secesh movement in Wes-
tralia is wonderfully like the Secesh that was
born dead in N.S.W. The wail of Monger,
M. thattheother States are “battening on
the life-blood” of W.A. is Joe Carrutliers’ cry
over again thatthey are “battening” on N.S.W.,
and Monger’s fable that Westralia only entered
the union on the promise of its transcontinental
railway is almost a repetition of the Ananias of
Premier Joe and his friend Lindon Biddulph that
N. only entered the union because it was
promised an Australian capital 100 miles from
Sydney. There is a wealth of imagination about
Secesh that, if devoted to writing testimonials for
patent medicines, would produce shocking conse-
quences.
“ A Planter ” shed tears all over the pages
of the S.M. Herald, the other day, re the horrors
which, he says, await the Kanaka when he goes
back to his sunny native isle. (It seems from his
lament that the native isle is just the savage old
place it was long ago ; that all the tales we have
heard about the success of the missionary man are
bogus and brass and “brumrny” ; and that we
ought at once to stop all contributions to the
missions as sheer waste of money and a foolish
contribution to the support of a confidence
trick.) “ A Planter” proposes, as an alternative,
that the Kanakas be sent to Fiji, where he
and other planters will gladly employ them for a
darned long term at darned low wages. Which
looks very kind of “ A Planter’’—not “ A Plun-
derer.”
An old superstition turns up again :• —
Tlie Victorian Street-Betting Suppression Act forbids
betting with “ infants ” —persons under 21 years of age—
the penalty being £lOO fine. For 10 years that portion of
the Act has been a dead-letter, because no detective or
policeman is under 21, and, if the ’tec. hires a person
under 21 to bet, he has himself broken the law and made
himself liable to £lOO. Consequently, there is no
evidence, though it is well-known that half the betting
done at the clubs and tote-shops in Melbourne is by legal
“infants.” Still, a North Melbourne betting publican
has had the bad luck to bump up against the law.
It was that accursed number 18 again. This is how the
story is told for the prosecution. The pub. is
the 13th on the list for that suburb. It is just
13 doors from the nearest pub. on each side. It
was June 13, and a little girl 13 years of age
named Alice Tucknell (13 letters in her name)'came in
and put 12 pence on the horse numbered 13 on the card
for the first race at Ascot. She backed it for a
place, and it won, and then she backed No. 13 in the
last race both ways and it also won. All these 13’s must
have alarmed the bookie something considerable; but he
held out. And he paid the little girl all her winnings.
At least, he said he did. She went home to her father,
told him all about it, and showed him the 7s. she had
won! He figured it out, and then went mad. He
said she should have got 135., and away he went
to the bookie to expostulate. He came back with
one black eye, a bruised ear, and 11 other injuries—
making 13 in all—in various parts of his body.
(This part of the yarn is invented to keep the
13 superstition going.) He rushed off to the police-
station (which also has 13 letters in it) and informed the
sergeant in charge. The. sergeant took down the state-
ment of the girl and her father, and forwarded them to
the Crown Law Department. It sat on the matter for
weeks and har.ched a prosecution. A summons was taken
out calling on the publican to come before a court on
August 13.

Public Notice.
Commissioners of Savings Banks.
FOR ADVANCES DEPARTMENT.
(KNOWN AS CREDIT FONOIER.)
ELEVENTH ISSUE OF
Debentures for £lOO,OOO
at 3J per cent.
TO MATURE 31st OCTOBER, 1917.
To be issued under the authority of the Acts of'
Parliament of Victoria, Nos, 1481, 1722,
1778 and 1878.
CONDITIONS FOR TENDERS.
Tenders will be receivable at the Commissioners’ Head
Office, 29 Market-street, Melbourne, until noon on Tues-
day, 28th August, 1906.
Debentures will be issued in denominations of £lOO, or
£5OO, as may be desired.
interest at 3£ per cent, per annum will be payable half-
yearly on 30th April, and 31st October, the first such pay-
ment being due on 30th April, 1907, at the Commissioners’
Head Office, or at any Savings Bank in Victoria.
Any tender below £9B per £lOO will not be entertained. :
Tenders must be accompanied by a deposit in cash, or by
a Bank Draft payable to the Commissioners of Savings
Banks, amounting to 10 per cent, of the face value of the
Debentures applied for, and if the Tender be accepted in
full, or pro rata, the balance must be paid on or before
28th February, 1907, or may be paid at an earlier date.
Interest at per cent, per annum will be allowed on
accepted Tenderers’lodgments of 10 percent, and over
from date of receipt to 31st October, 1906, and will be ‘
payable up to that date on the issue of the debentures.
Commission will be allowed only to Brokers who are'
members of recognised Stock Exchanges, at the rate of
ten shillings per cent, on the face value of Debentures for 1
accepted Tenders.
The due payment of these Debentures, and of all inter-
est thereon, is a charge on all the securities held by and
the funds of the Commissioners, under Division 3 of Part
1 of the Act No. 1481, and is also guaranteed by the Go-
vernment of Victoria.
Copies of the authorising Acts may be inspected, and
Conditions and Form of Tender can be obtained upon ap-
plication at the Head Office of the Commissioners or any
of their Savings Banks.
By order of the Board, GEO. E. EMERY,
Inspector-General of Savinas Banks.

Order of the Honorable W. GREGORY WALKER,

John Wood, who died on the 15th day of March, 1904,

of the Estate of the said John Wood to the Curator of

proceed to distribute the Estate of the said John Wood
as if he died without leaving a Widow. The address of
the said Curator is Queen’s-square, Sydney. Dated this
29th day of June, 1906. T. W. GARRETT, Registrar of

One Thomas Henley, of N.S.W. State
Parliament, informs the public through the press
(27/7/’O6) that he is profoundly impressed with the
failure of State Socialism as evidenced by the
suspension of the State motor ’bus service. A
mere nodding acquaintance with Henley, who is
a plumber and an authority on septic tanks,
would reveal the improbability of his being im-
pressed with anything of larger importance than
a soldering iron or a roll of lead. While Henley
is in the humor, he might become profoundly
impressed with his own inconsistency in retaining
his seat on the Board of Water Supply and Sewer-
age. If State Socialism is a failure then the
Board of Water Supply and Sewerage is a failure,
and it is partly run by a failure and plumbhr
named Henley.
Ex-Premier Philp, of Queensland, has
suddenly developed a strong weakness for cold
tea. In the Assembly the other day, he called
the drinking and gambling evils “ the real curses
of Australia, ” and argued that the hotels should
be brought under the provisions of the Factories
and Shops Act, which would mean closing at 6
p.m. “ Will you support a measure to ensure
this reform ? ” said Home Secretary Airey,
quickly. “ Yes,” shouted Philp, and so pinned
himself down to something he will be very sorry
for by-and-bye.

actuhl ice.' This is a boon to those in the Country and

MAN’S INFLUENCE
OVER MAN.
Remarkable Words on
Personal Magnetism from
an Eminent Divine.
No subject has excited so much interest or
awakened so much discussion among thinking
people of late as that of Personal Magnetism. It
is worthy of note that clergymen, doctors, college
presidents, and men of science everywhere are
giving it deep thought. Many of them have
openly certified to its wonderful powers. The
outspoken words of the Rev. Paul Weller of
Gorham, N.Y., in this connection will call forth
no little additional comment:
REV. PAUL WELLER.
In answer to a personal letter from an intimate
friend, asking him if it were true that he (Rev.
Mr. Weller) had become a firm believer in
Personal Magnetism and Hypnotism, Rev. Weller
wrote:
The facts are: The subject of Personal Magnetism or
Hypnotism, to which I have devoted many years of study,
was recently more forcibly than ever called to my
attention through reading a scientific work on the subject,
now being circulated by the New York Institute of
Science of Rochester, N.Y.
I am a minister of the Gospel, but I do not hesitate to
say that the reading of that book and the subsequent
study of its contents have worked an all-powerful, im-
portant, and good influence over me. My recommendation
of Personal Magnetism, a subject every man and woman
may study with profit, is njade after thorough investi-
gation and with complete knowledge of its great value. I
make this statement deliberately. The study of Personal
Magnetism, as set forth in the admirable books I have
mentioned above, should be next to the study of the
Holy Bible.
Personal Magnetism embodies all the laws governing
man’s influence over man. It is the power that makes
men mould the minds of men. It turng life’s failures
into successes. It develops the latent powers of the will
and makes one capable of the accomplishment of great
deeds. I have received many letters on the subject, and
to all writers I have answered : “ Write to the New York
Institute of Science, Rochester, N.Y., asking for their
scientific work on Personal Magnetism and Hypnotism.
It will be sent to you free of charge. If its perusal does
as much for you as it did for me you will thank me the
longest day you live for having called your attention to
the book.
Yours truly,
ruiy,
(Rev.) PAUL WELLER.
It will be only necessary for you to send your
request to the New York Institute of Science,
Dept. 22 R., Rochester, N. Y., U.S.A., and you
will receive the volume Rev. Paul Weller recom-
mends by return mail. IT IS FREE. A lid.
postal card will bring it.
Some interesting literature is being scat-
tered about Australia by “Mary” and “Benja-
min,” the un-surnamed Yanks who coaxed a
number of Christian Israelites with untamed hair
away from this country last year, on the ground
that they had to “gather the long-lost sheep of
the house of Israel ” into a vessel bound for
Benton Harbor, Mich., U.S.A. At that time the
“seventh trump” was reported to be sounding, as
a signal for sheep-shearing or something Ihe
seraph who is performing on the instrument must
have a phenomenal lung development, and his
clarion call has lasted long enough to enable
Benjamin and Mary to return for another load of
sheep with long hair and flowing whiskers. (There
is probably no truth in the rumor that the trump is
a steam whistle, and that the “sheep” are unloaded
at a Chicago meatvvorks siding.) Among other
printed matter Polly and Ben carry a stock of
Shiloh’s Messenger of Wisdom, the principal
press organ of the hirsute sect, which has a curious
way of mixing religion with hair and dollars. In
spite of their belief that “ the earth shall be puri-
fied and return to its Eden state,” and that there
shall be no more death among the faithful who
father at Mich., U.S.A., the “Israelites of the
louse of David ” think it strictly advisable for
possible converts to send some coin along with
their applications for Shiloh’s Messengek, or for
the books of alleged wisdom which are also pre-
pared at Jerusalem (now situated in Mich.,
U.S.A.). In sending 50 cents, one gushing person
(sex not stated, perhaps because it is quite obvious)
writes to Shiloh’s Messenger :
l got a little book from a handsome young man with
soft whiskers round his face and beautiful ringlets of
golden hair hanging on his shoulders. Another one was
with him, who might be his brother, with long ringlets of
dark chestnut hair around his shoulders. Both had
calm, happy faces. They reminded me so much of Christ,
as His picture always appears with long ringlets of hair.
They might have been angels sent to deliver the message
sounded by the seventh angel; or they might have lived
in the days of Christ and have been resurrected to go
forth and carry the glad news of His second coming.
They were neither shabby nor dirty, but the clothes they
wore looked cheap and sunburnt.
An angel with “soft whiskers around his face” is
an original conception.

“Pyin," from Dunedin
The tide in the affairs of man that leads, etc., dumped
me down in the Scoto-Maoriland town of Dunedin some
three months ago, and I’m just waiting 1 for that tide to
swing me back again somewhere on the coast of Aus-
traha. Any part of it’ll do, I’m not in the way of feeling
particular just now. I came here filled with hope,
t £ that Maoriland bore the brand from top to toe
of God s own country.” I’ve been looking around ever
~y e been here for that brilliant prosperity
which they say belongs to these islands alone, and
which is pitiably absent from Australia. I can’t
find it, and, as far as I can see, Maorilanders (or
that portion of them who camp together in this part
of the island) are also ignorant of its whereabouts. True,
there is no surface poverty ; neither should there be in a‘
small community like this; but it’s the devil’s own job
for a lot of the people to get through the week with 21
meals. Rents are as high as the lower heavens, and the
necessaries of life are about double what they are your
side. Maoriland has been boomed, and folks here are
hugging the boom to their bosoms in order to keep it
warm. If the cold blast of real fact gets to it one of these
days, that boom is going to bust as flat as a decayed toy
balloon, sure as you’re living. Then again, the local
residents; They’re narrow-minded, self-opinionated, pro-
vincial, and utterly incapable of seeing further than the
incarnadine extremities of their probosces. This is no
exaggeration ; ask any man who’s had the misfortune
to be pitchforked into this Caledonian paddock. And
one of the most peculiar phases of this democratic
country is that the. worst payers of the whole crowd are
the Gabor organisations. There’s a rag - here, run under
the aegis of the unions, and the men who run the show on
behalf of Labor go round, hats in hand, and beg for con-
tributions, and ask the casual available scribe to do his
good graft for nix ! This is in “ God’s own country.” Oh!
send me a whiff of Wexford-street!
“Melb.”: Talking of Fawkner’s house,
preserved in Launceston. How much more impor-
tant was his first house in Melbourne ! I went to
see it, in sorrow, just before it was pulled down.
It stood at the rear of the Union Club Hotel.
Yet was not Batman’s house, corner of William
and Collins streets, before Fawkner’s?
“ A.D.” tells a tale of horror :
I don’t enthuse over the young Australian who wears
beer froth on his waistcoat; but I prefer him to
the white-lipped, cigarette shadow who haunts the
chemists’ shops assuaging his unhealthy thirst with
pink and yellow fountain drinks. The temperance advo-
cate has not arrived who is game to accuse the medicine
and pill shops of selling' drinks that, in some instances,
are rank enough to kill a Hottentot. (Probably the local
medico could speak with point upon the subject.) After
five years inside knowledge of the trade, I conclude that
the thirst quencher supplied by the pill man is not the
stuff to build up the stomach of a young nation. Stand-
ing near a city chemist’s, one muggy night, I observed
the usual band of squash drinkers leaning against the
wall outside. They were deadly sober, to be sure, but as
they moved in and out the shop their green and pinky
drinks made strange noises inside them. They addressed
each other in sad voices. One of them, a gaunt, high-col-
lared youth, told a cigarette-shadow beside him that the
almonade and raspberry-syrup was losingits bite. Another
crainpy ” boy said he always woke up after a night of
ginger-squash and pink-shakes as though lie had been
swallowing sweetened vitriol and jam tarts. For six
years or thereabouts I managed an electro-plating de-
partment where pumps and fountains came in regularly
to be renovated and repaired. Some of these elaborate
silver-washed machines are a network of miniature
stills and winding tubes. The common beer pump
exuded an ordinary nine-inch smell when dissected, but
the almonade-raspberry fountain gave out a sublimate of
stinks that poisoned the air and corroded the office
fittings. We preserved the greeny-yellowy slime that
oozed from the middle tubes ; it was quicker than caustic
for burning off warts, and I discovered that it made first-
rate ink. Consoling to know that one has penned good
cow-verse with the dregs of raspberry-squash.
A society called “ Vierderiliterabeagi-
tationskommissiondelegierten versammlungspeti-
tion ” has been formed in Switzerland to protest
against the price of beer being raised.

Bright’s Disease.— From. Mr. William John Yuille, Harveytown, Eaglehawk,
Vic., Ist September, 1905. . , T
“Despite all the skill and attention bestowed on me by the hospital doctors and nurses, 1
gradually grew worse, the doctors having diagnosed my complaint as Bright s Disease, lhe doctors
gave me up as incurable, and gave me but a few weeks longer to live. I was then taken from the
hospital to my home to die (as my friends thought), as my father did not wish me to die m the
hospital. But I had a feeling within myself that there was hope whilst there was life, and, unlike my
friends I did not give up in despair. On the way home my father procured a bottle of Warner s bate
Cure but, on arrival, I was in a state of collapse from the shaking I had undergone on the way. My
father thinking that the Safe Cure would do me no harm if it did me no good, gave me a small dose,
continuing to do so at intervals throughout the day. I felt a little better, the agonising pain being
less severe. I continued to take Warner’s Safe Cure, and was soon able to get a little rest and sleep,
and my strength very gradually returned. My friends began to have hopes of my ultimate
recovery. Each day investigation showed less albumen. My health steadily improved. Each
bottle of Warner’s Safe Cure I took hastened my recovery. I was then able to get up and about, and
in less than three months was restored to perfect health and strength. _ From that time up to the
present I have had no return of the disease, and for six years have been m constant employment on
the mines.”
gladder Disease.— From Mr. F. H. White, 37 Errol-street, West Footscray, Vic.,

The Inky Way.
“The Mole”: Sydney received a shock on a recent
Saturday morning, when a report spread that Granny
Herald had ceased publication—shut up shop—tucked
her capital under her crinoline, and left the country.
The rumor had some foundation, as printing was stopped
for about five hours through a disabled machine. When
Sydney recovered from the scare, it tried to imagine
what would happen if the careful Hunter-street dame did
shut up and fly, before the Socialistic Tiger got a good
grip.
* * * *
“Lil”: A recent Melbourne Herald correspondent
wailed that “ Tiie Bulletin, with its insidious cleverness,
is sapping the moral fibre of thousands.” Evidently
“moral fibre” is the latest synonym for “ anti-Australian
principles.” I, for one, would chance my moral fibre
being sapped if certain ponderous journals would intro
duce enough cleverness to make themselves readable.
But they don’t, so they’re guilty of nary a sap.
* * * *
“Hamer”: The wonder about the average provincial
paper in New South Wales is, not that it’s a poor kind of a
rag, but that it is any rag at all. Certainly the local
paper reports the local news, but that is seldom a
penn’orth—quite a lot of papers have to charge sixpence
to make things worthwhile —and the Sydney'dailies are
the sole source of general news. Yet in spite of this the
country papers fight each other on every available occa-
sion, and when two of them meet in one town they
quarrel like cats shut in a bag. Orange boasts
two dailies —they were bi-weeklies or tri-weeklies
once, but in the effort to get ahead of each
other they became dailies, and would probably
become hourlies if they could, and this rivalry is enough
to make a stranger weep. The result of the general poor-
ness of the country press is reflected in their advertise-
ment rates. Theatrical ads., for instance, run at 2s. or
2s. 6d. for the first inch, and Is. 6d. each additional inch ;
in some cases it is Is. 6d. all through ; and there is a dis-
count on the whole bill—if the bill happens to be paid.
InM.L. the same advertisements would cost 4s. and 35.,
and 3s. and 25., and on the whole there w'ould be better
value for the money.
• * # *
“Snell”: I have just been told of an extraordinary
happening, which is substantiated to a certain extent by
independent evidence. The hero is a journalist who can
afford to drink lager with his devilled oysters now-a-
nights, but not long back he was writing scrap para-
graphs, and scattering them over the place in the hope
that a few would drift into print and earn him rent and
bread. He was living in a small room in a dingy
old building over a tobacconist’s, and of late so
few of his paragraphs had found openings that
the seedy proprietor of the shabby apartment had fixed
the day and hour of the ejectment, and Penn didn’t know
where to go next. He was doing a bitter think, when his
raised window rattled in the breeze, and a fo-note blew
in. Penn might have thought it a miracle, only he looked
out of the window and saw much excitement in the street
below, where a portly warehouseman had dropped a roll
of notes, and was dancing in agony as they floated about
in the high wind. Penn did not return that fiver. He
says that would have been flying in the face of Providence ;
and perhaps, after all, it would have been.
* * * *
“ N.”: The veteran sits hard on the shoulders of a news-
paper, like Sinbad's Old Man of the Sea, curling his thin
legs round its throttle. Now there was a dinner to newly-
made editor Cunningham, of the Argus, the other night.
The youngsters were, of course, allowed helow the salt,
but apparently no man under 50 was permitted to make a
speech. Yet a special corollary of Cunningham’s well-
earned elevation is the forcing up of young blood. There
is always a danger, on every paper, of venerables sticking
so firmly together that in the end they have to be shunted
in a bunch. Then they slide, like plaster saints off the
board on the head of a Dago when he is tripped up.
« * * *
i “Genung”: My first job was on a weekly paper in
i Ballarat. I was bound apprentice by an anxious father
! to learn type-setting, but mighty little of that I learned.
I found out many other things. Our “news” consisted
principally of twelve columns of “ jars ” to more or less
respectable gentlemen and more or less virtuous ladies.
Each of the little towns round Ballarat had its weekly
half-column of such matter as this : “ B.J. should use the
same stuff to color his nose as his wife does for her cheeks.
It is cheaper, and doesn’t smell so strong”; or, “Why
does Joe T. stay at home now at nights? Is he afraid of
Maggie’s big brother ? Oh, you naughty man ! ” The
circulation was enormous. One week it reached 2000.
The boss ran the rabbit that night seven times.
At the head of the leading article, or more often where
that unread thing should be, was a notice :
“ All contributions to the column of Personalities must
be accompanied by the sum of threepence in penny-
stamps for each item.”
Sometimes they were, sometimes not, but they were all
published. The boss was a careless sort in money-
matters, and would go through his mail—sometimes a
hundred letters —revising and altering items, and nearly
alwa.ys forgetting the stamps. As soon as he went out
for his “eye-opener” we boys formed a co-operative
party, and went through the waste-paper basket looking
for our wages.
At last the boss went too far. He had a par in-
quiring why Jeremy Bones, a very leading citizen, was
doing some extra-bad wickedness. Bones went out and
bought up some of the boss’s liabilities, and set the law
in swift motion. A few days after, while we were dissing,
a posse of police and bailiffs marched in. One of them
shoved a blue paper into the boss’s face —all “whereas”
and “therefore.” Then they set to work, took the
machinery' to pieces, put it and type and all on to a dray,
and carted the things off, leaving the boss flooding the
air with curses. I asked him what I should do next, and
he threw a bucket of “pie” at me. That ended my first
job.
* * * *
In his posthumous “memoirs,” English journalist
Wemyss Beid says he regularly wrote 1500-word leaders
in from forty to fifty' minutes. Also, he rather enjoyed
writing with people talking around him, and felt no
difficulty' in joining in the conversation. The accom-
plishment is accounted for by the fact that in his early
day*s he had to turn out his Monday leaders thus rapidly',
or miss catching the long-distance train which took him
for a Saturday*to-Monday visit to his best girl. Best girls
have a lot to answer for.
Arbor Day, depicted by “ H.G.”
A great example of the perversion of an excellent idea
to base ends is provided in the so-called Arbor D .V
brations, conducted by the Victorian Education Depart-
ment. Tbev were intended to instil a love of the beauties
of nature into the infant mind, and at the same time to
enlarge the timber supply and furnish healthy exercise.
They have degenerated into “loyal functions at ''hicl
local councillors turn out in the unwonted discomfort oi
frockcoat and ill-fitting belltopper to spell-bind in aitch-
less frenzy about “ the throne and empire, while
one of their number stoops with difficulty oyer a Bent-
like bingv to shove one anaemic sprig into the earth as
the signal for the local brass band to beef out a discordant
“ Gawsave.” Minister of Eduction Sachse stated, a year
or two back, that the Education Department would one
day be self-supporting, through the sale of the valuable
timbers planted by the children. According to present
indications, there won’t be sufficient timber from this
source to provide the teachers with walloping-material,
but there will be a coming - generation that is gone in the
legs, its supports having been worn out in infancy,
through weary hours of standing about to listen to the
shouts of officious municipal potentates.
Sydney Trades and Labor Council is
letting the public into the secrets of the success
of the people who are able to keep motors, race-
horses, yachts and other fast things. It lies in
the miserable wages paid to milliners, shop-assist-
ants and dressmakers by the chiffon kings. A
woman is put in charge of a department—say
dressmaking or millinery—and to make her room
pay, and make her salary (which is anything from
£3 to £7 in big houses) she must sweat the girls.
The rate of wages is fixed by her, as she is sup-
posed to know the capabilities of each worker;
needless to state, she doesn’t overrate them.
• Overcrowding is another evil, and it is a common
practice, when the inspector calls, to send the sur-
plus girls out of the room on various pretexts,
with a caution not to return untill the coast is
clear. Singular as it appears on the surface, still
it is a fact that most of the employees regard the
inspector as their natural enemy.
“HYS BOKE.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ah ! the book he might have written,
Aye, have written years ago,
When the Muse was ever-gracious,
And Hope not all mendacious ;
When the river-song, a-ripple, ran in clear andjendless
flow.
And Life’s mirage fond, fallacious,
Rose-redden’d all the spacious
Sounding concave of the dome, whence, pure as snow,
The clouds went out a-sailing
Towards where day’s glory 7 failing
Returned to blaze anew in one splendid after-glow.
Years agone—full years of pleasure,
With no time to count the treasure
As his rapt heart joined in concert with the music of the
spheres.
Years all joy withouten measure,
One long deep-drawn draught of pleasure,
Years of ecstacy ineffable bey r ond the depths of tears.
Now, he’s glad that book's not written,
Sure, his heart was on his sleeve,
What time fond youth was smitten
With the love of “ make-believe ” ;
Diffuse and crude the phrases,
The sober judgment says ;
At Hampton Court the maze is
Less devious in its ways,
lie’s certain now his script is
Far safer where it lies
Than scattered wide in pica large
Before a people’s eyes.
VIC.
G. G. M‘C.

MOTHER SEIGEL’S SYRUP.
Mrs. Emma Relton is to-day a hearty, plesant-
faeed woman, living at 218 George-street, Circular
Quay, Sydney, where her husband carries on
business as a tailor. For nearly a dozen years she
was a great sufferer from indigestion, and was
hardly ever able to enjoy a meal. She tried
many kinds of medicine, and had treatment from
doctors, but with no lasting benefit, and finally
lost all hope of being cured. In 1900 a friend
persuaded her to try Mother Seigel’s Syrup In
a letter, dated March 2nd, 1900, she says
A HAPPY WOMAN NOW.
“ taking only three small bottles .1 could
eat and sleep better than I had dime for years,
and was able to relish my food. This relief was in
itself a great boon, and. feeling encouraged by it,
I went on using the Syrup, gaining health and
strength from every dose, until, after emptying
six bottles, more, I was feeling perfectly well again,
and 1 have remained well ever since. Mother
Seigel s Syrup is the sole cause of my cure, and is
the.best medicine I know of, and I always keep it
hapdy in the house.”

‘‘Bend ’Or,” on an undesirable sort of
citizen :
The professional lunatic is a type known in many hos-
pitals for the insane. This bogus inhabitant of Bedlam
leaves one asylum with the set purpose of getting into
another as soon as his little capital is worked out. The
States have a happy knack of passing this undesirable on
to one another, A free railway pass to the nearest border
town brings the malingerer within the lunacy law of
another State. He has been put on the train at the other
end with half-a-sovereignanda newrig-oot, perhapsaswag
Alighting, he proceeds to have a good time on his limited
capital and the proceeds of the asylum-made clothes.
Perhaps-he strikes a patch, and hangs on to a man who
is knocking down his cheque. He has a keen eye for these
genuine lunatics, and, being a plausible rogue, fattens on
them to the final and ultimate bean. At last he faces a
future absolutely unrelieved by the hope of a single beer.
He then proceeds to work the “system.” He generally
chooses a public place in broad daylight, turns his coat
inside out, hat ditto, and proceeds to be a wildly irre-
sponsible atom dancing on the face of the earth. A crowd
.collects. Then a policeman assembles, and makes an
arrest on a charge of being unusual and disorderly.
Throughout his meanderings the bogus lunatic has pre-
served his certificate of discharge from the last asylum,
and this being found on him when he is searched, is con-
sidered by the examining medico—generally not a
specialist in insanity—as proof that he has got hold of a
relapsed case. Committed to the nearest mental hos-
pital, the wandering one gets good food, bed and extra
diet called'medical comforts (which include! even beer)
until, fattened with good living on an idle life, he feels
the call for freedom again. Then he begins to drop the
wild expression out of his face, and to walk soberly and
put off Iris antic, disposition, and is soon discharged as
ured.
- Tusi Ata ” : Curious what a fascination
exotic names have for most people. Notice that
one of your (rejected) correspondents—a lady,
judging by her pretty-sounding nom de 'plume—
has chosen a pen-name that would bring a blush
into the face of the most hardened Samoan
damsel. A N.S.W. railway station also bears a
most untranslatable Maori name, and among the
many ..suburban villas with South Sea. titles I
have noticed a few whose real meaning is most
likely hidden from the occupants. They are
generally said to mean something like “ Pleasant
View” or “Butterfly Arbor,” or thereabouts.
Sometimes they do, but I know for certain that
sometimes they don’t. Came across one, foT in-
stance, the literal translation of which is “Vomit-
ing upon the Qeean.” A pleasant name that for a
home !
Homo Rule for Ireland is likely to come,-
after all, in a perfectly quiet sort of way, and
with no insectoriarifuss. Lloyd George, a member
of the Bannerman Ministry, recommends local
Legislatures for Ireland, Scotland, and Wales
(and presumably for England also) on the ground
that the old Mother of Parliaments is sagging
down and on the point of breaking in the middle
through overloading, arid has. really only time to
be an Imperial Parliament. That is where-the
Unionist movement must go to pieces ultimately.
Whether Unionists like it or not, there will have
to-be an Irish Parliament and other local Parlia-
ments, because old Ma Parliament in London is
breaking up with fatigue and a load of details.

WHAT PEPS ARE.
PEPS are a germicide and
antiseptic of the highest
value in colds, coughs, bron-
chitis, sore throat, or tight-
ness of the chest. Their won-
derful healing essences are
brought into direct contact
with the diseased surfaces of
the throat and lungs. Liquid
cough mixtures, merely swal-
lowed into the stomach, do
not reach the seat of disease
as Peps do. Between the
stomach and the lungs there
is no direct connection, and
all lung and bronchial disease
must be treated direct. Peps
do this. They strengthen the
actual tissues of the lung and
wind-pipe. They bring won-
derful relief to the asthmatic
victim, and cure the worst
influenza cold. No home can
afford to be without this
remarkable medicine.
BRONCHITIS EVILS,
BRONCHITIS means in-
flammation of the delicate
membrane lining the tubes
that carry pure air into the
lungs. The membrane lining
these air-passages is extremely
sensitive to sudden change and
chill. The rational treatment
is by breathing in the pine
fumes emitted from a few Peps
as they dissolve on the tongue.
It is the method that is most
certain. The whole of the
inflamed air-passages and the
nasal organs will be bathed in
the soothing and healing in-
fluence of Peps. The painful
inflammation is allayed, the
glands that secrete the mucous
are helped back to their normal
activity. Of all Chemists,

the PEPS PASTILLE CO.,

June, 1906.. 4111 15 „ „ ~ 3375 736 52,408
The reserves consist of a formal reserve of £20,010, re-
serve for renewals, &c., £29,675, meter fund £1063, insur-
ance fund £450, and undivided profit £l2lO. The nature of
the renewals reserve is not clear. Current renewals (ordi-
nary) appear to be charged to profit and loss, and this
reserve seems to be provision for extraordinary renewals
that the future may call for. It is added to as a rule by
£5OO per half-year. If £5OO is a fair thing to cover a
half-year’s decay, in addition to the amount spent on
renewals and charged direct to profit and loss, then that
£5OO should come off the profits shown above. But that
is probably in excess of the requirements. The co. is
strong financially. Besides works and landed property
(£77,099),.it has £14,532 in N.S.W. public stocks, and,
after clearing off all its debts to the public and paying
the present dividend, it has a surplus of £5763
of other liquid assets. If the works, &c., are
worth £77,099 depends upon whether they ’ have
been kept written down (in other words, whether the
renewal reserve shown on the other side of the balance-
sheet is a true reserve and not merely a reserve against
actual depreciation). The total assets certainly return
as much as £7 2s. 6d. per cent, on the year’s profit-figures
as shown above. But a high return doesn’t necessarily
mean that a gas co.’s assets are sound; because a main
which has served 15 out of its (say) 20 years will earn just
as much as if it were fresh in the ground. In any case,
however, this co., besides paying 15 percent., and paying
for repairs as it goes, is making ample provision for extra-
ordinary renewals, and it has the actual funds to make
them with.

The reserves consist of reserve fund, £5200; plant
renewal, £2039; meter renewal, £lO2B ; undivided profit,
£2lO. Most likely the plant renewal and meter accounts
are not true reserves, being a set-off against actual
depreciation ; and the amount added to these reserves in
the half-year (£145) is in that ease not true profit. The
fixed investments amount to £12,565. Five years ago
they amounted to £13,061. Apparently, there-
fore, in addition to the renewal reserve, general
provision is being made for depreciation by writing down
works out of profits. The money thus accumulated is
being kept in liquid assets. Five years ago these
amounted to £4333 ; they are now £7ll4—before pay-
ment of div. in both cases. The capital is £lO,OOO. The
position is strong, and the financial management appa-
rently very satisfactory.

This is one of those very rare mining cos. that
have actually got their reserves in cash or its
equivalent. The equivalent is stated merely as
“loans”; and that --certainly isn’t very definite.
Also it isn’t possible to say what the loans are earning,
as all receipts are lumped. But though the co. has its
reserves in cash or its equivalent, there is a doubt as to
whether all its capital is represented by assets ; in other
words, it isn’t too sure whether the reserves are really
reserved at all, “ Mining property, including buildings,
tunnel, pits, &e.,” are down for £94,422 ; and at June,
1900, they were down for only £90,603. In those six
years a vast quantity of coal has been taken out, and the
mine—pits, tunnel and all—has got six years nearer the
time when it will be a mere hole in the ground ; and yet,
no doubt because something has been added to the plant,
the mine is taken in at £3SI9 more than it was six years
ago. It is pretty certain, then, that the reserves are not
real reserves, but at best only funds for the return of
capital. Certainly, most mining cos. have not provided
even that much. They make payments, and call them
dividends, whereas they are to a great extent merely
distributions of assets.
