Apparently plant and other property are being written
down out of profits not shown above ; but the process is
slow, and the extent uncertain. At December 31st, 1904,
plant was valued at £6650 ; it now stands at£6ooo. Free-
hold, &c., which stood at £15,034 18 months ago, stands
at £14,786 now ; but as apparently there have been some
purchases meantime, the full amount written off this
item is not ascertainable. In 1905, however, £5OO appears
to have been written off. Consequently, though the re-
serves are small, they are probably genuine, and so are
the profits as set out above. But, instead of the amount
written off being invested in permanent securities, the
whole of the savings of the past 18 months have gone to
wipe out the liability incurred over the John McLaughlin
share-transfer trouble. At December 31st, 1904, just
after the matter had gone against the co., £2149 was
entered as a suspense liability ; the item even now hasn’t
disappeared finally, but all except £l5B of the liability
has been wiped out. There may now, therefore, be a
chance to increase the deposits (representing reserve fund
and a fund for the return of capital), which in the past 18
months have only grown from £8365 to £8541. After
paying the dividend, the co.’s surplus of liquid assets
over liabilities is £11,305. At December 31st, 1904, it was
£9313, or £1992 less. The capital is £30,000.

Goodwill originally represented £57,500 of the total capi-
tal of £60,000 ; so that on that account alone, and pre-
suming there were no formation expenses handed over to
the co. to wipe out, Richardson and Wrench, Ltd., began
with a deficit of £57,500. In 17 years it has wiped out
£15,841 of it, and has good Treasury bills or solid cash
balances to show for that amount instead of vapory rights
to use two people’s names and pretend that it was those
people when everybody knew it wasn’t. Besides a small
amount (£713) of commissions in suspense, the co. has
now liquid assets £16,971 in excess of liabilities to the
public and the current dividend ; and, even though the
Sydney land-boom refuses to boom above a whisper, and
this eo.’s trading profits are consequently limp, the
gradual growth of liquid assets makes it an easier matter
to maintain the 5 per cent, dividend. Apart from the
name and the business and the office mat and the ink-pot,
the co. now owns something’ like ss. Bd. per share.

The deficit is probably more than this, as there is an item
“Improvement account, £1192,” among the assets, and
this is probably to some extent a goodwill item. (Of the
capital of £17,500, £15,000 is represented by goodwill,
which, being a paper asset, is here disregarded.) Without
allowing anything for depreciation, the ordinary profits
for the half-year were £343 ; but the co. has been in the
courts, at a cost of £983 ; hence the loss of £640 shown
for the term. The co.’s tangible assets, including stocks,
cash, sundry debtors, and expenses paid in advance
amount to £1372. Its liabilities total £1339. Surplus,
£33. Apart from goodwill and the doubtful improvement
and furniture accounts (£2125), that is all there is to
show for the capital of £17,500.

Watson’s Bay and South Shore Ferry Co.

1903 .. £1564 6 per cent. = £464 £llOO 1240
1904 .. 1208 6 „ „ 433 775 2015
1905 .. 1154 6 „ „ 433 721 2736
1906 .. 1867 6 „ „ 433 1434 4170
This is another case in which the public must make its
own allowance for depreciation ; and an asset that bumps
against a pier as often as a harbor ferry must depreciate
something considerable. The co. itself first shows (profit
and loss account) £967 as profit; and from that it appro-
priates £776 for depreciation—it had previously charged
profit and loss with £9OO direct, and added the amount
to depreciation account. If this means that £1676 is a
fair thing to charge for the year’s wear and tear, then
the net profits are cut down to £l9l, and a 6 per
cent, dividend should not have been paid ; if it
means that £9OO is written off for depreciation which has
occurred, and £776 is set aside to provide for future de-
preciation, then the two should not be turned into the
same account —one is a genuine reserve and the other
isn’t. It may also mean that £9OO is reckoned a fair
amount for the year’s wear and tear, but that there was
previous wear and tear which had not been provided for,
and £776is written off to cover or to help to cover it. In
that case previous profits were not as good as represented.
In any case, it isn’t satisfactory that figures in a balance-
sheet should be capable of meaning so many things. A
further comparison gives these results: —
1903. 1904. 1905. 1906.
Receipts .. .. £6,660 £7,181 £7,802 £8,829
Outgoings .. 5,097 5,972 6,648 7,512
Steamer property .. 11,916 11,803 14,226 19,394
Debt to public ’. .. 3,239 2,489 4,147 7,930
Of the debt to the public £5924 is now shown as owing on
debentures ; and there was £3024 of preference capital al-
ready. (The total share capital is £7221.) The line “ out-
goings” is worth less than it ought to be because the vari-
able amount charged direct for wear and tear cannot be
separated from other items; and a special expense,
“Contribution for Clovelly resumption, £350,” is not
included in the past year’s outgoings. Receipts have
grown satisfactorily, but the debt iq large ; ordi-
nary < shareholders will see that two people
now stand between them and dividends; and it is
doubtful whether any of the reserves are real reserves.
On the other hand, after paying 6 per cent, and providing
for current repairs, over £2750 has been put into new
property in three years—equal to a writing-off of just
over £9OO a year: qqd that is 6£ per cent, per annum on
the average holding of ship property. If that is just
enough to cover depreciation, then nothing really beyond
the bare div. has been earned. There doesn’t seem to be
much over that.

CHILBLAINS
The Chilblain is a slow inflammation of the skin of
the hands or feet, attended with swelling, itching, and
burning pains. Weak circulation ot‘ the blood is the
principal cause. People with imperfect blood circulation
generally suffer from cold feet and cold hands, and if they
put them into cold water and then near a fire the chilblain
will be formed. If you suffer from cold feet, rub them
frequently with
It will stimulate the circulation, and the chilblain
will thus be prevented. If chilblains are formed, bathe
with Dr. Sheldon’s flagnetic Liniment, and then cover
the parts with a piece of cloth on which Dr. Sheldon’s
Magnetic Liniment has been put. It will relieve the
itching, stinging pains, and after a few applications will
effect a cure.

ALLCOCK’S—The Original and only Genuine Porous Plaster.
Publicans and Sinners.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MOSS was a chap pretty
well known by most shearers up North.
He had a pub. at Dixon Springs—who
Dixon was nobody knew, an’ the springs was
never rnore’n billabongs when I seen ’em. But,
one way’n another, there was a good bit of traffic
-on that road, and bein’ as how there was nowhere
■else to go to, Moss got it all.
Nice man, Moss was. His father was a Jew,
-an’ his mother a Scotchwoman, which ain’t a cross
calculated to produce ladies’ benev’lent societies.
There wasn’t nothin’ benev’lent about their son,
Saunders. He was a rum-looking chap, too—big
an’raw-boned, with a shock of red hair (that was
the Saunders), an’ a face like a ferret, black eves
an’ a hook nose (that was the Moss).
My oath, he was hard ! I’ve known plenty of
shanty-keepers, an’ it’s not a soft breed, but
Moss, of Dixon Springs, beat ’em all. He’d start
a cove on good grog—had a tremendous stock, he
had—an’ then when yer got goin’ an’ he had yer
cheque, he’d fill yer up with the slickest poison
any man ever dealt out. I’ve got down to The
Springs just after a big shed had cut out, an’ seen
the chaps lyin’ about like flies on a bit of sticky
flypaper— all done, and Moss with every one of
-their cheques in his safe. An’ when yer woke up
he d kick yer out without as much as one finger o’
whisky. “ Tank’s round at the back,” he’d say
“ don’t waste the water ! ”
Well, about Jim Cassidy. He was the only pal
I ever did have as wouldn’t go on the spree. Had
a missus an’ three kids, Jim had, an’ he thought
the sun shone out of them. He wasn’t one of yer
blasted teetotal wasters—but he’d stop at two or
three drinks; wouldn’t take a shout when there
was a crowd about, and wouldn’t shout himself.
An’ the rum thing was, he was so jolly well-liked.
He was ringer on Bolwarra three years runnin’.
One year Jim an’ me was late leavin’ Bolwarra.
The boss had some young horses he wanted broke
in, an’ we took the job on—so it was a fortnight
•or so after the other chaps that Jim an’ I started
for home ; he lived near Albury, an’ I lived any-
where. We hadn’t all our money with us, ’cause
I d sent nearly all mine down to me old mother
in Sydney for her to look after, an’ Jim always
sent most of his to his missus. Boss was away
when we was leavin’ Bolwarra, an’ they paid us our
balance in cash. Jim had a little over £2O, an’ I
had a tenner.
We got to Dixon Springs two nights after, an’
found no one but ourselves at Moss’s pub. All
our crowd had gone, an’ we heard afterwards
that nearly every blooming one of the Bolwarra
cheques had gone into Moss’s account that year.
He must have made tons of money.
\\e was both tired an’ was sworn off drink very
strict (Jim s doing, of course), an’ I had a brute of
a headache, caused by my fool of a mare havin’
taken me under a limb where there was room for
her but very little for me. I was that sick with it
I couldn t eat anything, so I had a cup of tea and
went off to roost. Jim stayed to have a feed an’
yarned'a bit with Moss.
Next mornin’ I was all right, an’ we made an
early start. They got us breakfast in good time,
an’—rather to our surprise—Moss had his man (who
was as big a blackguard as himself) get our horses
ready, an’ he was round near the bar door when
we come from havin’ breakfast, lightin’ our pipes
an’feelin’fair toffs. I paid up for the beds an’
feed, an’ was just goin’ out to get on my mare
when Jim says, “ Well, I’ll bother you to get out
that money for me, Moss.”
Now, I didn’t know Jim had left his money
with Moss overnight, but I wasn’t surprised.
Moss had a good fire-proof safe ; so it was a com-
mon thing for a shearer as happened to have a bit
of cash an’ wanted to save it, to hand it over to
Moss to be locked up until he was leaving.
‘; Money ?” he says. ‘ ‘ What money ? ”
“ Why, the money I gave you to lock up for me
last night,” Jim says.
Moss shook his head, very sad an’ pained-like.
“Well, I knew you had a few in last night,” he
says, slowly, “but I didn’t think you was as bad
as that. You never left any money with me, Cas-
sidy, me friend.”
Jim had gone pretty white. He brought his
fist down on the bar counter.
“I handed you twenty pounds in this bar last
night, an’ saw you lock it up in your safe,” he
says, speakin’ very quiet. “ An’ you know it.”
Moss laughed.
“Oh, come now, that’s a good ’un !” he says.
“Twenty ! Why don’t you make it fifty or a
hundred while you’re about it? Just as easy.
Where’s yer witness?”
Jim turned to me, his voice shakin’.
“Dave,” he says, “you know I had more’n
twenty quid. I gave it to him last night; every
penny of it except the odd silver. You can
search me, an’ if you find ten bob I’m a liar. He’s
got my money.”
“Look here,” I says, “this is a poor joke,
Moss. Ante up an’ let’s get a move on.”
“ You can get a move on as soon as you dashed
■well like,” says Moss gettin’ narsty. “ I’m full
up of you drunken swabs—tryin’ to take a man
down like this. Spielin’ don’t act in this part o’
the country. You’d better clear out, both of you.”
Mrs. Moss had come in an’ was lookin’ on. She
was a big bold-faced woman ; had been a barmaid
in a shanty in some minin’ districk when Moss
married her. She had a tongue like a wood-rasp.
“When these gents has finished their little
game, Saunders,” she says, “per’aps they’ll let
you come in to breakfast.”
Moss took a key out of his pocket an’ chucked
it over to his wife.
“Open the safe, Yi’let,” he says. “See if the
gentleman’s money’s there.”
The safe was in the bar parlor, an’ Mrs. Moss
opened it with a flourish. It was quite empty.
Not that Jim troubled to look at it. He turned
back to Moss. Next thing I saw him put his
hands on the counter an’ give a spring, an’ then I
give one meself, an’ pulled him back. Moss had
put his hand quickly into the cash-drawer, an’ we
was both lookin’ down a revolver.
He sang out, an’ his man Joe came runnin’ in,
an’ we saw immejit why he’d had him an’ our
horses so nice an’ handy.
“Now you git.” Moss says. “ I’m full o’ this,
an’ I swear I’ll shoot if yer lift a hand. Take me
down for twenty quid, will yer ? Let their horses
go, Vi’let.”
Mrs. Moss ran out on to the verandah, an’ the
next minute we heard her say, “ Cl—k,” an’ then
our horses’ hoofs on the road. They was good
horses, an’ there was only one thing to do—chivvy
after them. I started at once, an’ Jim just waited
to say one or two tilings to Moss, an’ then he
come after me. We had no show with Moss —he
held all the cyards.
I had a reg’lar job with Jim. He was nearly
cry in’ an’ half mad—as much with bein’ had so
simple as over the loss of the money, which was
bad enough. He’d have murdered Moss if he
could have got a show ; an’ when we caught the
horses —which wasn’t for three mile —he was all
for goin’ back an’ havin’ it out. I persuaded him
to come on—two men an’ a barker an’ a shrew’s
no fightin’odds for unarmed blokes. We told a
p’liceman about it in the first township we struck ;
but he didn’t think we had any chance of besting
Moss. If Jim had only had a witness it would
have been all right.
Well, we got over it —I will say I got over it
quicker’n Jim—an’ I lent him some money, an’ he
managed all right. I said good-bye to him in
Sydney—he was in a hurry to get home. Then I
drifted about for another year. Jim paid the
cash I’d lent him back to me old mother, but I
never set eyes on him again till we met at Bol-
warra next shearin’.
Mighty glad to see old Jim I was. We started
yarnin’, and, of course, we got on to Moss.
‘‘l stayed at his place coinin’ up,” Jim says—
which rather surprised me. “ No, we never said
anything about my twenty quid. He was as sweet
as old pie— met me with a happy smile. 0’ course,
I was with a lot o’ chaps. An’ when I come away
it was, ‘ See yer goin’ down, I s’pose, Mr. Cassidy.’
The hog ! ”
“I like his darned cheek,” I says. “ What did
you say ? 9
“I said, ‘Oh, per’aps.’”
‘‘ I wonder you go near the cow,” I says—an’ I
couldn’t make it out. Jim give a little smile to
himself, but he didn’t say anything more.
We told a lot o’ the chaps, an’ they was wild.
For two pins they’d have smashed up things at
Moss’s goin’back. But Jim was dead off any-
thin’ like that, an’ the only thing he’d make the
boys promise was that they wouldn’t get on the
tear at Dixon Springs. It was a hard thing—
but they all promised.
That was a big year at Bolwarra. Jim was
ringer—his third year—an’ he must have made
a thumpin’ cheque. I don’t know what ho sent
along to Mrs. Jim, but the night before we left he
drew somethin’ over 50 quid—an’ took it all in
gold, which he had arsked previous to have. I
arslced him why, an’ he laughed.
There was a no-end jolly crowd of us leavin’
Bolwarra, an’ I reckoned .Tim ’ud find it hard to
get the boys to keep their promise about not
drinkin’ when we got to Dixon Springs the second
night. However, there wasn’t no trouble —y’see,
the boys was that hot about the way Moss had
sold Jim, they was glad of the chance to do him
a bad turn. We got to Moss’s late in the evenin’,
and, when we’d fixed up our horses, Jim says :
“ Come into the bar ’arf a mo’, boys.”
We all marched in. Moss was standin' there
rubbin’ his hands.
‘‘Tea’ll be ready in a minute, gents,” he says.
“ All right,” says Jim. “By the way, Moss, I
want you to look after a little money for me to-
night. Lock it up, will yer? Fifty”—an’ he
counted it out slowly on the counter an’ watched
Moss, whose little eyes was a-shinin’, lock it up in
the safe. We looked on in surprise. It seemed
a rum thing for Jim to trust him again after
the way he’d been fooled larst year. However, he
had witnesses this time.
“There, that’s safe, ain’t it, boys?” says Jim,
as Moss put the key of the safe back into his
pocket. “ Come on to tea.” An’ we all went an’
had a feed. Moss buzzed round us like an old
red-haired blowfly, an’ Jim was just as affable to
him as any bloke could be, jokin’ with him an’
makin’ us die laughin’ at the things he said. But
I can raise a grin now, thinkin’ of Moss’s face
when the whole lot of us trooped off to bed after a
smoke, without so much as glancin’ at the bar.
His jor just dropped.
None of us was in a hurry to get up next day.
When I went out to the stables, along o’ some o’
the other chaps, there was old Jim, singin’ softly
to himself an’ brushin’ down his inare. He put
the saddle on her, an’ was just gii-i '■m’ it up, when
he stopped an’arsked me if I'd swop saddles for
the day. I had a new-fangled military saddle as
he had a fancy to try. O’ course I says yes, an’
he changed the saddles and we got the horses
ready. Jim singin’ all the time, “There is a
happy land.”
“ You’re blooming cheerful,” I says.
“ Yairs, ” he says.
All the chaps was ready for the road before
breakfast, an’ after we had our feed we was all
in the bar settlin’-up—Moss lookin’ as black as a
thunder-cloud —when old Jim came in an’ arsked
Moss for his money.
“ Wha-at ? ” says Moss.
“ My money,” Jim says.
“ What are yer dreaming about ? —yer money ? ”
Moss snarls.
All the chaps got near, an’ yer heard a queer
little growl go round the bar. It was a bit too
barefaced—two years runnin’.
Jim laughs, puzzled-like.
“ Why, the fifty quid I gave yer larst night,”
he says, patient, as if he knew Moss was only
tryin’ to pull his leg. “ Come on, old man—
time’s precious.” .
We was all crowdin’ round, an’ Moss went a
dirty yellow, but he stuck out.
“Git along with yer,” he says. “Who are ye
tryin’ to take down now. Jim Cassidy? I ”
At that moment long Tom Pullinger broke in :
“D’yer mean to say he never give yer that
fifty?” he arsks. “Why, yer fool, the whole
crowd of us saw him ! ”
“ I know yer did,” Moss says. “O’ course he
give it to me. But I give it back to him before
breakfast this mornin’! ”
“Well, that’s a good’un!” Jim says, and he
burst out laughin’. “Come on, Moss, don’t be a
fool. I’m not on me own this time—yer don’t fool
me two years!” He leaned forward. “ Where’s
yer witness, Moss ? ”
Moss was holdin’ on to the counter, the pieksher
of disgust.
“You low pig!” he says. “I give yer that
fifty first thing this mornin’. Don’t yer remember
cornin’ in, an’ gittin’ a drink, an’ arskin’ for it,
an’ ” ——
“Oh, git out!” Jim says, beginnin’ to lose his
temper. “I’m chock-a-block o’ you, Moss —
surely you got enough out o’ me larst year.
Hand over my money, or I’ll break your neck ! ”
The boys began to growl louder and edge up.
I remembered Moss’s revolver of the year before,
so I slipped behind the counter and shoved my
knee against the cash-drawer —an’ a lovely look T
got off Moss for me kind action. He was shakin’,
an’ couldn’t get his words out.
“ Pay up, you dog ! ” some of the chaps called
out.
“ He’s got it now ! ” says Moss.
“Well, search me,” says Jim, impatiently;
“p’rhaps that’ll convince him.”
The chaps didn’t like it, but just for form’s
sake they went through Jim—boots ’n’ all. Moss
come round an’ took a hand in the searchin’, an’
all they found was thirteen bob an’ his youngest
kid’s photo. Then Moss slipped outside an’ had
a squint over Jim’s mare, old Black Polly, as
everyone knew —unsaddled her, an’ even hunted
in her mane an’ tail. By that time the chaps was
fair mad, an’ long Tom Pullinger took Moss by
the scruff of the neck and hustled him into the
bar parlor.
“Now you makeup your mind, you thievin’,
low-lived Scotch Jew,” he says. “ Stop yer game
an’ pay up ole Jim’s money, or we ll smash yer
place into post an’ rails ! ”
“Let’s do it now!” sang out someone. The
chaps was willin’ enough, an’ some started flickin’
bottles off the bar shelves with their whips. Mrs.
Moss rushed in screeehin’, an’ flew at the chap
lioldin’ her Saunders—but I caught her an’ held
her tight, f’r fear she might hurt herself. She
was a mass of woman was Vi’let !
Moss caved in suddenly.
“Let me go,” he says, “I’ll pay him—curse
him ! ”
Tom Pullinger lets him go, and he unlocks the
safe. You should have heard the howl that went
up! There was Jim’s little bag of sov’rins, all
sweet an’ ’andy on the top shelf ! For a moment
I thought the boys ’ud do for Moss, they was that
furious—-they hustled round him, cursin’, an’ him
screamin’ out that he’d paid Jim in notes already.
Long Tom Pullinger knocked him down at that,
an’then Jim says : “Oh, let him alone—what do
I care, so long as I’ve got the money!” So we
leaves Saunders Moss a-lyin’ on the floor an’
Vi’let swearing at us like a bulloeky across the
prostrite form, an’ we makes tracks.
“Jim’s road an’mine branched off from the
other boys’ that afternoon ; so we says good-bye at
a bit of a shanty where Jim shouted all round
very free an’ handsome, an’ thanked all the chaps
for backin’ him up.
A few miles along, Jim says suddenly :
“ D’you mind if we change the saddles now.
Dave ? ”
“Not a bit,” I says.
So we got down an’ slipped off the saddles. Jim
put mine on my mare, an’ slung his own on Black
Polly. He lifted up the flap on the off side. There
was a little pocket fixed under it very cunnin’, an'
he put his hand in an’ took out a little parcel, an’
opened it out on the saddle, me watcliin’ him.
I gaped. There was ever so many notes !
Jim counted ’em carefully. “Fifty,” he says
—an’ grins at me.
“My bloomin’ colonial ! ” I says.
He grins more.
“There’s twenty,” he says, countin’ ’em out.
“That’s larst year,” an’ he shoved ’em in his
pocket. “ An’ there’s thirty, an’ half o’ that’s
fifteen—an’ that’s yours, Dave.” He stuck the
notes into my hand.
“ Well Pm damned ! ” I says, bewildered-like.
“Then you did...before breakfast...?”
“ Yairs,” he says.
BOHEE.
Although Maoriland is fairly bubbling
over with political organisations of all sorts and
hues, another has bobbed up serenely, the
Workers’ Political Association. The new body
wants a State ammunition factory started, State
saw-mills and timber supply depots, State smelt-
ing works, State ferry service between the two
islands, State trawling plants to supply muni-
cipal fish markets, and State flour mills. Also,
it wants the B.N.Z. to be converted into a State
Bank with sole right of note issue ; the extension
of the Shearers’ Accommodation Act to agricul-
tural workers ; immediate stoppage of the sale of
State lands; periodical re-adjustment of rent of all
lands hereafter leased from the State; and no
future leases to be for more than GO years. Re-
garding old-age pensions, the new organisation
desires the pensions to begin at the age of 60, in-
stead of 65.

Since the Cities are the Cities.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FOOLS can parrot-cry the prophet when the
proof is close at hand
And the blind cm see the danger when
the foe is in the land !
Truth was never cynicism, death or ruin’s not a
joke,
“ Told-you-so ” is not a warning—Patriotism
not a croak.
Blame will aid no man nor country when the dark
days come at last—
As with men so with a nation, and the Warning
time is past.
Our great sins were of omission, and the dogs of
war are loosed —
And we all must stand together when those sins
come home to roost.
Since the cities are the cities and shall stand for
evermore,
Let us justify our being, be it peace or be it war.
For because we are the townsfolk, and have never
ridden far,
Shall we call the bush to aid us that has made
us what we are ?
Westward went our brothers, fighting distance,
drought, and loneliness
While we lived in light and comfort knowing
nothing of distress.
We who never shared the hardships when the
sunset led them on,
Now’s our time, O street-bred people, with our
faces to the dawn !
They have conquered with the cross-cut and the
wedges and the maul,
With the spade and axe and mattock and the
saddle-packs and all,
They have mighty work before them for the sake
of you and me—
Let us stand up to our duty ! We’re the Rear-
guard by the Sea.
Days of gibes at “street-bred people” by the
street-bred bards are done—
Shall the man who lays the yard-stick never learn
to lay the gun ?
Shall the crouched type-writer toiling for his
home in days like these
Touch the button the less firmly when we play on
other keys ?
We have seen in many countries what the street-
bred men can do—
In the desert, scrub and jungle they were men
who battled through !
Human weeds of grand endurance winning where
the strong men quailed,
Pigeon-chested leaders leading on where beef-born
courage failed.
Street-bred people down the ages—beggars, mobs
and democrats—
Fought through many desperate sieges (fought on
horseflesh, dogs and rats)
When their own cowed country failed them, then
the city soul was proved—
“ Street-bred people ” died in thousands for the
cities that they loved.
In the days when strength was needed—days of
pike and axe and sword—
Daylight found the peaceful burghers ready, keep-
ing watch and ward.
Clerks and tailors fought like heroes at the gates
and in the trench— •
(Even Falstaff brought his herrings with some
slaughter through the French).
Every man should have a cottage and a garden to
defend,
But the “ should : be” is for ever—cities stand
until the end.
Every farmer has a country that he loves when
war-drums roll—
Every clerk may have a city that he loves with heart
and sold.
Fat or lean, we all are sinners—lean or fat we al;
would be;
High or low or lean or fatted, ’tis fo» Nationality.
It will be till all is ended, as it was since al:
began—
’Tis the head and not the feathers ! ’tis the hear!
and not the “ man ’’ !
N.s.w.
HENRY LAWSON.
The Turning of the Worm.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MRS. JAMES’ creamy cheeks were
flushed pink, and her pretty mouth wore
a stubborn expression. Mr. Winter’s
shifty eyes looked first at her and then round at
the green trees, and between many “look heres”
and “my dearchilds” he strove to tell her that
the thing was impossible. Mrs. James fixed her
baby blue eyes on his, and her mouth grew less
sulky and more pouty. “ I don’t care,” she said ;
“ if she doesn’t apologise I’ll never speak to you
again. ”
“ She will never do it. We are m her hands—
the thing is impossible,” Mr. Winter once more
assured her.
* * * *
Mrs. Winter was an unimportant woman, who
had a baby every year and dressed dowdily.
There was a time when she was called graceful
and rather pretty, but that was before Mr. and
Mrs. James came to live in the house by the river,
and Mr. Winter took to spending his evenings
out. People called on her because she was
decently connected, but they found her dull. She
whined so about the children and the servants;
also she loved her husband, which was absurd of
her under the circumstances.
In vain her friends strove to open her eyes.
Her obstinacy annoyed them. Even her nurse-
girl had remarked casually that she had seen Mr.
Winter and Mrs. James in the park together-
remarked it so often that it seemed less casual
than spiteful.
What Mrs. Winter wanted most at, this time
was to visit her people. She was tired, poor soul,
of bringing cranky babies into the world—tired ot
incompetent servants, and most tired of the things
her friends told her about the man she had
married. „
She thought his business must be failing. He
did not see his way clear to let her go —in fact, he
simply couldn’t afford it. She reckoned up the
cash, and found the trip could be made for a few
pounds. She would “do up” her black hat, and
with the new blouse she was making, her clothes
would answer. But Mr. Winter remained obdu-
rate, and as he rose to go out he assured her the
thing was impossible. Just what he told Mrs.
James in the park, you remember, but that was
afterwards.
* * » *
On an afternoon, Mr. Winter’s married sister,
who really liked Mrs. Winter, and altogether dis-
approved of her own brother, called in amiable
mood. After hearing Mrs. Winter’s tale of woe,
and listening impatiently to her long, quivering
sighs and her plaintive talk about the black hat
and the new blouse, she found it impossible to
contain herself.
“If you’ve the spirit of an insect, my dear
Mary, you will be away before the end of the
week, and if you ever mention that black hat
again, which ought to be in the dustbin, or that
miserable mercerised blouse, you deserve nothing
but contempt. Go to the park to-morrow at 10,
or the next morning, or the morning after that —
oh, hang the children—and see for yourself. And
don’t leave this town with a penny under £5O.
And, Mary, when you get away, liven up. Your
happiness is gone for the present; win what you
can of it back. Go in for a course of skin treat-
ment. Get some decent gowns. Be naughty,
even—smoke cigarettes, or anything that’s up-to-
date, and don't have babies every 12 months.
And so it came about that a rather bewildered
Mrs. Winter walked aimlessly next morning into
the park, and in a secluded corner stumbled on
Mr. Winter, fastening something bright and
sparkling on Mrs. James’ dainty wrist. The
smiling baby-face was raised to his, and, as Mary
watched, Mr. Winter bent and kissed the sofo
lips with a lover’s kiss.
Mrs. Winter felt a quick stopping of the breath,
an instant’s nausea, and then insects—millions of
them—tearing all over her. Mr. Winter rose
quickly to his feet and stuttered something about
having picked up a trinket of Mrs. James’. Mrs.
James sat still, but looked interested.
It was a transformed Mary who spoke—a
woman with head thrown back and steely eyes, a
woman on her mettle, who looked down tho
insolent blue eyes of her rival—a masterful, con-
temptuous Mary, who ordered the dainty bundle
of muslin home to her husband and children, and
shut Mr. Winter’s lips with cutting words.
“No more lies, I beg of you. Come with me, I
have something to say.” And he went —her hero
—like a whipped cur.
Mrs. Winter accompanied Mr. Winter to his
office and got a cheque for £lOO. Next day she
left to visit her friends, leaving her babies behind
her. And it was some days after that when Mrs.
James sulkily demanded an apology for the words
used to her that day in the park, and because
that apology was not forthcoming Mrs. James
kept her word, looked out for fresh conquests,
and let Mary’s husband severely alone.
Some three months later Mr. Winter waited on
the wharf, rather nervously, for the arrival of the
Sydney boat and his wife ; and welcomed sheep
ishly a tall, smart-looking woman, who greeted
him patronisingly, but graciously—a woman with
a pretty color in her cheeks and glossy waved
hair, who talked brightly to a good-looking and
obviously admiring fellow-passenger.
And it was thus Mrs. Winter came into her own
again, and won back her husband’s allegiance.
But the episode has robbed her name of its fitting-
ness, and on Mary’s rejuvenated features rests a
lightly contemptuous expression, where once there
was only sweetness.
GIP.
Reginald.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Reginald aftermath bowed his
matted head over a table in the Ver-
micelli Caffi and wept. Reginald was
a bard of the Old Bohemian breed. He nourished
his tall, drooping body on spaghetti, salmi, and
risotto, and his free but fractured soul on cheap,
red, fortified wine—also on beer, rum, and hot gin
when the spirit moved.
Reginald was born out of his time, and he
wasn’t understood. Cast into a cold, harsh epoch,
with no appreciation for the Beautiful as repre-
sented by Reginald, he gave the Philistine world
the cynical shrug and bitter smile, and took refuge
in alcohol. Reginald’s dreamy lyricsoverhis “Lost
Loves of Yesteryear” (he always lost them), the
“Graveyard by the Saltwater River ” (where he
longed to dream again), and other sweet, mournful
themes of things dead and buried and forgotten,
had melted thestony heart of the editor of the Land
Agents’ Advertiser. The editor thought that a
poet of Reginald's fine feeling for the buried past
would add “tone” to the paper, and put his
readers into chastened mood for the advertise-
ments. He hired the Poet of the Tomb as
descriptive writer at so much a week.
Reginald smiled with lofty cynicism and drew
his first week’s screw in advance. Reginald began
on the Springvale Necropolis, and moved his
readers to tears. Elated by his success, he took a
week’s holiday and mused. When he had re-
covered by the want-of-gold cure, he essayed his
second prose fantasy. It was beautiful, but it
didn’t sell the goods. The harsh editor then
demanded something more practical from Regi-
nald—a mundane, advertising subject, but to be
treated in fragrant, poetical style. He was
turned loose on a new boiling-down works.
The poet shuddered, but he went through
bravely. He did a tannery, a soap-works, a
wool-store, an abattoirs, and a bone-mill. He
quivered, but stuck. Then the bard’s fine soul
rebelled. The editor required a soul-stirring
description of a big baths emporium, and re-
quested Reginald to test all the processes—
slipper, spray, hot, shower, swimming, Turkish,
Russian, Japanese baths —all kinds of baths,
more baths than Reginald had seen during all
his previous career. The noble soul of the poet
rose in protest, and he resigned. Next week he
saw in the paper a two-column article on a new
brewery, with realistic details. That would have
been his next job. So the Poet Aftermath bowed
his shaggy locks over a large pewter and wept.
